First published on 27th June 2023AbstractSolid-state electrolytes (SEs) have attracted overwhelming attention as a promising alternative to traditional organic liquid electrolytes (OLEs) for high-energy-density sodium-metal batteries (SMBs), owing to their intrinsic incombustibility, wider electrochemical stability window (ESW), and better thermal stability. Among various kinds of SEs, inorganic solid-state electrolytes (ISEs) stand out because of their high ionic conductivity, excellent oxidative stability, and good mechanical strength, rendering potential utilization in safe and dendrite-free SMBs at room temperature. However, the development of Na-ion ISEs still remains challenging, that a perfect solution has yet to be achieved. Herein, we provide a comprehensive and in-depth inspection of the state-of-the-art ISEs, aiming at revealing the underlying Na+ conduction mechanisms at different length scales, and interpreting their compatibility with the Na metal anode from multiple aspects. A thorough material screening will include nearly all ISEs developed to date, i.e., oxides, chalcogenides, halides, antiperovskites, and borohydrides, followed by an overview of the modification strategies for enhancing their ionic conductivity and interfacial compatibility with Na metal, including synthesis, doping and interfacial engineering. By discussing the remaining challenges in ISE research, we propose rational and strategic perspectives that can serve as guidelines for future development of desirable ISEs and practical implementation of high-performance SMBs.Jiawen HuangJiawen Huang is currently a PhD candidate in Shanghai University (SHU). He received his BS degree and MS degree from the University of Washington (UW), majorly investigating halide-based solid electrolytes in lithium-metal batteries. His current research interests focus on sodium-ion inorganic solid electrolytes and composite solid electrolytes for solid-state sodium-metal batteries, as well as other metal-based batteries.Minghong WuMinghong Wu is a Distinguished Professor at Shanghai University and the Academician of Chinese Academy of Engineering. She received her PhD in Shanghai Institute of Applied Physics of Chinese Academy of Sciences in 1999. 1. Introduction
The rapid growth of economy and thriving industrial activities have led to increase in global energy demand, as well as concerns over deteriorating environmental damage. The conventional energy structure, mostly relying on consumption of fossil fuels, has become inadequate to support the sustainable future of humankind. Hence, a diversified energy portfolio is urgently required to ease the threat of environment deterioration and energy crisis, by developing renewable energy resources, such as wind power,1 solar power,2 hydro power,3 and nuclear power,4 as supplements or even replacements for the traditional thermal power.5 Nevertheless, it is difficult to integrate these renewable energy supplies into the power grid because they are inherently intermittent and non-programmable due to the limitation of season and region. In this regard, designing a reliable and cost-effective energy storage system becomes the key consideration for balancing the supplied power with the electricity demand in an eco-friendly manner. Among various energy storage technologies, electrochemical energy storage systems, represented by secondary batteries, are still the most appealing candidates owing to the balance of their specific energy capacity, responsiveness, mobility, efficiency, operating cost, and scalability. In the few past decades, rechargeable lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have dominated the market for consumer electronics and electric vehicles (EVs). In the grand scheme of accelerating the electrification process, global LIB production capacity for EVs has leapt from 20 GW h in 2010 to 747 GW h in 2020, and the growth is anticipated to continue to reach 2492 GW h by 2025.6,7 The furiously competitive market prompts the sophistication of LIBs in terms of material selection, energy density, power density, fast-charging capability, lifetime, and cost.8,9 Several years ago, the commercially available 18650-type cells could already achieve a gravimetric and volumetric energy density of 260 W h kg−1 and 770 W h L−1, respectively, at the cell level.10 However, the booming market for LIBs meanwhile raises anxiety upon lithium reserves and pricing, along with worries for resource nationalism and monopolistic behavior, especially considering that the bulk of lithium mines is located in only a few countries,11 as shown in Fig. 1a. Therefore, it is necessary to explore novel battery systems that are comparable to or even better than the existing LIBs.
 Fig. 1  (a) Distribution of identified lithium resources, (b) element abundance in the Earth's crust and (c) comparison between critical properties of Li and Na. 
Among different metal-ion batteries, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), which are also known as Na-ion batteries (NIBs), are considered as one of the most prominent alternatives to LIBs with a fair trade-off between energy density and cost.12–14 The sodium resource is evenly distributed in the crust of the Earth, with greater abundance than that of lithium (2.36% vs. 0.002%) (Fig. 1b), ensuring ample supply and low cost of raw materials.15–17 Noteworthily, this value can still be underrated as it does not take into account the element abundance in seawater. In fact, the price of sodium carbonate is below $400 per ton, which is tens to over a hundred times lower than that of lithium carbonate regardless of the volatile price in the commodity market.18 The cost advantage of SIBs also reflects in material selection for the current collector, where cheap aluminum current collectors can be used in SIBs at both anode and cathode terminals because sodium does not undergo alloying with aluminum at low potentials, in contrast to lithium, where the more expensive copper current collectors must be used in LIBs.19,20 Meanwhile, the standard electrode potential of sodium (−2.71 V vs. standard hydrogen electrode (SHE)) is similar to that of lithium (−3.04 V vs. SHE), which guarantees the considerable cell voltage and energy density of SIBs comparable to those of LIBs (Fig. 1c).21 SIBs share almost the same battery configuration and working principle with LIBs, where alkali-metal ions shuttle between electrodes by a “rocking-chair” mechanism, while the Na+ storage mechanism depends on different electrode materials, such as intercalation, alloying, and conversion electrode materials. Taking advantage of the existing understanding and research routes for the LIB system owing to the similar chemical and electrochemical characteristics between Na and Li,22,23 the scientific research of SIBs has made substantial progress by developing a variety of material combinations, among which some of them have been successfully commercialized in stationary energy storage applications,15,24–26 e.g., high-temperature Na–S batteries27 and high-temperature Na–NiCl2 batteries.28
Unfortunately, room-temperature SIBs still suffer from undesirable specific capacity, low energy density, unpleasant rate capability and poor cycling performance due to the intrinsic properties of Na. Compared to Li, Na has larger atomic radius (1.06 Å vs. 0.76 Å), heavier atomic weight (23 g mol−1 vs. 6.9 g mol−1) and higher standard potential (Fig. 1c), which lead to the lower volumetric and gravimetric energy densities of SIBs than LIBs when the same battery chemistry is used.29 Meanwhile, the larger size of sodium results in worse cycling performance as electrodes experience larger volumetric changes that accumulate strain upon Na insertion and extraction, as well as worse rate capability due to the sluggish kinetics of the sodiation–desodiation process.30,31 Instead of graphite typically used as the anode in LIBs, hard carbon has been chosen for the anode of SIBs because of its larger layer spacing for reversible intercalation of Na+.32,33 Unfortunately, although the hard carbon anode displays a reversible sodium capacity of 300 mA h g−1, the wide voltage window to achieve this capacity renders a low overall working voltage and low energy density of SIBs.33–36 As the conversion-type and alloying-type anode materials exhibit specific capacities several times beyond that of hard carbon, they are readily prone to pulverization during the great volume change in the sodiation–desodiation process.37 Given these drawbacks of Na, the energy density of state-of-the-art room-temperature SIBs usually lies in the range of merely 75–165 W h kg−1 or 250–375 W h L−1.38
1.1. Emerging demand for SEs
In an attempt to enhance the energy density of SIBs, researchers are intrigued by sodium-metal batteries (SMBs) with metallic Na as the anode, by virtue of its superior theoretical specific capacity (1166 mA h g−1), low reduction potential (−2.71 V vs. SHE), abundance in nature, and low cost.39 As illustrated in Fig. 2, the superiority of the Na metal anode is demonstrated by comparing its specific capacity and plateau voltage with other commonly used anode materials in SIBs. The theoretical energy density of room-temperature SMBs, such as RT Na–S,40 RT Na–O2,41 and RT Na–CO242 batteries, can easily surpass 1000 W h kg−1.
 Fig. 2  Summary of common anode materials for sodium-ion batteries and the sodium metal anode by plotting their specific capacity and plateau voltage. Note that materials that are located closer to the upper left corner of the chart (high specific capacity and low plateau voltage) correspond to higher cell-level energy density, if the same cathode material is chosen, thereby becoming the most preferable. Data from ref. 35 and 43–183. 
However, the pursuit of high-energy-density SMBs has always been a rough journey, plagued by the low Coulombic efficiency (CE), poor cycle life, and questioned cell safety. Given the ultrahigh chemical and electrochemical reactivity of metallic Na, a rough and low-density solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer tends to in situ form because of the spontaneous and irreversible reaction between organic liquid electrolytes and Na metal. This unstable SEI layer is subjected to destruction under the repeated volume expansion of the host-less metallic anode, unceasingly depleting the electrolytes and active Na.184–186 Worse still, the inhomogeneous SEI layer, as well as the surface cracks after destruction, induces non-uniform surface electric field distribution and uneven Na deposition on the metal anode. In the repeated process of Na stripping and plating, the non-uniform electrodeposition of Na can gradually build up “needle-like” or “mossy” dendrites on the uneven patches, even at current densities well below the critical current density (CCD).187,188 On the one hand, when Na is stripped from the metal anode during battery discharging, these sodium dendrites easily “melt” and break off, then get trapped in the SEI wrap, becoming electrically insulated from the electrode mass, known as “dead Na”. These “dead Na” dispersed in the vicinity of the electrolyte/anode interface can largely increase the interfacial impedance and deplete the amount of reversible Na metal, emerging as the culprit of cell performance deterioration.189–192 On the other hand, as some of the sharp sodium dendrites grow constantly and uncontrollably in liquid electrolytes under the “tip effect”, that is, the strong electric field concentrates around their tips promoting further growth of dendrites, they will eventually penetrate the polymer separator and short-circuit the battery, causing thermal runaway.193,194 This is extremely dangerous in SMBs where the metallic Na anode and OLEs coexist, because the failed battery cells carry the risk of fire or even explosion due to the highly volatile and flammable OLEs. For example, the commonly used carbonate ester-based co-solvents diethyl carbonate (DEC) and dimethyl carbonate (DMC) have flash points of merely 31 °C and 18 °C, respectively, while the ether-based solvents that have been considered preferable for Na metal are not any better, e.g., diglyme (57 °C), 1,3-dioxolane (DOL) (1.7 °C), and dimethyl ether (DME) (0 °C).31,195–197 For reference, the commercially adopted carbonate-based OLE 1 M LiPF6/ethylene carbonate (EC):ethyl methyl carbonate (EMC), usually paired with the LiCoO2 cathode and graphite anode, also has a low flash point of 28 °C.198 Meanwhile, the use of OLEs can accelerate the cathode dissolution during the repeated charge–discharge cycling and shorten the cathode life.199 Moreover, the gas evolution of OLEs tends to become more obvious in Na-metal batteries, where the released CO2 and H2 may increase the risk of leakage and cause other safety hazards.200 A summative schematic of the major challenges in OLE-containing Na-metal batteries is given in Fig. 3. Thereby, the utilization of the unruly metallic Na anode highlights the safety concerns over the conventional organic liquid electrolytes. Several safer alternatives to organic electrolytes have been proposed, e.g., aqueous electrolytes201–204 and ionic liquids,205–208 but the former still exhibit an intrinsically narrow electrochemical stability window and the latter suffer from high viscosity and high cost.
 Fig. 3  Challenges in high-voltage sodium-metal batteries arising from organic liquid electrolytes. 
Solid-state batteries (SSBs) can be considered as a viable solution to the safety hazards as well as an effective approach to higher energy density. The schematic of a solid-state sodium-metal battery (SMB) is provided in comparison to that of a conventional SIB in Fig. 4. As the essential component of SSBs, the SE serves as an ion-conducting medium and a separator simultaneously by conducting Na+ and blocking electron exchange. The benefits of replacing OLEs with SEs are manifold in terms of safety, energy density, cost, and battery production. Most obviously, the incombustible SEs can guarantee the improved safety of SSBs and the ability to work at a wider range of temperatures. The use of electrical insulating SEs eliminates the need for separators which are regarded as the inactive mass of batteries, thus reducing the battery size and enhancing the energy density. The high thermal stability of SSBs, along with their nonfluidity, also allows specific designs for battery cell geometry, e.g. directly stacking cell units in a single battery package using bipolar electrodes.209,210 Such a bipolar stacking that connects single cells in series, as compared to the parallel connection in conventional liquid SIBs, can be used to effectively reduce the inactive volume and increase the voltage of a battery cell, as well as to achieve a higher module energy density. Some of the SEs are able to withstand higher voltages than liquid electrolytes, which enables higher energy density at the cell level by embracing high-capacity and high-voltage cathode materials, with the elimination of cathode dissolution.199,211 Conceptually, the formation of metal dendrites can also be inhibited by the ISEs due to their high mechanical strength, especially considering their much higher shear modulus compared to that of Na metal,212,213 though this principle may only work as a theoretical guide for practice.214–216 Moreover, for the possible industrial mass production up scaled from laboratory research, the solidification of electrolytes favors simpler assembling and integration processes at an industrial scale.217 However, despite the fascinating properties of SEs, their practical application is thwarted by some major challenges, mainly from the inherently poor room-temperature ionic conductivity and a series of electrode/electrolyte interfacial issues. Although some of the ISEs have already exhibited bulk ionic conductivity higher than that of commercial OLEs, most of the SEs still suffer from insufficient total ionic conductivity at room temperature, especially considering that the ideal ohmic loss in SSBs with the Na metal anode should be lower than 100 mV even at high current densities (3–10 mA cm−2), corresponding to the ionic conductivity above 0.4 mS cm−1 for a 25 μm-thick SE.218–220 Meanwhile, the inferior interfacial wettability between rigid solids, i.e., electrodes and SEs, especially ISEs, results in point contacts and large interfacial resistance, while the stiff SEs are incapable of buffering the infinite volume change during cycling.221–223 As the chemical and electrochemical stability of SEs against Na metal do not always exceed that of OLEs due to the complex interplay between thermodynamics and kinetic factors, the unstable solid–solid interface is still subject to a series of unpleasant interfacial issues.209,224 Recently, the rigid ISEs have been proven ineligible for eliminating the dendrite penetration, as the inferior interfacial contact and high electrical conductivity will promote the growth of dendrites along grain boundaries in polycrystalline SEs and lead to fast short-circuit. Therefore, many efforts have been devoted to enhance the total ionic conductivity of SEs and their interfacial stability/compatibility against metallic Na, among which ISEs manifest rather impressive advancements compared to others. In this regard, this review article will majorly focus on ISEs, while other related types of SEs may be briefly introduced.
 Fig. 4  Schematic of the battery configuration of conventional sodium-ion batteries and solid-state sodium-metal batteries. 
1.2. Criteria for ideal SEs
As the key part of SSBs, SEs have decisive impacts on the performance of batteries from the aspects of operating voltage, cycling stability, rate capability, thermal stability, safety, etc. To ensure consistency in material design and evaluation, a lucid definition of ideal SEs must be established (Fig. 5), with some of the critical requirements listed as follows:212,225–229
 Fig. 5  Criteria for ideal solid electrolytes. 
• High ionic conductivity (ideally >1 mS cm−1) at ambient temperature for efficient ion transport.
• Extremely low electronic conductivity (<10−12 S cm−1) to prevent mixed-ion-electron conduction and dendrite formation.
• Wide electrochemical stability window (>5 V vs. Na+/Na) to raise the cell voltage and eliminate unwanted interfacial reactions with high-voltage cathodes and Na metal.
• High chemical stability and interfacial compatibility against electrodes, especially highly reactive metallic Na.
• Non-flammable, cost-effective, and non-toxic nature.
• High thermal stability for battery operation in a broad range of temperature.
• Sufficient mechanical strength to prevent cracking, fracture, deformation, or other damage.
• Facile synthesis and processing procedures for large-scale application.
In this review article, two of the most important aspects of the criteria for ideal SEs in SMBs will be mainly interpreted, viz. the total ionic conductivity and the interfacial stability against Na metal.
1.3. Classification of SEs
There are three main categories of Na-ion SEs, namely, ISEs (inorganic solid electrolytes), solid polymer electrolytes (SPEs), and composite solid electrolytes (CSEs). The discovery of SPEs, made by dispersing sodium salts in polymer host materials, can be traced back to as early as 1970s when ion conduction in polyethylene oxide (PEO) containing alkali metal salts was found.230–233 Since then, many recipes have been developed for SPEs, among which PEO, poly(vinylidene fluoride-hexafluoropropylene) (PVDF-HFP), polyphosphazenes (PAN), poly(methylmethacrylate) (PMMA) and poly(vinyl chloride) (PVC) are the most commonly used polymer matrices, compatible with a variety of sodium salts.234 SPEs feature soft mechanical properties that offer intimate contact with electrodes and feasible processibility, benefiting the design of flexible SSBs or thin-film batteries. However, they usually exhibit low room-temperature ionic conductivity (≤10−6 S cm−1) and large ionic transfer resistance due to the insufficient amorphous regions with a high degree of crystallinity, as well as low oxidation voltage.235 Therefore, a common strategy to maximize the amorphous region, thus enhancing the ion conduction, is embedding inorganic fillers, such as SiO2, Al2O3, and ZrO2, into SPEs.236 The so-formed inorganic/organic composites are called CSEs, which tend to possess compensated advantages of both inorganic and organic materials, with higher ionic conductivity than that of SPEs but still lower than that of ISEs. On the other hand, compared to SPEs, ISEs usually exhibit much higher room-temperature ionic conductivity (≈1 mS cm−1), higher oxidative limit, better thermal stability, and single ion conduction. Some of the ISEs even have ionic conductivity higher than that of commercial OLEs. ISEs can be subcategorized into oxide-based, chalcogenide-based, halide-based, hydride-based, and antiperovskite-structured compounds (Fig. 6), while they can be further divided into crystalline materials and glass-based materials. Crystalline or ceramic electrolytes are represented by β-alumina and NASICON-type oxides, which manifest outstanding chemical stability but require harsh processing conditions to achieve the desired ionic conductivity. Meanwhile, their rigid and rough surfaces often lead to poor wettability of Na metal and insufficient contact with cathode materials.237,238 Glass-based electrolytes, including glass-ceramic and glassy electrolytes, represented by Na3PS4-type sulfides and some halides, have softer mechanical properties and less-developed grain boundaries due to the presence of amorphous phases, which allow better wetting of electrodes and facile preparation such as simple ball-milling and cold-pressing, while they are usually subject to poor thermodynamic stability as well as spontaneous decomposition forming an unstable SEI with the Na metal anode.239–242
 Fig. 6  Radar plot to compare the key properties of some major types of inorganic Na-ion solid electrolytes. 
Given the rapid iteration in the development of SEs, this review article aims to provide a comprehensive yet detailed elaboration on the recent progress of the state-of-the-art Na-ion ISEs, by unveiling the most updated understanding of their ion conduction mechanisms from the atomic level to the microstructure and macrostructure level, as well as their interfacial stability and compatibility against the metallic Na anode, followed by specific modification strategies for each material and exciting achievements made. From fundamental understanding and mechanistic study to material design and battery optimization, we hope to offer thorough understanding of sodium-ion ISEs and establish the general principles for future research in the related fields.
2. General principles of complex Na+ conduction
In crystalline ISEs, mobile Na+ ions usually reside at lattice sites with the lowest site energy, or known as ground-state sites, in the interstices of the skeletal framework. Driven by the spatial gradient in the electrochemical potential, they overcome the energy barriers and achieve ion diffusion by hopping between ground-state sites and/or transient-state sites, in the form of either single-ion or concerted-ion motion.243 The nature of Na+ conduction within ion conductors involves the continuous long-range movement of mobile Na+ along the inter-connected migration channels constructed by the framework anions, through the grain bulk and across the boundaries/interfaces. Therefore, Na+ conduction within ISEs is a complex function of different mechanisms and influencing factors at multiple scales, e.g., atomic interactions, lattice defects, disorder, crystal structure, lattice dynamics, etc.209,210,244–247 Overall, the ionic conductivity of ISEs (σ) in the simplest form is subject to two factors, namely the concentration of charge carriers (n) and the mobility of charge carriers (μ), as described by eqn (1): 
σ = qnμ
(1)
2.1. Atomic scale
Single-ion hopping. Everything starts with an individual mobile Na+ hopping from one site to another available one. At the atomic scale (or the so-called zero-dimensional scale), this short-range or local diffusion of Na+ is assisted by point defects and can be described by one of the three classical diffusion models: (1) vacancy mechanism, (2) interstitial mechanism, and (3) interstitialcy mechanism, though the last one is inherently correlated. As shown in Fig. 7, in the vacancy diffusion mechanism, an individual Na+ jumps to a neighboring intrinsic vacant site, meanwhile creating a new vacancy at its original position. In the interstitial mechanism, a Na+ located in the interstices of the matrix atoms travels to another adjacent interstices that is not fully occupied. Since Na+ migrates within the crystalline materials via point defects, e.g., vacancy and interstitial, the diffusion of charge carriers is identical to the diffusion of point defects. In other words, the concentration of charge carriers n is equivalent to the concentration of point defects and such a value is positively associated to the ionic conductivity of a solid. Noteworthily, the concentration of point defects is thermally controlled and is related to the defect formation energy in the intrinsic regime, such as the formation energy of Schottky and Frenkel disorders, or subject to the charge compensation mechanism induced by aliovalent ion doping in the extrinsic regime.
 Fig. 7  Schematic illustration of ion diffusion at the atomic scale, including single-ion hopping and correlated-ion motion mechanisms, as well as the existence of grains and grain boundaries at the microstructural scale. 
To further explain this basic understanding of ion conduction, the simplified Arrhenius expression (eqn (2)) is given, 
(2)to describe the temperature dependence of ionic conductivity, where T, q, kB, σ0, and Ea represent the absolute temperature, elementary charge, Boltzmann constant, conductivity prefactor, and activation energy for ion conduction, respectively. Herein, as the conductivity prefactor σ0 takes into account all possible factors influencing the ionic conductivity,248 except activation energy, the activation energy Ea is negatively related to the ionic conductivity in an exponential manner and it is contributed by both defect formation enthalpy, ΔHf, and migration enthalpy, ΔHm, described by eqn (3), 
(3)or expressed in a simpler and more conventional form (eqn (4)), 
Ea ∝ Em + Ef
(4)where Ef is the energy required for defect formation and Em is the energy required to overcome the energy barrier in the migration process, viz. transient state.249–252 As mentioned above, Ef is a vital component of Ea that regulates the concentration of mobile defects in the intrinsic regime, thus affecting the ionic conductivity. On the other hand, regarding Na+ migration as continuous jumps between ground-state sites and transient-state sites, the highest site energy difference between these states, or the so-called energy barrier, is equally crucial in terms of mobility, as shown in Fig. 8a. In some cases, the activation migration barrier, Em, can be significantly lowered in certain ion sublattices and coordination environment, e.g., bcc-anion framework or triggered concerted ion motion.
 Fig. 8  The migration energy landscape of (a) single-ion hopping, i.e., vacancy and interstitial mechanisms, and (b) correlated-ion motion along Na+ migration paths. Adapted with permission from ref. 249 and 253. 

Concerted ion motion. Concerted ion motion is a very important mechanism in fast ion conductors. While the vacancy and interstitial mechanisms merely concern the jumping model of a single Na+ on an isolated sublattice, the ion hopping in ISEs is not necessarily limited to individual Na+. The Na+ motion via the interstitialcy mechanism, or called “knock-off” migration, is inherently correlated by involving two ions, where an interstitial Na+ replaces its neighboring lattice site occupied by another Na+ by pushing it to an interstices (Fig. 7). Interestingly, the concerted ion migration often results in much lower activation energy barriers or a flat energy landscape, compared to that of single-ion hopping, that originates from the local environment or bonding in coordination of the mobile charge carriers, as illustrated by Fig. 8b.249,253 For example, concerted ion migration with reduced migration energy is enabled by intentionally adding extra mobile ions until they outnumber the available ground-state sites in materials such as NASICONs.253,254 In this way, the metastable (high-energy) sites, e.g., octahedral sites, are occupied by the excess mobile ions as the low-energy sites, e.g., tetrahedral sites, are already filled to capacity. When the mobile ions at high-energy sites migrate downhill, they offset the energy required for the uphill-migrating ions at low-energy sites, triggering the low-energy-barrier concerted ion diffusion. The Nernst–Einstein relation (eqn (5)) relates the mobility to the self-diffusion coefficient D of charge carriers, 
μ = qD/kBT
(5)where D is defined by eqn (6), 
D = zNc(1 − c)a2v/kBT
(6)where z is the number of nearest neighbor sites of the charge carriers, viz. the coordination number, N is the density of possible sites for the charge carriers, c is the site fraction of the charge carrier on its sublattice, a is the distance of intersite hopping, and v is the hopping frequency. From this relation, the high diffusivity and high mobility of Na+ are closely related to c, the high density of charge carriers on its sublattice, and (1 − c), the high density of available sites accessible for mobile charge carriers.
2.2. Crystal structure scale
At a larger scale, the crystal structure of crystalline ISEs plays a significant role in Na+ conduction mechanisms related to the mobility of charge carriers, reflected by the parameters in Arrhenius and Nernst–Einstein expressions, such as hopping distance a, hopping frequency v, activation energy Ea, and local coordination environment including z, N, c, etc. Meanwhile, the concentration of mobile Na+ can also be influenced by the crystal structure as it determines the maximum accommodation. Considering Na+ conduction as an extremely complex process, all these structure-determined factors synergistically contribute to ion migration. As stated, Na+ conduction is a long-range movement along the available channel, so the ease of such migration is largely dependent on their local environment in the pathways. In the skeletal structure of crystalline ISEs, the constructing stationary anions possess considerable interactions, i.e., Coulombic force, with mobile Na+ during their shuttling, reflected in the Em part of Ea. Because it is strongly affected by their atom location, site occupation and local bonding, an ideal fast ion conducting structure should have sufficient wide conduction pathways for Na+ traverse, reduced interaction between Na+ and skeleton anions, and equal-energy hopping sites.
Lattice volume and bottleneck regions. The lattice volume reflects the size of free space for mobile Na+, that is, its hopping frequency and distance. In a similar manner, bottleneck regions (a triangle delineated by three framework anions) in the Na+ migration paths, with their size usually positively related to the lattice volume, represent the maximum energy barriers that mobile ions must overcome (Fig. 9a). Intuitively, larger lattice volume and bottlenecks should always result in a lower migration barrier of Na+ and reduced interactions with framework anions, thus achieving faster ion conduction. However, the truth is that ion migration can only be effectively facilitated by carefully tuning the lattice volume of the crystal structure and the size of bottlenecks with appropriate dopant ions, due to their complex impacts on the site energy of Na+,255,257–261 which will be specified in the sections for particular materials.262
 Fig. 9  Schematic illustration of influencing factors at the crystal structure scale, including (a) appropriate lattice volume and bottleneck size for successful ion hopping, (b) nearly equal-energy ion migration pathways with a minimal activation barrier in bcc-like anion arrangement compared to those in other typical anion configurations, reprinted with permission from ref. 255, (c) paddle-wheel mechanism of (PS4)3− polyanions promoting Na+ migration in Na3PS4 and (d) rotational motion of (PS4)3− polyanions enlarging the bottleneck regions in Na11Sn2PS12, reprinted with permission from ref. 256. 

Equal-energy conduction pathway. Na+ tends to occupy the preferred coordination, viz. low site energy, which means that it is prone to experience a high migration energy barrier if the local environment varies significantly in the conduction pathways.258 From this perspective, some types of crystal symmetries have been found to allow similar or equipotential Na+ sublattice configurations that require fairly low energy for rearrangement of these mobile Na+. For example, ion conduction in the body-centered cubic (bcc)-like anion framework was observed to be significantly faster compared to that in other close-packed frameworks (Fig. 9b), because of its interlinked energy-equivalent tetrahedral sites that allow Na+ to hop in a flattened energy landscape and with a lower activation barrier.255 Akin to the bcc-like lattice structure, the novel superionic conductor Na11Sn2PS12 also exhibits the quasi-isotropic fast diffusion of Na+ between equal-energy Na+ sites due to the effectively equivalent Na+–Na+ distance and the same coordination environment.263,264 Speaking of which, the structural frustration and disorder of the Na+ sublattice are promising mechanisms to reduce the activation energy barriers and facilitate ion conduction. In the frustrated structure, e.g., distorted polyhedral sites for mobile ion occupation, the ground state of Na+ can be destabilized creating a shallow potential well for Na+.265 For example, this frustration-induced superionic conduction has been demonstrated in composition-deviated Na8.5(AlS4)0.25(SiS4)0.75 as a result of low local coordination symmetry of Na+ and minimized Coulombic attraction.266 Meanwhile, frustration usually prompts disorder in the anion sublattice, as well as concerted ion motion, both facilitating ion conduction.
Lattice dynamics. In addition to the static structural factors of the skeletal framework, lattice dynamics further complicates the implementation of Na+ conduction based on the ion–phonon interactions. At a given temperature above absolute zero, atoms thermally oscillate around their equilibrium sites within the crystal lattice until they are able to jump with sufficient energy.267 This is usually associated to the reduced activation barrier of Na+ migration under the low average vibrational frequency and low phonon density of states, or a defined descriptor known as the “phonon band center”.268 The low phonon band center has been tightly correlated with fast Na+ ion conduction. On the other hand, the constant vibration of skeletal anions can also soften the framework structure and lower the activation energy for Na+ migration, which are especially obvious for the anions with high polarizability and large size.269–271 While replacing S2− with more polarizable Se2− seems to effectively soften the lattice and mitigate the Na+ migration energy barrier in Na3PS4, this ion–phonon coupling effect from lattice dynamics may not necessarily obey the rule of “soft lattices are always better” since the Arrhenius prefactor σ0 (eqn (2)) also decreases with the activation barrier for ion conduction. Meanwhile, the low anion band center seems to worsen the electrochemical oxidation stability.272 Taking into account that the comprehensive and in-depth understanding of ion–phonon interactions is still missing, the effect of lattice dynamics on Na+ conduction should be analyzed based on specific materials and may not be appropriate to act as a universal designing principle.Recently, a special model of lattice dynamics of polyhedra has been unveiled in various types of sodium-ion SEs, named the “paddle-wheel” mechanism,273–275 to significantly promote the Na+ migration by the rotational polyanions (Fig. 9c), such as (BH4)−,276 (BnHn)2−,277,278 (CBn−1Hn)−,279,280 (PS4)3−,256,281 and (SbS4)3−.256 As the superionic conduction in some of ISEs, e.g., Na2B10H10, may mainly stem from the paddle-wheel mechanism, the rotational dynamics of these polyhedra assist Na+ hopping to a greater or lesser extent, by either widening the bottleneck area (Fig. 9d) or dynamically frustrating the energy landscape and expediting the cation mobility.

2.3. Microstructure scale
Grain boundary effect. So far, the abovementioned Na+ conduction mechanisms merely concern the cases within the grain bulk of ISEs; nonetheless the effect of grain boundaries and pore surfaces cannot be neglected since most of the ISEs are polycrystalline materials with microscopic defects. In fact, these internal interfaces, e.g., pores, cracks, and grain boundaries, can deteriorate the Na+ conductivity by several orders of magnitude, depending on their densities, tilting angles, and segregated impurities, even in the same materials with identical composition and crystal structure.282 Among these microstructure defects, the grain boundary is regarded as the biggest barrier for Na+ conduction because the kinetics of ion flow across grain boundaries tend to become extremely sluggish, which largely increases the activation energy for Na+ migration and reduces the total ionic conductivity of polycrystalline SEs. This phenomenon has been commonly observed in oxide-based,283,284 chalcogenide-based,285–287 and antiperovskite ISEs,288–290 even though the blocking effect from grain boundaries is usually less obvious in sulfide based SEs compared to that in oxide based SEs.291 Despite the difficulty in complete interpretation of the grain boundary effect from its fundamental origin, due to the limited spatial resolution of modern experimental techniques,291–294 the impedance of grain boundaries may be ascribed to the following reasons: (1) the formation of poorly conductive secondary phases; (2) the structural distortion in their core region; and (3) the depletion of charge carriers as a result of the space charge effect in the vicinity of grain boundaries (Fig. 7).295,296From the atomic scale to the microstructure scale in the ISEs, the conduction of Na+ exhibits multi-scale characteristics, depending on both dynamic and static factors, as well as some mysterious influences awaited to be revealed. Meanwhile, the evaluation of ISEs within SMBs is subject to several considerations, mainly including ionic conductivity and interfacial stability against Na metal. These components are entangled together in an extremely complex and interrelated manner, resulting in profound and complicated effects on the ISEs.

3. General principles of interfacial compatibility
As some of the SEs have already exhibited sufficiently high ionic conductivity at room temperature, the limiting factor to the performance of full-cell batteries shifts to the compatibility between SEs and electrodes, i.e., the degree to which two materials can work together at their interface without significant degradation or failure. Since SEs have always been regarded as the finest and ultimate solution to the high-energy-density SMBs, their interfacial compatibility with the metallic Na anode is another very important indicator. The interfacial compatibility between ISEs and the Na metal anode consists of several contributions, namely, chemical and electrochemical stability (thermodynamic stability), formation of the SEI layer (kinetic stability), and physical contact of the solid–solid interface. In other words, the interfacial compatibility may be described as interfacial stability and interfacial resistance.
The chemical stability of SEs is the first prerequisite for their practical application considering the inevitable impurities and interference from the ambient environment during operation or production, such as O2 and H2O. The chemical instability can lead to severe chemical reactions of SEs, thus degrading the battery and causing safety issues. For example, sulfide-based SEs are typically sensitive to air and water, and can be hydrolyzed by moisture releasing toxic H2S gas, while oxide-based SEs generally exhibit less air- and water-sensitivity ensuring reliable operation under ambient conditions.297,298 The electrochemical stability window (ESW) refers to the potential range of an SE within which it is thermodynamically stable, that is neither oxidized nor reduced. As illustrated by Fig. 10a, the ESW of an SE is represented by the band gap, i.e., the distance from the top of its valence band (VB) to the bottom of its conduction band (CB), corresponding to the highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) and the lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) in OLEs.299,300 If the ESW of an electrolyte is narrower than the electrochemical potentials of the anode and cathode (μA and μC, respectively), it will be reduced by the anode (μA > CB) or oxidized by the cathode (μC < VB), unless a passivation layer is formed by the decomposition products to impede the electron transfer between them and induce a high overpotential. This explains the instability of the carbonate electrolyte in the Na metal anode, because its LUMO is approximately 1 V below the μA of metallic Na.212 The large ESW of an electrolyte is the key to the high operating voltage and high energy density of the SMBs, as it determines the thermodynamic stability of SEs against the high-voltage cathode and Na metal. However, the actual stability window of ISEs usually deviates from the theoretical thermodynamic stability window, in that some “true” stability windows are wider than the theoretical ones and some are narrower. In fact, the narrower “true” stability window of SEs in practical application usually occurs in sulfide-based ISEs, where their ESW can be significantly overestimated by the tricky characterization apparatus and experimental settings (Section 5.1.3).301 Instead, the “true” ESW of SEs must be evaluated by using more sensitive equipment (current detection with pA-grade resolution) or increasing the contact area between the electrolyte and the working electrode during the test.302,303 On the other hand, the wider experimental stability window may originate from the in situ formation of the SEI layer to maintain the kinetic stability of the interface after the initial decomposition, where the decomposition products can cause sluggish kinetics and high overpotentials for further reactions (Section 5.2.3), thereby expanding the “true” ESW to a wider range and allowing additional options for cathode and anode.303–307 Recently, a novel concept of critical interphase overpotential was defined as an intrinsic property of the interface as a function of electronic/ionic conductivity, metal-phobicity and mechanical strength, that represents the resistance to dendrite growth at the interface.308 To summarize the thermodynamic stability and kinetic stability of the interface, three types of electrolyte/metal anode interfaces are outlined as shown in Fig. 10b, which are (I) the intrinsically thermodynamically stable interface without interfacial reaction, (II) the reactive but passivating SEI layer that blocks electron transfer and maintains kinetic stability, and (III) the unstable and reactive interface conducting both ions and electrons (mixed ion-electronic conducting interface, MCI),309,310 among which the first one is always preferred but difficult to achieve, while the second one is acceptable for long-term interfacial stability.
 Fig. 10  (a) The electrochemical stability window or thermodynamic stability range of SEs corresponding to three types of electrolyte/anode interfaces. (b) Three common types of in situ formed interfaces between SEs and the Na metal anode, which are the (i) thermodynamically stable interface, (ii) kinetically stable SEI layer, and (iii) unstable MCI layer. (c) Loss of interfacial contact between SEs and the Na metal anode due to the poor initial wettability (left panel) and the voids formed during the Na plating/stripping process (right panel). 
Another essential aspect of interfacial compatibility is the interfacial resistance, which originates from the initial wettability of Na metal on the SEs and the physical contact between them during the stripping and plating process of Na (Fig. 10c). This is particularly important for rigid SE ceramics with Na metal at room temperature, where the inferior Na+ transmission through the point contacts can significantly restrict the capacity and rate performance of the batteries, or facilitate the undesired side reactions and growth of metal dendrites.39,311–313 On the one hand, the micro-contacts between rigid SEs and Na metal (point–point contacts) result in a very limited contact area for the flux of Na+ or electrons, thus inducing a significantly increased local electric field and current density at these regions, which act as the hotbed for metal dendrite growth (Fig. 10c, left). Although SPEs are inherently flexible that better interfacial wetting of Na metal may be ensured, they generally have rather low Na+ transference numbers (<1) at room temperature, easily leading to ion depletion at the electrolyte/anode interface and formation of a space charge layer, thus creating a high local electric field promoting dendrite growth. On the other hand, even though good initial wettability has already been established at the interface between Na and SEs, the repeated stripping and plating process of Na inevitably changes the surface morphology of the metallic Na anode, thereby influencing the interfacial contact (Fig. 10c right). For example, loss of contact and formation of pores tend to occur as a result of the unduly accumulation of vacancies, when the stripping current density of Na exceeds the limiting value of Na vacancy diffusion in its bulk metal. The loss of physical contact at the interface not only increases the interfacial resistance, but also worsens the interfacial stability by disturbing the Na deposition and facilitating the metal dendrite growth.314
4. Oxide-based ISEs
4.1. Na-β-alumina
The β phase of alkali-metal alumina, namely β-alumina (β-Al2O3), was first identified during the investigation of the ternary system Ca2O–Al2O3–MgO early in 1916, though it has been interpreted as merely an allotropic modification of alumina (α-Al2O3) since then.315 Until 1936, when the trace of alkali oxide, e.g., Na2O, was discovered in its composition, Na-β-Al2O3 was recognized as an alkali aluminate with the empirical formula of Na2O·11Al2O3.316,317 The β′′ phase, Na-β′′-Al2O3, yielding the empirical formula of Na2O·5Al2O3, was subsequently found in the study of the Na2O–Al2O3 system.318 Since fast ion conduction within β-Al2O3 was unveiled in 1967, β-Al2O3 SE has largely encouraged the development of Na–S and Na–Cl high-temperature batteries.27,319–322 Meanwhile, several other compositions were discovered in the β-Al2O3 family, such as β′ and β′′′-Al2O3. Due to the great similarity in the crystal structure and minimal difference in composition, the Na-β-Al2O3 family, often mentioned as Na2O·nAl2O3, is tentatively categorized into two crystal structures, Na-β-Al2O3 (Na2O·8–11Al2O3) and Na-β′′-Al2O3 (Na2O·5–7Al2O3).320,323,324 Both β- and β′′-Al2O3 exhibit layered structures composed of alternate stacking of conduction planes and non-conducting slabs, in which the conduction plane is made by loose-packed O2− and mobile Na+, while the non-conducting slab, referred to as the spinel block, is close-packed by corner-sharing AlO4 tetrahedra and AlO6 octahedra.325,326 Such a sandwich structure provides two-dimensional diffusion channels for anisotropic ion conduction of Na+ ions, so they can move freely within the ‘ab’ planes under an applied electric field while their movement along the c axis is blocked by the dense spinel block layers.
4.1.1. Ion conduction in β/β′′-Al2O3. As shown in Fig. 11a, β-Al2O3 (hexagonal; P63/mmc; a0 = 0.559 nm, c0 = 2.261 nm)316,327 and β′′-Al2O3 (rhombohedral; Rm; a0 = 0.560 nm, c0 = 3.395 nm)324,328 have different chemical stoichiometries and stacking sequences of oxygen ions. β-Al2O3 is constituted by one layer of conduction plane sandwiched between two non-conducting spinel blocks, where each conduction plane contains only one mobile Na+. The ion conduction in the β-Al2O3 structure contributes to two of its important features, viz. the off-stoichiometric composition and the high disorder of the mobile ions.
 Fig. 11  (a) Crystal structures of sodium-β-alumina and sodium-β′′-alumina, adapted with permission from ref. 329, (b) interstitial oxygen stabilized by the Frenkel defect, (c) Frenkel defect accommodating two Na+ ions, (d) interstitialcy mechanism in the conduction planes of β-Al2O3. 
There are three crystallographically distinct sodium sites within the conduction plane of β-Al2O3, namely the Beevers–Ross (BR) sites, the mid-oxygen (mO) sites, and the anti-Beevers–Ross (aBR) sites.325 Although Na+ preferably occupies the BR sites, β-Al2O3 typically contains 15–30% excess Na+ over its ideal composition, resulting in Na+ ions outnumbering the available BR sites, where the excess Na+ are accommodated in the interstitial sites, some occupying mO sites and a very small amount in aBR sites.333,334 Noteworthily, despite the preferable occupation of Na+ in different sites, the site energy difference among them is found to be rather small, indicating the flat potential surface for Na+ in “fluid-like” conduction planes.335 The charge neutralization for the extra cations from the off-stoichiometry is achieved by the charge compensation mechanism of interstitial oxygen. To stabilize the interstitial oxygen, a Frenkel defect is intrinsically formed by moving two adjacent Al3+ from their lattice sites in spinel blocks to the interstitial sites on both sides of the conduction plane, as illustrated by Fig. 11b.335,336 Such a unique Frenkel defect (Fig. 11c), denoted by VAlAli–Oi2−–AliVAl, which associates each Oi2− in the conduction plane with an Al3+ vacancy/Al3+ interstitial pair in the spinel block, can accommodate two excessive Na+ while maintaining the charge neutrality of β-Al2O3.330,337 Meanwhile, it tends to induce large ion relaxation and leads to local disorder in its vicinity.335
Initially, the ion conduction within β-Al2O3 is believed to follow the interstitialcy mechanism, where Na+ ions at an BR site and aBR site (BR-aBR interstitialcy configuration) hop together in a pairwise pattern.338 Nonetheless, the partial occupancy of mO sites suggests a rather complex ion transport mechanism instead, as shown in Fig. 11d, especially considering the excessive sodium content due to the almost unavoidable off-stoichiometry.339 At low temperatures, all excessive Na+ ions are trapped in the region around the charge-compensating interstitial oxygen, referred to as the associated region, and the ion movement is negligible (Fig. 11d, left panel). The ion conduction occurs at elevated temperatures, where the mobile ions are thermally activated to escape the associated regions.331 At the initial stage, a configuration of interstitial pairs is formed by an initially occupied BR site and an aBR site occupied later by an excess interstitial Na+ ion (Fig. 11d, left panel). Then, the BR–aBR pair performs an interstitialcy jump to the mO–mO configuration (Fig. 10d, middle panel), followed by a random jump to either the original BR–aBR position or another neighboring BR–aBR configuration (Fig. 10d, right panel).331,332 In this way, the concerted motion of interstitial pairs renders low energy barriers for Na+ ion diffusion, hence the high ionic conductivity.331,340,341 As a result of off-stoichiometry and formation of charge-compensating defects, Na+ ions are found to be highly disordered within the rigid lattice of β-Al2O3, and their atomic positions usually deviate from the theoretical ones. The conduction paths within the disordered structure create a honeycomb network in the conduction plane, which allows ion transport with low energy barriers.337
In the unit cell of β′′-Al2O3, there are two conduction planes separated by three spinel blocks, where each conduction plane has two mobile Na+ distributed among two types of crystallographic sites, BR sites and mO sites, constituting conduction pathways undulating slightly above and below the ab plane due the threefold symmetry of the spinel blocks.342 With similarity to β-Al2O3 in the basic constitution of the lattice framework, β′′-Al2O3 has a higher concentration of BR sites than β-Al2O3, leading to more vacant Na+ sites in β′′-Al2O3 (about 17%) even when considering the higher concentration of Na+ in its conduction planes, which means the ion conduction within β′′-Al2O3 is dominantly driven by the vacancy mechanism.343 Meanwhile, the correlated ion motion near the vacancies corresponds to an extremely low activation energy of diffusion with only 0.02 eV.344 In addition, the larger volume of the unit cell and higher Na+ concentration in β′′-Al2O3 benefit the facile ion transport. The single-crystal β′′-Al2O3 shows ionic conductivity as high as 0.1 S cm−1 at room temperature and 1 S cm−1 at 300 °C,345 while the more accessible and common polycrystalline β′′-Al2O3 typically exhibits an ionic conductivity of 0.22–0.33 S cm−1 at 300 °C due to the significant impedance from grain boundaries,346,347 but still about an order of magnitude higher than that of polycrystalline β-Al2O3.348 Speaking of which, the blocking effect of grain boundaries largely contributes to the total ionic conductivity of this polycrystalline material.349–351 As the grain size and porosity determine the path length of ion conduction, a longer distance must be crossed by ions within materials that have numerous small grains and pores. As a proof, the coarse-grained polycrystalline β′′-Al2O3 exhibits higher ionic conductivity than the fine-grained one due to the lower concentration of grain boundaries and less impedance to the ion transport within conduction planes.347 Moreover, the disordered arrangement of abnormally growing grains in polycrystalline β′′-Al2O3 ceramics can also result in both low mechanical strength and tortuous ion migration paths.352
Although the β′′ phase is preferred in terms of ionic conductivity, the practically used beta-alumina solid electrolyte, known as BASE, usually consists of both polycrystalline β and β′′ phases because the attempts to synthesize the homogeneous β′′ phase often end up with mixtures with β phase as well as other undesired phases as by-products. By a similar token, pure single-phase β′′-Al2O3 is not commercially appropriate due to its high cost of synthesis, thermal instability, poor mechanical strength and sensitivity to humidity.353 In fact, the ionic conductivity of β/β′′ phase-mixed BASE largely depends on several factors, i.e., composition, β/β′′ phase ratio, and microstructure. Therefore, the modification strategies and synthesis approaches to effectively enhance its ion conduction are based on composition optimization, β/β′′ phase ratio tuning, and microstructure refinement.

4.1.2. Modification strategies for ion conduction. 
Aliovalent doping and isovalent substitution. As the ionic conductivity of both β- and β′′-Al2O3 is closely related to the amount of excess Na+ ions accommodated in the interstitial sites, composition optimization of BASE is represented by increasing the solubility of Na+ within the conduction planes, hence increasing the effective concentration. Apart from the abovementioned intrinsic charge-compensating Frenkel defect that harbors two Na+ for each interstitial oxygen, incorporation of excess Na+ can be managed through aliovalent ion doping of Al3+ sites in the spinel blocks, by which the concentration of Na+ can be greatly altered to induce significant deviation of stoichiometry. Through doping lower-charge cations into the Al3+ sites, a charge imbalance initiates either less aluminum vacancies in the spinel block, or the accommodation of a much greater number of Na+ and the reduced amount of interstitial O2− in the conduction planes.354 Meanwhile, some of the dopant ions, either isovalent or aliovalent, can stabilize the β′′ structure and suppress the sodium loss during its high-temperature processing (T > 1600 °C) by filling the tetrahedral or octahedral voids in the spinel blocks, and thus are referred to as stabilizers. An ideal stabilizer ion should have ionic radius less than 0.97 Å because small cations (r < 0.97 Å) can enter the spinel block while large cations (r > 0.97 Å) are not capable of doing so and they may replace Na+ in the conduction planes or enlarge the area of ordered microdomains.355 Among numerous candidates, Li+ and Mg2+ are the most preferred choices to enrich the Na+ content, yielding the ideal composition of Na1.67Al10.33Mg0.67O17 and Na1.67Al10.67Li0.33O17.321,356 Several other dopants have also been validated to effectively enhance the ionic conductivity of BASE, such as divalent cations Cd2+,357 Co2+,358 Cu2+,359 Zn2+,360 Fe2+,361 and Mn2+,362 trivalent cation Cr3+,363 tetravalent cation Ti4+,361,364 pentavalent cation Ta5+,362 etc. For example, by doping 1.5%wt TiO2 in Li+-stabilized β′′-Al2O3, the β′′ phase can be increased to as high as 93.4% in Na-β′′-alumina and the ionic conductivity is increased from 0.2 S cm−1 to 0.3 S cm−1 at 300 °C.364 In this way, the properly selected dopants can simultaneously increase the concentration of Na+ and tune the β′′/β ratio, corresponding to faster ion conduction.The effects of ion doping in β′′-Al2O3 can go beyond the modification of its composition and phase ratio, in that its microstructure can also be regulated, including grain boundaries, porosity, crystallographic orientation, etc. Meanwhile, the grain size must be properly modified as excessive grain growth causes deterioration of mechanical strength.365,366 Some of the doping ions, such as Y3+367 and Ti4+,364 are prone to appear at the grain boundaries instead of the grain bulk and mitigate the grain boundary resistance. For example, on increasing the TiO2 doping amount, the intragranular ion conduction in β′′-Al2O3 remains unchanged, but the intergranular ionic conduction becomes much faster as the material is dominated by larger grains along with a reduced amount of grain boundaries. To modulate the grain size and porosity, a small amount of MgO was doped into β′′-Al2O3 ceramics, which not only retained up to 95% of the β′′ phase content after a high-temperature processing, but also densified the ceramics (relative density > 98.1%) by forming a second phase (MgAl2O4) at the grain boundaries, thus inhibiting the grain growth and lowering the porosity for a more homogeneous and compact microstructure.368 The higher densification and mechanical strength have also been achieved by doping ZrO2 and TiO2 into the pristine β′′-Al2O3 ceramics, which exhibited a mechanical strength of 192 MPa and an ionic conductivity of 2.8 × 10−1 S cm−1 at 350 °C.369 As a consequence, the improved mechanical strength allows the fabrication of thin-film BASE with a thickness of only 50 μm and an ionic conductivity of 5.4 mS cm−1, enabling the flat cell designs and room-temperature operations.370 A high degree of grain oriented alignment within BASE was accomplished by Ma et al., who synthesized Li-doped Na1.67Al10.67Li0.33O17 through the freeze-drying technique, ending in a high ionic conductivity of 1.852 × 10−1 S cm−1 at 350 °C.371 In this regard, the fabrication of a homogeneous, dense, compact, and well-oriented microstructure with refined grain size for faster ion conduction BASE is synergistically affected by the synthesis and processing conditions, which also determine the composition and phase ratio in the final products due to the inevitable sodium loss and β′′-structure instability at high temperature.
Optimizing the synthesis and processing conditions. Interestingly, as the ionic conductivity of β-Al2O3 ceramics depends on both mobile ion concentration and the strongly bonded ion conducting framework, these two important factors seem to be mutually conflicting and hardly achieved during the traditional synthesis process, i.e., the dense structure usually requires long-time high-temperature sintering while the sodium tends to evaporate at elevated temperatures. As the most common synthesis approach for industrialized BASE, the conventional solid-state reaction usually requires a sintering temperature of around 1600 °C, which facilitates the loss of sodium, decomposition of desired β′′-Al2O3 and formation of the undesired β phase and NaAlO2, as well as the abnormal grain growth with tortuous ion migration pathways, deteriorating both electrochemical and mechanical property.27,322,347,353,372–375 Thus, the optimization of synthesis routes and processing conditions should start off by either lowering the sintering temperature and shortening the sintering time, or orientally aligning the grains and refining their size. Inspired by the strategy of applying pressure to acquire densified and grain-oriented structures,376 β′′-Al2O3 ceramics with their flake-like crystals aligned in the direction of the c-axis were prepared via hot-pressing, which largely mitigated the ion migration energy and improved the ionic conductivity.377 However, as they still experienced the high-temperature sintering for a long time, the grains could hardly get away with the abnormal growth, ending up with a duplex microstructure containing mixed-size grains and grain matrix.326,346,347 To eliminate the undesired products and abnormal behaviors, the sintering temperature and time must be decreased (ideally < 1300 °C and <30 min, respectively). Freeze-drying and spark plasma sintering (SPS) methods have been adopted to overcome these concerns, as well as to achieve grain growth with preferential orientation. Without dopants or additives added, a highly grain-oriented β′′-Al2O3 ceramic was prepared via the SPS technique, which lowered the sintering temperature to 1300 °C (10 min), thus achieving an ionic conductivity of 1.83 × 10−1 S cm−1 at 350 °C with a bending strength of 250 MPa, almost double that of the ones with abnormal grain growth.352,368,378 Another c-axis oriented β′′-Al2O3 ceramic with anisotropic ionic conduction was obtained via the SPS method, resulting in a total ionic conductivity of 19 mS cm−1 at room temperature.379 Later, Li et al. adopted both freeze-drying and SPS techniques to synthesize BASE with both higher bending strength and ionic conductivity of 273 MPa and 2.39 × 10−1 S cm−1 at 350 °C, respectively, attributed to the formation of a “brick-bridge-mortar” crystal structure that provides better mechanical strength, in addition to the reduction of grain boundary resistance from larger-sized grain groups and shortened ion migration paths.380 Cold sintering dramatically lowered the sintering temperature below 400 °C for densification of BASE, which resulted in a theoretical density over 90% and an ionic conductivity of 7.6 × 10−3 S cm−1 at 300 °C, as the carbonates and water molecules contained in the sample could not be removed at such a low temperature which greatly deteriorated its room-temperature ionic conductivity.381 Another alternative preparation method, vapor-phase synthesis, was also demonstrated to benefit from the restrained sodium loss and full conversion of β′′-Al2O3 at lower temperature, as well as maintaning the grain size throughout the reactions.382–385 Impressively, the precursor has a huge impact on the product quality of BASE as well. By choosing precursors from the low-cost and more available hydroxyl alumina group, e.g., boehmite and bayerite, single-phase β′′-Al2O3 can be acquired through the solid state reaction, along with reduction of the sintering temperature and suppression of sodium loss.386,387In addition to the abovementioned attempts to optimize the preparation of BASE, a variety of approaches have been developed, including but not limited to the sol–gel process,388–390 co-precipitation technique,391 spray-freeze/freeze-drying method,392,393 microwave heating,374,394 and mechanochemical methods,395 though little effect was observed on the enhancement of ion conduction.

4.1.3. Compatibility with the Na metal anode. BASE is considered thermodynamically stable against the Na metal anode at 0 V vs. Na/Na+ according to density functional theory (DFT) calculations, except for NaAl11O17 that can be reduced into Al and NaAlO2 below 0.14 V, while its decomposition is rather slow with reaction energy <25 meV per atom.396 The oxidative limit of BASE is approximately 4 V, but the upper threshold shifts to lower values for increasing Na content. Its interfacial stability against the Na metal anode has been investigated in the symmetric Na/β′′-Al2O3/Na cell, where BASE exhibited extraordinary chemical and electrochemical stability against Na metal, without interfacial degradation or reaction products observed over a long time period.240 Such a remarkable interfacial stability of BASE is consistent with the previous literature and has been widely proved in numerous Na-BASE battery systems.397,398 Surprisingly, after delicate heat treatment that eliminated the hydroxyl groups and carbon contaminations on the surface of BASE, the symmetric Na/β′′-Al2O3/Na cell endured a CCD up to 12 mA cm−2 at room temperature, which was ten times as high as that of garnet-type Li7La3Zr2O12 (LLZO) from the same testing condition.399,400 Despite the wide ESW and compatibility with the Na metal anode, room-temperature-operated BASE is susceptible to penetration by sodium dendrites along grain boundaries when charged above a certain current density, where the rapid penetration, about 1 cm s−1, was observed when the current density was increased to 200 mA cm−2.214,401 On the other hand, compared to that in high-temperature operation with molten Na, sufficient wettability of Na metal on the surface of BASE may be harder to achieve at room temperature. The inadequately wetted surface may result in instability and high interfacial resistance, especially when impurities reside at the interface between BASE and the metallic Na anode, e.g., calcium, which tends to form an oxidized surface film impeding the ion transport.402,403 The penetration of metal dendrites and degradation of the interface between BASE and the metallic Na anode are mostly ascribed to the mechanical-stress-induced bulk deformation/cracks (Fig. 12a), poor initial wetting at room temperature (Fig. 12b) and gradual formation of voids during cycling (Fig. 12c).404–407 Moreover, BASE suffers from serious instability in humid air because of its sensitivity to water, which is especially obvious in β′′-Al2O3, thus forming a sodium oxide layer on the surface, deteriorating the interfacial wetting of Na. The occlusion of water and diffusion of hydronium ions occur in different time spans, and both greatly increase the ionic resistance of BASE.408–411 Meanwhile, the undesired by-product NaAlO2 commonly formed at the grain boundaries is also prone to react with water and carbon dioxide, thereby making BASE unstable under ambient conditions.
 Fig. 12  The most common cause of interface degradation and metal dendrite penetration at the interface between BASE and the Na metal anode. (a) The three-dimensional (3D) imaging of sodium dendrite penetration and pellet cracking in BASE after the rapid short-circuit of the symmetric Na/BASE cell, obtained by ex situ X-ray microcomputed tomography, reprinted with permission from ref. 404. (b) The solid–solid initial contact between rigid BASE and the Na metal anode at room temperature, compared to molten metallic Na intimately wetting the surface of BASE at elevated temperature, adapted with permission from ref. 407. (c) Asymmetric Na stripping and plating process gradually forming voids and causing loss of interface contact during the cycling of the Na anode with BASE, adapted with permission from ref. 406. 

4.1.4. Interfacial modification of BASE for the Na anode. The early commercialized Na–S batteries have already shown huge success operating at elevated temperature around 300 °C while using molten Na and S as electrodes and β′′-Al2O3 as the SE, which provides a theoretical capacity of 1672 mA h g−1 based on the complete reaction from S to Na2S.412,413 In the past few decades, various solid-state cell systems have been developed using BASE, including thin-film batteries414 and Na metal batteries, committed to achieve high energy density at lowered operating temperature. According to the previous section (Section 4.1.3), the major focus on the interfacial modification of BASE in the Na anode should emphasize on (1) reducing the bulk and surface defects, including grain boundaries, micro-cracks, surface impurities or contaminants; (2) improving the intimate interfacial contact between BASE and the Na anode at the initial stage and during cycling; (3) minimizing the interfacial impedance caused by the rigid interfaces and (4) avoiding water intake from the ambient environment.The interfacial modification of BASE and Na metal is mainly achieved by four strategies, which are heat treatment, surface coating, composite electrolytes and ultrasound welding. A simple heat treatment of BASE in an argon atmosphere removed the hydroxides and carbonate impurities on its surface, which effectively reduced the interfacial resistance in a symmetric Na/BASE/Na cell from ∼20 kΩ cm−2 to 8 Ω cm−2 and enabled the ultra-high CCD of 12 mA cm−2 at room temperature.399 Spencer et al. also validated this method to eliminate surface contaminants and reduce the interfacial impendence between Na and BASE through X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and AC impedance spectroscopy.406 Introducing a coating layer on the surface of the BASE has long been demonstrated to significantly mitigate the poor wetting of Na metal at lower temperatures,321,415–420 e.g., nanowire networks, Na alloys, thin films, carbon coating, and micron-sized particles. A novel inorganic/organic composite electrolyte was fabricated by compactly coating PVDF–HFP-based GPE on a cross-linked β/β′′-Al2O3 nanowire (AN) membrane to ensure a smooth electrolyte/Na interface and inhibited dendrite growth.421 The full cell battery using this composite BASE electrolyte with the Na3V2(PO4)3(NVP) cathode and the Na metal anode exhibited a long cycle life of 1000 cycles at 1C and 25 °C, with a capacity retention of 95.3%. Another composite electrolyte, consisting of β-Al2O3 nanoparticles embedded in the poly(vinylidene fluoride-co-hexafluoropropylene)/poly(methyl methacrylate)-based gel polymer electrolyte membrane (PVDF-HFP/PMMA-based GPE), was used in a full-cell battery with the NVP cathode and the Na metal anode, which exhibited an initial discharge capacity of 94.1 mA h g−1 with 85% of capacity retained after 300 cycles at 0.5C at room temperature.422 Deng et al. prepared a composite electrolyte by incorporating yttria-stabilized zirconia (YSZ) into BASE, where the added YSZ phase assisted the formation of a stabilized YSZ@BASE/Na interface, meanwhile inducing an “interface anchoring effect” ensuring the strong contact between the Na metal anode and the SE. As a result, the quasi-solid-state battery NaNi0.45Cu0.05Mn0.4Ti0.1O2/YSZ@BASE/Na, with a trace amount of eutectic NaFSI–KFSI molten salt at the cathode surface, achieved a high initial capacity of 110 mA h g−1 at 1C and good cycling stability of 500 cycles at 4C, with the average Coulombic efficiency over 99.99% and a capacity retention of 73% at 80°C.423 Recently, the atomic-level ultrasound welding technique was applied to weld the rigid interfaces between BASE and the Na metal anode, ensuring a compact interface for lower interfacial impedance and suppressed dendrite growth.424 The seminal development of β/β′′-Al2O3, as one of the very earliest SEs, has unfolded the potential of SE systems and motivated the following research of room-temperature solid-state SMBs, which is continuously inspired by novel material fabrication and cell designs.425

4.2. NASICON
To overcome the restriction from anisotropic ion conduction in the layered structure of BASE, an ionic conductor with equivalently fast ion conduction in three dimensions is desired. In 1976, Goodenough et al. and Hong proposed a highly conductive skeletal structure, namely NASICON (Natrium(Na)Super(S)Ionic(I)Conductor(CON)), where alkali ions partially occupy the interconnected interstitial spaces, donating electrons to stabilize the skeletal array of atoms and constructing open 3D channels for Na+ ion transport.426,427 As the mobile ion is not limited to Na+, the empirical formula of NASICON-structured materials is denoted by AM2X3O12, where A can be alkali cations (Na, Li, K, etc.), alkaline earth ions (Mg, Ca, Sr, Ba, etc.) and other mobile ions, or it can be vacant; M can be multivalent transition metal ions maintaining charge balance; and X is usually P or partially replaced by Si or As. The rigid 3D covalent framework of NASICON is made of interconnected polyhedra, where the corner-sharing MO6 octahedra and XO4 tetrahedra constitute so-called lantern units and these XO4-linked lanterns form infinite ribbons along the c-axis, which creates interstitial sites enough to accommodate up to 5 alkali cations per structural formula, providing conduction tunnels for mobile ions.428 The crystal structure of NASICON varies with its composition, from rhombohedral, monoclinic, triclinic, orthorhombic, Langbeinite, Garnet, and SW type to corundum-like.429
4.2.1. Composition and crystal structure. The originally proposed and most widely studied NASICON-structured SE is NaxZr2Six−1P4−xO12 (NZSPO), where 1 ≤ x ≤ 4, which is derived from partial substitution of P5+ by Si4+ in NaZr2P3O12 and the subsequent addition of excess Na+ ions to compensate charge imbalance. As a solid solution of binary NaZr2P3O12–Na4Zr2Si3O12, NZSPO exhibits two distinct crystal structures depending on different compositions and temperatures. The rhombohedral structure with Rc symmetry presents at room temperature (ar = 8.815(1) Å, cr = 22.746(7) Å) except for the composition with 2.8 ≤ x ≤ 3.2, where a slight shear distortion occurs, turning the structure into a monoclinic structure with C2/c symmetry (am = 15.586(9) Å, bm = 9.029(4) Å, cm = 9.205(5) Å, β = 123.70(5)°).426,427 The monoclinic structure is considered as a low-temperature phase which experiences a phase transformation to the high-temperature phase of rhombohedral structure at elevated temperature above 150 °C.430 When the ionic conductivity of NASICON is plotted versus Na+ content, viz. the variable x, an occurrence of curvature is observed corresponding to the phase transformation, in that the monoclinic NZSPO exhibited a higher ionic conductivity of 0.67 mS cm−1 at room temperature and 0.2 S cm−1 at 300 °C when x = 3, i.e. Na3Zr2Si2PO12, despite its lower symmetry compared to that of the rhombohedral structure.426,427 Initially, this phenomenon was explained by the reduced energy barrier for Na+ ion conduction because of the stronger Na+ interaction, the presence of the high-energy Na+ site, and the enlarged cell volume in the monoclinic structure. In later research, this observation was challenged by discovering the higher room-temperature bulk ionic conductivity of 15 mS cm−1 and total ionic conductivity of 5 mS cm−1 in Na3.4Zr2Si2.4P0.6O12, due to a preferable ratio of occupied/vacant Na+ sites, as well as the activated correlated ion migration.431 In this case, the optimum Na+ content is determined as approximately 3.4 mol per formula unit in the equivalent system of Nax+yMx−1M′3−xSiyP3−yO12.431–437 This theory will be further expanded by elaborating the ion conduction mechanism within NASICON. Note that in this particular review article focusing on sodium-ion ISEs, the nomination of NASICON and NZSPO can be interchangeable considering the excessive variety of NASICON-structured materials.
4.2.2. Ion conduction in NASICON. In both rhombohedral and monoclinic phases, the (Zr2Si2PO12)3− framework is constructed by corner-linked ZrO6 octahedra and SiO4/PO4 tetrahedra, while Na+ ions reside in the cavities of the skeletal framework, as shown in Fig. 13a. The bulk ionic conductivity of NASICON relies on both concentration and mobility of effective charge carriers, where the former is determined by Na+ site occupation and the latter is mostly related to the structural geometry.
 Fig. 13  (a) The crystal structure of the rhombohedral (top panel) and monoclinic phase (bottom panel) of NZSPO, adapted with permission from ref. 438. (b) The ideal ratio of mobile Na+ ions and Na+ vacancies for fastest single-ion hopping, in which min(ΔE) represents the minimum absolute difference in electrostatic energies between the ground-state (GS) site and the excited state (ES), adapted with permission from ref. 439 and 440. (c) Correlated-ion motions in NZSPO with corresponding migration energy landscapes in comparison with that of single-ion hopping, adapted with permission from ref. 439. 
Concentration of mobile charge carriers. According to the common belief, there are two distinct Na+ sites in the rhombohedral structure, namely NaR1 (one per formula) and NaR2 (three per formula). NaR1 is coordinated by oxygen atoms from ZrO6 octahedra in an antiprism geometry, while NaR2 is coordinated to neighboring oxygen atoms from ZrO6 octahedra and SiO4/PO4 tetrahedra in a distorted cubic coordination, respectively.440 Driven by the Na-vacancy mechanism, ion conduction within NZSPO is attributed to the disordered distribution and well-balanced concentration of both mobile Na+ ions and available vacancies, where either overly excessive or insufficient sodium content in the NASICON structure results in deficient ionic conductivity.432 When only one Na+ is incorporated per formula, corresponding to NaZr2P3O12, merely the NaR1 site is occupied and all other NaR2 sites are left vacant, resulting in extremely high resistivity of ion conduction in this compound. When charge-compensating Na+ ions are introduced by substitution of Si for P in NASICON, the occupation shifts to NaR2 along with the variable x increasing in NaxZr2Six−1P4−xO12 as the partial site occupation of mobile Na+ ions distributes over several inequivalent Na+ sites in a disordered manner.426,427 Recently, an additional NaR3 site has been proposed as a metastable site for Na+ migration in the NaR1–NaR2 diffusion pathways where only very few Na+ ions occupy this type of site.436,439,441 As the concentration of mobile Na+ ions increases in the range of 1 ≤ x ≤ 3 in NaxZr2Six−1P4−xO12, the reduced site occupation of NaR1 induces stronger electrostatically repulsive force between the ZrO6 octahedra piled in the c-axis direction, leading to the expansion of cell volume alongside the room-temperature phase transition from rhombohedral to monoclinic.426,440 Due to the slight structure distortion in phase transition, those Na+ sites in the rhombohedral structure split into numerous inequivalent Na+ sites in the monoclinic structure, regardless of a great discrepancy still existing in the ambiguous Na+ site and migration pathways. In contrast to the previous assertion of three427,442–445 or four types of Na+ sites,441 five types of Na+ sites have been recognized after a detailed reexamination of the Na+ arrangement in the monoclinic phase,436 where each one of the NaR2 and NaR3 site is divided into NaM2 + NaM3 sites and NaM4 + NaM5 sites, respectively.
