All five occupied Na+ sites contribute to ion conduction, even including the high-energy NaM5 sites with the lowest occupation of 0.12 among all. An additional site NaM6 was found to be split from the NaR3 sites, but this empty interstitial NaM6 site only acts as an interstices allowing Na+ ions passing through, instead of an equilibrium site.438 As the Na+ content increases in NZSPO, followed by phase transition and cell volume expansion, as well as a redistribution of partially occupied Na+ sites, the ionic conductivity of NASICON increases to a limiting value until a certain amount of Na+. Based on the single-ion vacancy hopping mechanism, such a locally highest ionic conductivity of NZSPO is achieved when x = 3, i.e., Na3Zr2Si2PO12.440,446 At this Na+ concentration, the difference of site energy is minimized on the hopping path from the “start point” to the “end point”, indicating a flat electrostatic energy landscape and therefore a low migration barrier (Fig. 13b).However, as single-ion migrations dominate in the stoichiometries with the low range of Na+ concentrations, correlated ion migrations can be triggered when x reaches 3.4 in NaxZr2Six−1P4−xO12.431 Correlated ion migration that involves simultaneous collective motion of several atoms manifests a much lower energy barrier than single-ion migration (Fig. 13c). This correlated ion migration is activated by the stronger Coulombic repulsion between the migrating Na+ and the neighboring mobile Na+ in the environment of highly concentrated Na+ ions.292,447,448 Due to the necessary assistance from available vacancies to accomplish the correlated ion migration, the optimal sodium content is determined as 3.3–3.55 mol per formula unit.
Structure determined migration pathways and bottlenecks. The mobility of Na+ in NASICON is strongly related to its crystal structure considering that the mobile Na+ must move along the 3D migration channels scaffolded by the skeletal framework. The (Zr2Si2PO12)3− framework in NZSPO consists of octahedra and tetrahedra bridged by oxygen atoms, while every three oxygen atoms create a triangular area in the migration pathways that mobile ions must squeeze through. These triangular areas are called “bottlenecks”, as shown in Fig. 14a, and their size determines the difficulty, or the energy barrier, of inter-site Na+ migrations. In the rhombohedral structure, there are two bottlenecks in the NaR1–NaR2 pathway, viz. TR1 and TR2, corresponding to NaR1 and NaR2 sites, respectively, in the shape of rings circled by an edge of ZrO6 octahedra and two vertices of SiO4/PO4 tetrahedra. Later, an additional type of TR3 bottleneck emerges along with the presence of the NaR3 site. In this case, Na+ must pass through the TR1 bottleneck for NaR2–NaR3 migration, the TR2 bottleneck for NaR3–NaR1 migration, and the TR3 bottleneck for NaR3–NaR3 migration (Fig. 14b). These short-range segments constitute long-range ion conduction pathways, i.e. NaR1–NaR3–NaR2–NaR3–NaR1 and NaR2–NaR3–NaR3–NaR2 channels, among which the first is preferred.439 These bottlenecks represent the narrowest gate in the ion conduction pathways, thus requiring the smallest diameter of its area to be greater than twice the sum of anion and alkali-ion radii for unhindered ion hops, which is 4.7 Å in the case of Na+ conduction within an oxide.427,438 For example, the TR1 bottleneck is smaller than the other bottlenecks in rhombohedral NZSPO; therefore its size becomes the critical factor for Na+ ion conduction. It turns out that the activation energy of Na+ transport decreases in a linear manner with the increasing size of the TR1 bottleneck.449 The excessive Na+ sites in the monoclinic structure of NZSPO result in even more convoluted Na+ ion conduction pathways and more complex bottleneck regions. In each monoclinic cell, there are four equivalent NaM1–NaM6–NaM2–NaM6–NaM1 channels and eight equivalent NaM1–NaM4–NaM3–NaM5–NaM1 channels mainly responsible for long-range ion motions, as well as some relatively less conductive local channels around NaM1 sites such as NaM4–NaM5, NaM4–NaM6, and NaM5–NaM6 channels. Correspondingly, nine distinguishable bottlenecks are formed along these migration pathways, including four (two of them are identical) in each NaM1–NaM6–NaM2–NaM6–NaM1 channel, four in each NaM1–NaM4–NaM3–NaM5–NaM1 channel, and other three of them along the channels around NaM1 (Fig. 14c).438 One of the bottlenecks in the NaM1–NaM6–NaM2–NaM6–NaM1 channel, namely TMB, has a diameter of 4.46 Å which is smaller than twice the sum of O2− and Na+ radii, thus becoming the limiting factor for the ion jump events in this channel. On the other hand, the bottlenecks in the NaM1–NaM4–NaM3–NaM5–NaM1 channel have diameters larger than the appropriate size, with the smallest bottleneck TMD larger than 4.76 Å, thereby allowing Na+ migration through this tunnel and the occupancies of Na+ sites along this type of channel becoming important factors for fast ion conduction.
 Fig. 14  (a) The Na+ hopping determined by bottleneck size in the NASICON structure. Identified Na+ migration pathways and the corresponding bottlenecks in the (b) rhombohedral NZSPO anion framework, adapted with permission from ref. 439, and (c) monoclinic NZSPO anion framework, adapted with permission from ref. 438. 
To accurately measure the impact of skeletal framework geometry on the mobile Na+, a geometrical parameter reff is defined in a more general form of NASICON-structured materials Na1+2w+x−y+zM(II)x−1M(III)3−xM(V)yM(IV)2−w−x−y(SiO4)z(PO4)3−z, as the effective mean ionic radius of the cations M(II), M(III), M(V), and M(IV), calculated by eqn (7),449 
(7)The optimum value of reff has been determined as 0.72 Å, where the NASICON structure exhibits a higher ionic conductivity. This geometrical parameter is very important in the application of the aliovalent doping strategy in the pristine NZSPO structure for effective enhancement of bulk ionic conductivity, i.e., rMg2+ ≈ rZr4+ ≈ 0.72 Å.450
Blocking effect of grain boundaries. Akin to β-Al2O3 and all other oxide-based ISEs, grain boundaries and pores have non-negligible effects on the total ionic conductivity of NASICON-structured materials, especially at low temperatures (<100 °C), in that the grain boundary conductivity, σGB, becomes predominantly crucial compared to the bulk material conductivity.451 Meanwhile, the grain boundary impedance can be further escalated by the preferential formation of insulating impurity ZrO2 along the grain boundaries, because of the volatilization of sodium and phosphorus content after the high-temperature processing or low reactivity of monoclinic ZrO2 in precursors.452 Worse still, the presence of grain boundaries favors the rapid growth of sodium dendrites along the small crevices and eventually the penetration. As a matter of fact, it is this grain-boundary-dendrite-growth mechanism that overthrows the resistance to metal dendrite penetration by the rigid and stiff ceramics. After overviewing the limiting factors to ion conduction within NASICON, the enhancement of ionic conductivity should stress on (1) widening the bottleneck area to construct free ion migration pathways, (2) tuning the concentration of effective charge carriers or triggering the correlated ion migrations, (3) decorating the grain boundary facilitating total ion conduction, and (4) preparing fine-microstructure and dense ceramics.

4.2.3. Modification strategies for fast ion conduction. 
Aliovalent doping or isovalent substitution. To improve the bulk ionic conductivity of NASICON, charge carrier concentration tuning and bottleneck broadening are usually accomplished by directly adding excess sodium content and/or aliovalent/isovalent substitution of the skeletal atoms. For example, an addition of 10 at% excess sodium content into pristine Na3Zr2Si2PO12 substantially increases the total ionic conductivity to around 1.2 × 10−1 S cm−1 at 300 °C, by slightly enlarging the unit cell volume and expanding the smallest bottleneck TMB.451 As the most effective approach to simultaneously increase the concentration of charge carriers and tailor the bottleneck size, aliovalent or isovalent substitution typically happens at the Zr4+ site in NZSPO. The common dopants include divalent Mg2+,284,453,454 Co2+,455 Zn2+,455,456 Ni2+,284,455 and Ca2+;457 trivalent Y3+,455,456,458–460 La3+,434,458,460 and Sc3+;433,461–463 tetravalent Ti4+453,456 and Ce4+;464–466 or pentavalent V5+,453 Nb5+,467 and Ta5+,453 among which the Mg2+, Y3+, La3+ and Sc3+-doped NZSPO exhibit the most outstanding results in enhancing the ionic conductivity. On the one hand, aliovalent doping introduces excess sodium content in the crystal lattice under charge compensation, in that the influx of mobile Na+ in the conducting pathways evokes faster ion conduction. On the other hand, doping lower-charge cations at the Zr4+ site weakens the electrostatic interactions between stationary cations and mobile Na+, thereby reducing the migration energy required for Na+ hopping through bottlenecks.455 For example, through the substitution of Zr4+ by Zn2+, with similar ionic radii, Na3.4Zr1.8Zn0.2Si2PO12 exhibited a bulk ionic conductivity of 3.75 mS cm−1 at room temperature, which is approximately three times higher than that of the undoped NASICON.455 Another Zn2+-doped NZSPO, Na3.4Zr1.9Zn0.1Si2.2P0.8O12, exhibited an ultra-high ionic conductivity of 5.27 mS cm−1 at room temperature upon simultaneously increasing the Si/P ratio.468 The investigation of its structural geometry revealed that the substitution of Zn2+ for Zr4+ enlarged the bottlenecks in the main Na+ migration pathways. The size effect can be substantially obvious by doping ions with different sizes at the Zr4+ site, i.e., bottleneck expansion and creation of free space for ion migration, thus reducing the activation energy and facilitating ion conduction. As mentioned in Section 4.2.2, the ionic conductivity of the NASICON structure is optimized when the effective mean ionic radius reff is as close to 0.72 Å as possible. In NZSPO, this theory implies that the doping ions substituted at the Zr4+ (r = 0.72 Å) site should have the same or a little larger size for ideal structure adjustment, e.g. Mg2+ (r = 0.72 Å) and Sc3+ (r = 0.745 Å),449,454 and not much smaller sizes, such as Co2+ (0.65 Å, 0.16 mS cm−1) and Al3+ (0.54 Å, 0.44 mS cm−1).455 Song et al. demonstrated that doping Mg2+ into NZSPO not only increases the concentration of charge carriers, but also significantly enlarges the smallest bottleneck (in Mg2+-doped ∼6.522 Å2 vs. in undoped ∼5.223 Å2), resulting in a high total ionic conductivity of 3.5mS cm−1 for Na3.1Zr1.95Mg0.05Si2PO12 at room temperature.454 Nonetheless, this remarkable outcome of Mg2+ doping was doubted by later research, particularly with such a low doping level of 0.05 at% Mg, and replication of the same composition only delivered an ionic conductivity of 1.3 mS cm−1.284 Accordingly, the scandium-substituted NASICON Na3.4Sc0.4Zr1.6Si2PO12 exhibits a superior ionic conductivity of 4 mS cm−1 at room temperature,433 while another Mg2+-doped NZSPO, Na3.3Zr1.85Mg0.15Si2PO12, delivers an ionic conductivity of 3.54 mS cm−1 at room temperature.469 By isovalent replacement of Zr4+ with Hf4+, the as-synthesized Na3.2Hf2Si2.2P0.8O12 possessed a high ionic conductivity of 2.3 mS cm−1.470 Not so commonly, the ionic conductivity improvement was achieved by F− partially occupying the O2− sites via the NaF additive, which promoted the exsolution of Na3PO4 and ZrO2 and increased the Si/P ratio and Na+ concentration, leading to the high ionic conductivity of 4.98 mS cm−1, while the Hf-co-doped novel Na3.2Hf2Si2.2P0.8O11.85F0.3 displayed an ionic conductivity of 2.39 mS cm−1.471,472 Meanwhile, the Ca2+-doped Hf-based NASICON Na3.2Hf1.9Ca0.1Si2PO12 showed an ionic conductivity of 1.07 mS cm−1 with an ESW of 8.80 V.211Some of the doping ions, such as Mg2+, La3+, Nb5+ and F−, are proved to effectively decorate the grain boundaries in NASICON. For example, in addition to the enlarged bottlenecks and increased sodium concentration induced by Mg2+ doping, the enhanced ionic conductivity of Mg2+-doped NASICON is also attributed to the conducting secondary phase Na3PO4 formed at the grain boundaries to mitigate the grain boundary resistance.284 Likewise, doping La3+ into NZSPO has the same effect at grain boundaries with the formation of Na3La(PO4)2, La2O3 and LaPO4, which facilitate the ion conduction at grain boundaries and increase the density of ceramics, while Na3La(PO4)2 further raises the concentration of mobile Na+.434 As a result of both improved bulk and grain boundary conductivity, Na3.3Zr1.7La0.3Si2PO12 shows a preeminent total ionic conductivity of 6.7 mS cm−1 at room temperature.458 With the addition of NaF into the precursor, a “binder-like” Na–Si–P–O–F glassy phase forms in situ around the grains that modifies the grain boundaries and improves ionic conductivity.473 Meanwhile, F− doping facilitates the grain growth in NZSPO to minimize the grain boundaries for a dense microstructure, in that the NaF-assisted synthesized NZSPO exhibited a total ionic conductivity of 1.41–3.6 mS cm−1 at ambient temperature.473,474 Interestingly, as Al3+ tends to lower the bulk ionic conductivity of NZSPO due to the size effect, it can increase the relative density of the ceramic and contribute to the total ionic conductivity. Another high ionic conductivity achieved by the doping strategy of NZSPO is 5.51 mS cm−1 for Nb5+-doped Na3.3Zr1.9Nb0.1Si2.4P0.6O12, which increased both the bulk and grain boundary conductivity through alteration of sodium concentration as well as the local structure, while benefiting from reduced intergranular impedance due to increased ceramic density and depressed formation of impurities.467 A summary of the ionic conductivity and activation energy of the modified NASICON-type NZSPO is presented in Fig. 15, achieved by adopting different elemental doping strategies.
 Fig. 15  Summary of the ionic conductivity and activation energy of NASICON (NZSPO) doped with different elements. The shaded region is the corresponding activation energy for comparison. 
Optimizing the preparation conditions. Beyond ion doping strategies, optimizing the synthesis and processing conditions is essential to the enhancement of ionic conductivity in most of the oxide-based ISEs, and NASICON is no exception. Akin to BASE in Section 4.1, tuning the preparation conditions mostly influences the microstructure and density of NZSPO ceramics, hence alleviating grain boundary impedance and increasing total ionic conductivity.475 As a general principle for oxide-based fast ion conductors, the ionic conductivity is favored by high purity, high densification, and homogeneous grain size. Sintering the oxides at higher temperature and for longer time periods often results in the higher density of the ceramics and the larger grain sizes, respectively, which seem to benefit fast ion conduction.459,476 However, the high sintering temperature can cause volatilization of Na and P content, as well as a change in Si/P arrangement, which disturbs the formation of the phase-pure product. Meanwhile, some glassy and secondary phases with lower ionic conductivity may emerge in this process, such as ZrO2, Na2CO3 and NaOH, and they can greatly hinder Na+ ion transport.477 Although this problem can usually be solved by adding excessive amounts of sodium and phosphorus in precursors before high-temperature processing,451,478 it is still difficult to empirically determine the precise amount to be added and unexpected off-stoichiometry can exist in the final products. Some additives have been used in the synthesis of NZSPO to reduce the sintering temperature while maintaining the relative density of ceramics, such as Na3BO3 through liquid phase sintering (700 °C, 0.1 mS cm−1),479 Na2O–Nb2O5–P2O5 glass through SPS (1000 °C, 1 mS cm−1),480 antimony-tin oxide (ATO) through solid-state reaction (1100 °C, 1.43 mS cm−1),481 and Na2SiO3 through liquid phase sintering (1100 °C, 1.28 mS cm−1 and 1175 °C, 1.45 mS cm−1).482,483 A few other research studies are dedicated to optimizing the processing conditions. For instance, Chong et al. investigated the influence of ball-milling duration on the microstructure and conductivity properties of NASICON synthesized by the solid-state method, and found that varied milling durations formed different types of ZrO2 and caused different ionic conductivity.484 Niazmand et al. prepared NZSPO with a high ionic conductivity of 1.7 mS cm−1 by adopting the sol-gel method and SPS technique, which was improved compared to that from conventional solid-state sintering because of lower sintering temperature, shorter time, higher density and nano-particle size.485
High entropy alloying. To overcome the limited options for charge-compensating dopants and the complicated synthesis process, high-entropy metal cation mixing was recently demonstrated for effectively enhancing the room-temperature ionic conductivity of NASICON, via introducing local distortion and overlapping distribution of Na+ site energies, thus allowing Na+ ion migration with lower activation energy. According to theoretical calculations and experimental results, the high-entropy NASICON (Na(Ti,Zr,Sn,Hf)2(PO4)3) provided enhanced ionic conductivity in combination with the strategy of introducing excess Na+.486

4.2.4. Compatibility with the Na metal anode. As the thermodynamic stability is found to vary with the composition, NASICON-structured SEs generally have high oxidation potentials but become thermodynamically unstable at low potentials according to the theoretical calculations,242,396 as shown in Fig. 16a. For example, the ESW of stoichiometric NZSPO, Na3Zr2Si2PO12, was predicted to be 1.11–3.41 V vs. Na+/Na, while the slightly off-stoichiometric compositions exhibited shifted ESWs within this range.487 Despite the thermodynamic instability of NZSPO against metallic Na, the kinetics of the reduction reaction of NZSPO is relatively slow and an electronically insulating SEI layer forms at a rather small decomposition energy (−0.27 eV per atom) (Fig. 16b, upper panel). The large portion of electronic insulators, including Na4SiO4, Na2ZrO3, Na3P and ZrSi, in this SEI layer yields surface passivation and prevents further decomposition of bulk materials. This implies that NZSPO SEs can be kinetically stable against the metallic Na anode in practice, instead of being thermodynamically stable. Interestingly, the driving force for decomposition of NZSPO against metal Na tends to decrease as the Si/P ratio increases.487,488 Likewise, the oxidation overpotential of NZSPO can readily extend its stability above 5 V due to the formation of the insulating layer and the sluggish oxidation reaction kinetics, which is made possible by using a wide range of cathodes, including NVP,434,457,473,489–494 sulfur,454,495 NaTi2(PO4)3,238 Na2MnFe(CN)6,199 Na0.9Cu0.22Fe0.3Mn0.48O2,496 and NaxCoO2.497,498
 Fig. 16  (a) The thermodynamic stability of Na3Zr2Si2PO12 and the decomposed products at different potentials and sodium content, reprinted with permission from ref. 487. (b) Reaction energy of Na3Zr2Si2PO12 (upper panel) and volume change (bottom panel) with different cathode and anode materials, adapted with permission from ref. 242. (c) Optical imaging of the preferential growth of sodium metal dendrites along grain boundaries in the NASICON SE pellet, inducing dendrite penetration and ceramic cracking. 
Unfortunately, the pristine NASICON oxides are still considered incompatible with the metallic Na anode at room temperature due to the large interfacial resistance of the solid–solid interface between them, as well as the incapability of the rigid interface to buffer the significant volume change during cycling (Fig. 16b, bottom panel). The CCD of Na/pristine NZSPO/Na symmetric cells is usually reported in the range of 0.07–0.2 mA cm−2, without interfacial modification.215,487,499 The rigid nature of oxide ceramics may be insufficient to buffer the volume change of the metal anode during the sodiation–desodiation process, in that Noguchi et al. fabricated an all-solid-state battery NVP/Na3Zr2Si2PO12/Na exhibiting the rapid decay of cell capacity and loss of cycle life, with specific capacity declining from 80 mA h g−1 to 53 mA h g−1 within 50 cycles at 80 °C.491,492 Most recently, the interfacial stability of Na3.4Zr2Si2.4P0.6O12 with Na metal was investigated in detail by Janek et al., wherein several contribution factors to interfacial resistance were properly interpreted, i.e., charge transfer process, SEI formation, and current constriction (dynamic and static).500 Based on the in situ characterization and monitoring of the temporal evolution of the interface, it was found that the kinetically stabilized and inert SEI layer exhibited rather low charge transfer resistance, indicating that the large interfacial impedance attributes to current constriction from the insufficient physical contact at the interface, as well as the formation of the interfacial pore structure during anodic dissolution. Meanwhile, such a poor interfacial contact between NASICON and the sodium metal anode induces a non-uniform Na+ flux across the interface and uneven sodium plating that promotes growth of sodium dendrites. Regardless of the high shear modulus of NASICON, the sodium dendrites can grow rapidly along the grain boundaries where the electric field is enhanced locally during charging, and eventually induce bulk cracks and penetrate the SEs, which has been confirmed in our experimental demonstration (Fig. 16c).214,237,238
In practical application, NZSPO should be carefully handled in ambient air because it easily reacts with H2O and CO2, to form byproducts such as Na2CO3 and NaOH on the sample surface, causing significant interfacial resistance and poor wetting between NZSPO and electrodes, though this can be restored by a high-temperature treatment.501,502 Hence, different strategies have been developed to engineer the interfaces between NASICON and the Na metal anode, focusing on (1) enabling intimate contact of the electrolyte/anode interface and (2) protecting NASICON ceramics from dendrite penetration through grain boundaries or internal defects.

4.2.5. Modification strategies for a stable NASICON/Na metal interface. Before surface-treating the electrolyte/anode interface, the stability of NASICON against the metal Na anode can be improved by doping. For example, Zn-doped Na3.4Zr1.9Zn0.1Si2.2P0.8O12,468 Pr3+-doped Na3.3Zr1.7Pr0.3Si2PO12,503 and La-doped Na3.3Zr1.7La0.3Si2PO12434 demonstrated significantly improved CCD of 0.5, 0.9, and 1.1 mA cm−2, respectively, in Na symmetric cells at room temperature. On the other hand, interfacial modification between NASICON and the metallic Na anode can be achieved by addition of liquid electrolytes,454,495 in situ formation of the interlayer,238,490,504,505 introduction of a sandwich structure,238,457,506 use of a composite anode215 or a 3D Na metal anode,457 chemical plating,457,490,499 application of higher uniaxial compression,216 and ultrasound solid welding.507 By adopting diverse methods for interfacial modification, some all-solid-state or quasi-solid-state battery systems have been successfully fabricated. A Na–S battery was fabricated using the sulfur/carbon nanofiber (CNF) composite cathode, the Na3Zr2Si2PO12 solid electrolyte coated with a layer of polymer with intrinsic nanoporosity (PIN) on the anode side, and the sodium metal anode, and the full cell overcame the polysulfide-shuttle problem and provided a discharge capacity of 700 mA h g−1 at C/5 with 550 mA h g−1 maintained after 100 cycles.495 With a coating layer of AlF3 decorating the interface between Na3Zr2Si2PO12 and Na metal, NVP/Na3Zr2Si2PO12-AlF3/Na delivered an initial discharge capacity of 111.0 mA h g−1 at 1C, retaining a superior reversible capacity of 89.6 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles.499 By modifying the Na3.2Hf1.9Ca0.1Si2PO12 electrolyte pellet with SnO2 at the Na metal interface, a mixed-ion-electron layer of Na2O/NaxSn was in situ formed to ensure the intimate interface contact and regulated Na+ stripping/plating, and NVP/Na3.2Hf1.9Ca0.1Si2PO12–SnO2/Na maintained a capacity of 103.1 mA h g−1 at 0.5C for over 300 cycles with CCD up to 2.0 mA cm−2 without an obvious short circuit.211 Lu et al. developed a robust monolithic SSB with high rate capability and excellent cyclability, where the Na metal anode was integrated into the porous layer of the Ca2+-doped NASICON Na3.4Zr1.8Ca0.2Si2PO12 (Ca-NZSPO) SE with a sodiophilic surface created by impregnating with SnO2 particles, forming a monolithic electrolyte–anode architecture. The full cell exhibited specific capacities of 80.5 mA h g−1 at 4C and 96.7 mA h g−1 at 1C with retention of 94.9 mA h g−1 after 450 cycles at room temperature.457 By directly growing a graphene-like thin layer on the Na3Zr2Si2PO12 ceramic electrolyte, a uniform Na+ flux across the NASICON/Na interface was promised by the graphene defect network, leading to a reduced interfacial impedance by ten-fold and inhibited pervasion of sodium dendrites into grain boundaries. The full cell battery with the NVP cathode and the Na metal anode demonstrated a high reversible initial capacity of 108 mA h g−1 at 1C with 85% of the capacity retained after 300 cycles, while maintaining a CE of almost 100%.490 Most recently, Ma et al. proposed that the formation of sodium dendrites on the NZSPO surface can be prevented simply by blocking the atmosphere with a protective coating of sodium salts, such as NaNO3 or NaCl, where the CCD of the symmetric Na/NZSPO/Na cell reaches 14 mA cm−2.508
5. Chalcogenide-based ISEs
Chalcogenide-based SEs contain at least one or more chalcogens from group 16 elements, such as S and Se (but excluding O), that establish covalent bonding with the atoms from group 14 or 15 elements, e.g., P, Sb, Sn, etc. The formed polyanionic polyhedra, e.g., (PS4)3−, (SbS4)3−, and (SnS4)3−, build the skeletal framework of the ion conductors, with mobile Na+ residing at the interstices.509 The Na+ diffusion in this chalcogenide-based conducting framework is intrinsically faster than that in oxide-based ones, realized by the enlarged bottlenecks and migration channels in the Na+ transport pathways owing to the smaller ionic radius of S2 compared to that of O2−, as well as the weaker electrostatic force between the sulfide framework and Na+ ions because of the lower electronegativity of S2−. Moreover, chalcogenide SEs are known for their simple and low-temperature manufacturing requirements, along with their great deformability for both lower grain boundary resistance and more intimate contact with electrodes, thus lower interfacial resistance. However, it was found that mere cold pressing of chalcogenide SEs still leaves voids in their compact components, detrimental to their electrochemical properties.510 On the other hand, chalcogenide SEs are considered extremely unstable in air as they undergo hydrolysis on exposure to moisture which releases toxic gas H2S and results in a sharp drop of ionic conductivity. Based on preliminary cyclic voltammetry (CV), chalcogenide SEs generally exhibit a wide ESW ≥ 5 V,226 but such values may be overestimated because the conventional CV techniques fail to detect very small decomposition currents.209,239,303,305 The “true” ESW of chalcogenide-based SEs was found to be rather narrow, indicating the inevitable occurrence of serious side reactions and unstable SEI layers at the electrolyte/Na anode interfaces.240,241,511 Chalcogenide SEs can manifest glassy or glass-ceramics characteristics, in which the former exhibits an amorphous phase with the lack of long-range order and contains concentrated mobile Na+ within the sublattice, while the latter is crystallized from the glasses and exhibits both crystal-structure framework and glassy grain boundary, thus rendering lower ionic conductivity than its glassy precursors but meanwhile lower grain boundary resistance than the polycrystalline materials.225,512 Accordingly, ceramization and vitrification can usually be applied for tuning the ionic conductivity and electrolyte stability under harsh operation conditions, as well as for simplified battery packaging.513–516
5.1. Na3PS4-type
5.1.1. Ion conduction in Na3PS4. The most signature composition in chalcogenide-based SEs is the Na3PS4-type, with the general composition of Na3MX4 (M = P, Sb; X = S, Se), along with its isostructural compounds such as Na3PSe4, Na3SbS4, and Na3SbSe4. Na3PS4 exists in three crystal structures including the room-temperature tetragonal α-phase (P21c, a = 6.9520 Å, c = 7.0757 Å), the high-temperature cubic β-phase (I3m, a = 6.9965 Å), or the orthorhombic γ-phase (Fddd, a = 6.6055 Å, b = 11.7143 Å, and c = 20.7378 Å) which was recently found at even higher temperature.281 Inspired by the Li2S–P2S5 system, tetragonal-phase Na3PS4 (α-Na3PS4 or t-Na3PS4) was initially synthesized showing an inferior ionic conductivity of the order of magnitude of 10−6 S cm−1 at 50 °C.517 Later in 2012, the high-temperature cubic-phase Na3PS4 (β-Na3PS4 or c-Na3PS4) was crystallized from the glassy state and stabilized at room temperature, exhibiting a higher ionic conductivity of 2 × 10−4 S cm−1.301 Its room-temperature ionic conductivity was further enhanced to 4.6 × 10−4 S cm−1 using high-purity 99.1% crystalline Na2S as the precursor and the simple ball-milling process as the synthesis method.518 The cubic phase of β-Na3PS4 consists of (PS4)3− tetrahedra in a body-centered cubic (bcc) arrangement, where two types of Na+ sites are embodied within the lattice, namely Na(6b) and Na(12d), with the former locating at the face center and midpoint of the lattice edge of the unit cell, and the latter locating at the midpoint of two adjacent Na(6b) sites, as shown in Fig. 17a.513 While Na(6b) sites are predominantly occupied by Na+ ions (occ. ≈ 0.8), Na(12d) sites (occ. ≈ 0.1) are considered as metastable transition sites acting as interstices for Na+ pass-through. Through the interconnection of each Na(6b) to other four co-planar Na(6b) via intermediate Na(12d), the 3D diffusion channels are constructed for the inter-site hopping of Na+ in directions parallel to the lattice edges.517,519 The cubic-to-tetragonal phase transition occurs as the temperature drops below ca. 530 K, reflected by the converged lattice parameters, the slightly redistributed orientation of (PS4)3− polyhedra, and the minorly displaced Na+ ions.517,519 During the process of tetragonal distortion of the lattice, Na(6b) splits into Na(2a) and Na(4d), and the latter is shifted 0.5Å away from its original position in the cubic phase, making up three types of Na+ sites in α-Na3PS4, namely Na(2a), Na(4d) and Na(12d); however this has a negligible influence on Na+ ion distribution (Fig. 17b).520 In the temperature range of the α-to-β phase transition, the lattice volume of Na3PS4 changes linearly with the temperature. However, such a continuity of lattice volume–temperature relationship is broken by the β-to-γ phase transition, in that a sharp increase of the unit cell volume (≈10%) manifests at temperature above 773 K and the local structure of the γ-phase becomes “liquidized” with expanded vibrational freedom for the (PS4)3− polyanions.521 The volume expansion and “melting” of the Na+ sublattice destabilize the bcc-like packing of anionic units in the original α- or β-phase, which consists of a more complex arrangement of (PS4)3− tetrahedra and a lot of extra interstitial space for Na+ ions around these tetrahedra, resulting in a highly disordered arrangement of Na+ ions among multiple sites within the γ-Na3PS4 in lower symmetry, as shown in Fig. 17c.521,522
 Fig. 17  Crystal structure of stoichiometric Na3PS4 in (a) cubic phase (β-Na3PS4) and (b) tetragonal phase Na3PS4 (α-Na3PS4). (c) Calculated structure of the orthorhombic phase (γ-Na3PS4) with relative Na+ site occupation marked, reprinted with permission from ref. 281. 
Defect concentration determined ion diffusion. In α- and β-Na3PS4, there is only a minor difference in the lattice parameters (1.1%) and lattice volume (0.2%) between the cubic and tetragonal structures, where these two phases are easily interchangeable via element doping, and both can be expressed by the structural motifs of the tetragonal polymorph on a small length scale.521,523,524 Therefore, the huge disparity in the ionic conductivity of α-Na3PS4 and β-Na3PS4 is unlikely to be caused by crystal structures as initially assumed,301 but probably by the concentration of defects within the crystal lattice, especially the Na+ vacancies as their formation energy is substantially lower than the others.520,525 Highly conductive α-Na3PS4 with a tetragonal structure has been synthesized by adopting ball-milling methods in a similar way as the cubic-phase compounds prepared by Hayashi et al,518 showing ionic conductivity up to 3.39 × 10−3 S cm−1 at room temperature.526 Herein, it highlights the effects of the mechanochemical synthesis routes, which not only introduce extra mobile Na+ defects into the crystal lattice of chalcogenide-based SEs, but also develop more homogeneous product powders with decreased particle sizes from the repeated impacts during ball-milling, favoring the following consolidation process for highly conductive pellets.519 Such an approach to reinforce the mobile ion conduction of SEs is particularly effective for mechanically soft materials, in that increased ionic conductivity has also been observed in mechanochemically synthesized Li3PS4,527 LiBH4,528 Ag3PS4,529 Na11Sn2PSe12,530 and Li3YCl6.531Regarding such a fact, the fast Na+ conduction within the Na3PS4 crystal structure is only permitted in the presence of point defects, in that inferior Na+ ion diffusivity is predicted in both pristine α- and β-Na3PS4 with the lack of point defects.520,532,533 According to molecular dynamics (MD) simulations, an introduction of 2% vacancies into the stoichiometric structure (Na2.94PS4) increases the Na(6b)–Na(6b) ion jump rate by a factor of 17, indicating significantly more frequent inter-site ion transitions and higher Na+ mobility (Fig. 18a), as well as the reduction of 0.12 eV on activation energies for the Na(6b)–Na(6b) transition, corresponding to a bulk ionic conductivity of 0.2 S cm−1 for the cubic phase and 0.14 S cm−1 for the tetragonal phase.520 When the vacancy concentration reaches 17% (Na2.50PS4), the bulk ionic conductivity of cubic and tetragonal phases is further increased to 0.42 S cm−1 and 0.43 S cm−1, respectively. In addition to Na+ vacancies, the existence of Na+ interstitials and Na+ disorders also contribute to the fast ion conduction of Na3PS4. For example, the adoption of the Na2S precursor with high purity yields improved ionic conductivity of β-Na3PS4 by virtue of the reduced likelihood of sodium deficiency and increased possibility of Na+ interstitial formation.518 In contrast, stoichiometric β-Na3PS4 shows poor ionic conductivity and relatively low occupancy of the Na(12d) transition sites, suggesting inactive Na+ transport. When the Na+ interstitials are introduced into the crystal lattice, the enhancements of both Na(12d) occupancy and Na+ conductivity can be observed experimentally. In practice, the introduction of Na+ interstitials is likely to cause a slight displacement of Na+ from their normal Na(6b) and Na(12d) sites because of the strengthened Coulombic repulsion between them, and this redistribution of Na+ creates a flat energy landscape triggering the correlated motion of Na+ with lower activation barriers and increased conductivity.534
 Fig. 18  (a) The Na+ distribution in pristine Na3PS4 (upper panel) and 2% vacancy added Na2.94PS4, showing drastically increased Na+ mobility, acquired from 100 ps. MD simulation at 525 K, adapted with permission from ref. 520. (b) Plot of ionic conductivity with temperature variation in Na3PS4, with a dramatic jump of ionic conductivity and reduction of activation energy corresponding to plastic transition from β to γ phase, adapted with permission from ref. 281. (c) Eigenvectors of two soft modes with different vibrational symmetry that couples Na+ transition and tilting of tetrahedra, computed by DFT calculations, adapted with permission from ref. 535. 
Lattice dynamics facilitated ion diffusion. The ionic conductivity of Na3PS4 is also observed to be positively correlated to temperature, where the unit cell lattice expansion creates extra free volumes around the mobile Na+ at elevated temperature. This trend is rather linear until the temperature is raised up to 773 K where a new γ phase forms and another ion conduction mechanism is involved.281 Akin to the amorphous or liquid systems, the new polymorph γ-Na3PS4 exhibits partially plastic crystal features as a result of the increased stiffness of the covalent intramolecular bonds in (PS4)3− tetrahedra and reduced ionic interaction between the (PS4)3− units and Na+ during the β-to-γ phase transition. Thereby, the “molten” Na+ sublattice upsets the bcc-like packing of anionic units as in the α- and β-phase to a lower-symmetry anionic arrangement, and the reorientational motion of (PS4)3− tetrahedra promotes the cationic diffusion, leading to a fast ion conduction about ten-fold higher than that in β-Na3PS4 (Fig. 18b).521,522 In fact, the lattice dynamics also manifest in α- and β-Na3PS4 and they are important structural factors for the promotion of Na+.269,535 Herein, a special anharmonic motion coupling mobile Na+ and the host lattice features soft mode motion, as illustrated by Fig. 18c. Instead of functioning in the manner of the paddle-wheel mechanism, this soft mode lattice dynamics facilitates the Na+ hopping through the dynamically altered bottlenecks.
Grain boundary effects. Although the blocking effect of grain boundaries in chalcogenide SEs may be considered minimal compared to that in oxide SEs due to the soft nature of chalcogenides and preferred intergranular ion conduction in Na3PS4,291,510 the influence of grain boundaries still cannot be completely neglected. The segregation of point defects, e.g., Na+ vacancies, at the vicinity of grain boundaries hampers the continuous ion conduction, especially when the ion conduction within Na3PS4 is dominantly mediated through defects. On the other hand, the grain boundary core may act as a trapping site for Na+ due to the change of the atomic environment around Na+ hopping in this region. As a result, the experimentally measured ionic conductivity of Na3PS4 is usually lower than the theoretical values by two orders of magnitude due to the grain boundary and interfacial resistance. Both increased fraction of the amorphous phase and enlarged grain sizes are proved to improve the total ionic conductivity of Na3PS4.532,536 Meanwhile, grain boundary engineering is regarded as beneficial for further enhancement of ion conductivity within Na3PS4, such as tuning the symmetric tilting angle of grain boundaries and doping elements to adjust energy variation in the grain boundary core.295,537

5.1.2. Modification strategies for fast ion conduction. 
Aliovalent doping for concentrated defects. Aliovalent doping is a direct approach to regulate defects in the crystalline solids, thereby promoting defect-mediated ion conduction in Na3PS4-type SEs. Based on the charge compensation mechanism, the introduction of vacancies in Na3PS4 is mostly accomplished by doping either higher-valent cations into the Na+ sites, e.g., Ca2+, or lower-valent anions into the S2− sites, e.g., Cl−. When doped Ca2+ replaces the Na(2a) site in cubic Na3PS4, the inter-site Na+ hopping is accelerated by the generated vacancies and the migration energy barrier is lowered from 0.21 eV to 0.19 eV, compared to the undoped phases.538 Moon et al. found that the experimentally prepared Na2.73Ca0.135PS4 exhibited a tetragonal-to-cubic phase transition upon doping a small amount of Ca2+, achieving a maximum ionic conductivity of 0.94 mS cm−1 at room temperature.539 Interestingly, the activation energy was observed to increase with the concentration of doped Ca2+, because the more positively charged Ca2+ can easily trap the Na+ vacancies nearby, leading to a high migration barrier. It implies that the enhancement of VNa concentration is so impactful for the ion conduction in Na3PS4 that it overcomes the impediment of the high energy barrier. The substitution of sulfide ions by halide ions has already been commonly utilized to introduce vacancies and improve ion conduction in lithium sulfide materials,540 and this strategy is also effective in Na3PS4. The effect of a series of halogen dopants (F, Cl, Br, I) has been investigated by Klerk et al. through ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations.520 The ionic conductivity of doped-Na3PS4 was found to increase with the increase of the ionic radii of halogen dopants going from F− to Cl− to Br−, except for the oversized I− for which its large ionic radius results in a long I−–P distance and a smaller bottleneck area. Among these halogen dopants, Cl− is considered the most preferable due to its closest ionic radius to S2− and avoidance of HF exposure in F− doping, as well as the preferred dopant formation energy, in that Na2.9375PS3.9375Cl0.0625 exhibited an ionic conductivity exceeding 1 mS cm−1 at room temperature,541 assisted by the spark plasma sintering technique that largely eliminated the grain boundary resistance and interparticle resistance.542,543 In later research, the maximum ionic conductivity of Cl− and Br−-doped Na3PS4 was determined to be 1.96 mS cm−1 and 2.37 mS cm−1 at room temperature, respectively.544,545 On the other hand, Na+ interstitials can be created by doping lower-valent cations into the P5+ sites, mostly tetravalent ions, including Ti4+, Si4+, Sn4+, and Ge4+. The effect of partial substitution of Si4+ for P5+ in beta-Na3PS4 was investigated by preparing a series of Na3PS4–Na4SiS4 glass ceramics, among which the 94Na3PS4–6Na4SiS4 glass ceramic SE exhibited the highest room-temperature ionic conductivity of 0.74 mS cm−1 and 90Na3PS4–10Na4SiS4 showed the widest ESW of 5 V as well as thermodynamic stability with metallic Na.513,546 Other tetravalent dopants, such as Ge4+,547 Ti4+, and Sn4+, can not only increase the concentration of Na+ interstitials, but also enlarge the lattice volume and broaden the Na+ diffusion channels, resulting in a lower effective migration barrier for interstitialcy-like motion at the cost of phase stability.548 The ionic conductivity of 6.25% Sn-doped β-Na3PS4 was predicted to be 10.7 mS cm−1 at room temperature according to AIMD simulations,534 while the experientially obtained 10% Sn-doped sample achieved the highest ionic conductivity of 0.25 mS cm−1 among the others, as well as superior full-cell SSB performance.548
Isovalent substitution for structure tuning. Isovalent substitution, mostly accomplished by replacing P or S with larger atoms, is another strategy to enhance the Na+ mobility in Na3PS4-type SEs, which may even benefit other electrochemical or chemical characteristics, such as ESW and air stability. Some of the representative compositions have shown outstanding outcomes, including Na3SbS4, Na3PSe4, and Na3SbSe4. Instead of changing the concentration of effective charge carriers, the substituted ions can manage the chemical and spatial environment of the skeletal structure, such as enlarging the crystal volume, widening the bottleneck area, and softening the anion framework. In Na3PS4, the average bond length of the Na–S at transition state is approximately 2.65 Å, significantly shorter than that of stable lattice sites (∼2.86 Å), which implies the necessity to expand the bottleneck to allow Na+ to hop through.519 For example, by replacing S2− with Se2−, the lattice structure was expanded to create extra free volume for Na+ transport because of the larger ionic radius of Se2− than S2−, while an uncommon electron-density distribution on Na(12d) was observed, which might be attributed to the insertion of interstitial Na+. As a result, the obtained cubic Na3PSe4 exhibited a higher room-temperature Na+ conductivity of 1.16 mS cm−1 with a reduced activation energy of 0.21 eV.549 Meanwhile, the more polarizable Se2− reduces the binding energy between the mobile Na+ and stationary anions in the skeleton, so that the mobile Na+ can move more easily within the softened framework.195,550 Likewise, substituting P5+ with Sb5+ or As5+ is also effective due to the lattice expansion and the weakened Sb–S or As–S bond strength. In addition, they are proven to improve the air stability of Na3PS4 (will be illustrated in Section 5.1.4). As a result, Na3SbS4 with tetragonal crystal structure (P21c; a = 7.15270 Å, c = 7.2874 Å) shows a remarkable ionic conductivity of 1.05 mS cm−1 and a low activation energy of 0.22 eV, as well as excellent air stability.551 Remarkably, these signature compositions obtained through isovalent substitution of Na3PS4 provide a cornerstone for the following aliovalent doping on top of them. By partially replacing Sb with Ge, Na3.1Ge0.1Sb0.9S4 exhibits a further improved ionic conductivity of 5.1 mS cm−1 at 25 °C and an activation energy of 0.156 eV, as well as enhanced air stability.552 The single-phase Na3SbSe4 polycrystalline compound was obtained by simultaneously substituting Sb5+ and Se2−, yielding an exceptionally high ionic conductivity of 3.7 mS cm−1 and a low activation energy of 0.19 eV.553 The best result came from Hayashi et al. by doping W6+ into Na3SbS4, where Na2.88Sb0.88W0.12S4 exhibited a superb ionic conductivity of 32 mS cm−1 at room temperature.554 Soon after, a series of W-doped solid solutions of Na3−xPn1−xWxS4 (Pn = P, Sb) were prepared by Fuchs et al. to systematically investigate their underlying structure–activity relationship.555 The incorporation of W6+ leads to the formation of vacancies, where the increasing W6+ content decreases the activation barriers for ionic transport by lowering the defect formation enthalpies. Meanwhile, the W6+ substitution reduces the tetragonal distortion and lowers the phase transition temperature, resulting in a structural change from the tetragonal to the cubic phase at room temperature. Correspondingly, the acquired Na2.9P0.9W0.1S4 and Na2.9Sb0.9W0.1S4 exhibited a superior ionic conductivity of 13 ± 3 mS cm−1 and 41 ± 8 mS cm−1, respectively, exceeding some of the commercialized OLEs. Surprisingly, the exceptional ionic conductivity of Na3−xSb1−xWxS4 (x = 0.125) does not involve the commonly believed paddlewheel effect (explained in Section 6) coupled to the rotational dynamics of complex anions (e.g., PS4),256,273,556–560 but merely the conventional vacancy diffusion mechanism accompanied by the strong overlapping from cation vibrational modes with anion vibrations.561

5.1.3. Interfacial stability and interface design in SMBs. Notwithstanding the facile processing and remarkable ionic conductivity, Na3PS4-type SEs are intrinsically incompatible with high-voltage cathode materials and the Na metal anode due to their high chemical reactivity and thermodynamic instability. Wenzel et al. investigated the interfacial stability of pristine Na3PS4 against the Na metal anode by performing in situ XPS and monitoring the interfacial impedance and polarization resistance through time, which revealed the thermodynamically unstable nature of the Na3PS4/Na metal anode interface.240 The formation of electronically insulating Na2S and Na3P at the interface strongly hinders the interfacial transfer kinetics, increasing the areal resistance of the SEI, deteriorating the cell performance. In fact, the commonly believed ESW of Na3PS4, as wide as 5 V determined by conventional CV analysis, is significantly overestimated,301 since the results are limited by the insensitivity of the analysis to detect slow decomposition reactions in full cells due to the very small currents generated. By combining computational and experimental tools, Na3PS4 was determined to be only stable from 1.55 V to 2.25 V vs. Na+/Na, and Na3PSe4 showed an even narrower ESW of 1.8–2.15 V vs. Na+/Na.239 As most of the intercalation-type cathodes (NaFeO4,562 NVP,434 NaCrO2,563 and Na2/3Co2/3Mn1/3O2564) have working voltages readily exceeding the decomposition potential of Na3PS4-type SEs, low-voltage conversion-type cathode materials are usually utilized, such as TiS2,565,566 FeS2,567 S,568 and Na2S,569 as well as some organic cathode materials,570,571 though the SSBs can still suffer from the large volume expansion of conversion-type cathodes and thereby show unsatisfactory cycling stability.Meanwhile, the practical application of sulfide-based SEs is hindered by their instability in the ambient environment with high sensitivity to water, due to which they can be hydrolyzed by moisture releasing toxic H2S gas,297,298 significantly deteriorating ionic conductivity and causing safety issues.510 The pristine phase of Na3PS4 is considered unstable in air because of the high affinity between S and O, which can be mitigated by replacing the “hard acid” P5+ with “soft acids”, e.g., Sb5+, As5+, and Sn4+. On the basis of the hard and soft acids and bases (HSAB), the soft acids preferentially react with the soft base S2− than the hard acid P5+, and the formation of stronger bonds between soft acids and soft base can partially compensate the S–O affinity.572 For example, the abovementioned Na3SbS4 manifested improved air stability compared to Na3PS4.551 By partially replacing P5+ with As5+, Na3P0.62As0.38S4 achieved an ionic conductivity of 1.46 mS cm−1, in addition to the improved moisture stability by shifting easy-forming reaction products to difficult-forming ones.573,574 Air stable Na4−xSn1−xSbxS4 (0.02 ≤ x ≤ 0.33) shows ionic conductivity up to 0.51 mS cm−1 at 30 °C, as well as excellent air stability and solution probability using water.575 Without the release of toxic H2S gases in contact with water, Na4−xSn1−xSbxS4 can reversibly precipitate from homogeneously dissolved aqueous solution, rendering the possibility of intimate contacts by SE coating on active materials and fabrication of thin-film electrodes by the infiltration method.
While alloy anodes are always chosen as anode materials owing to the instability of Na3PS4-type SEs against metallic Na,580 e.g., Na–Sn alloys,565,573,575,580–583 some efforts have been made to ameliorate the unstable interface between them, with common strategies summarized in Fig. 19. The typical approaches include doping elements (Cl− and Ca2+) to stabilize the interface,541,544,576 designing composite electrolytes,577,584,585 surface coating or interlayer insertion,566,578,579,586 and hydration-induced surface passivation.306 For example, by homogeneously coating the PEO layer on the surface of Na3PS4, its immediate contact metallic Na anode was artificially avoided, thus alleviating the unpleasant interfacial reactions between them. Using this strategy, the cycling stability of the solid-state SMB SnS2|Na3PS4@PEO|Na was largely improved compared to the pristine ones, delivering 230 mA h g−1 after 40 cycles.578 The Cl-doped Na3SbS4 was prepared using mechanochemical processes followed by heat treatment, and Na2.9375SbS3.9375Cl0.0625 displayed room-temperature ionic conductivity up to 2.9 mS cm−1, and the introduction of NaCl enhanced its electrochemical stability by suppressing the interfacial deterioration that accompanied stripping/plating.587,588 Another composite solid electrolyte was prepared by encapsulating BMPTFSI(IL)-coated Na3SbS4 powders in the vertical channels of the flexible PVDF membrane, which achieved an ultra-high ionic conductivity of 3.18 mS cm−1 at room temperature, and the full-cell battery NVP|PVDF-NSS-0.2IL|Na exhibited an improved cycling stability of 400 cycles at 1C and 50 °C with 80% capacity retention.577
 Fig. 19  Common modification strategies to stabilize the thermodynamically unstable interface between Na3PS4-type SEs and the metallic Na anode. Adapted with permission from ref. 306, 566 and 576–579. 

5.2. Na11Sn2PS12-type
Another iconic type of SE in the chalcogenide system is Na11Sn2PS12, where P and S can be replaced or partially substituted by Sb and Se, respectively. It was firstly inspired by the direct replacement of Na+ in the Li-ion superionic conductor Li10GeP2S12 (LGPS), intending to inherit its exceptionally fast ion conduction feature (σLGPS = 12 mS cm−1), by which Na10GeP2S12 and its analogous compositions indeed exhibited high room-temperature ionic conductivity (σNa10GeP2S12 = 4.7 mS cm−1, σNa10SnP2S12 = 0.94 mS cm−1, and σNa10SiP2S12 = 10.3 mS cm−1) according to first-principles calculations.589–591 However, the ionic conductivity of the experimentally obtained materials was rather disappointing, due to the huge amount of poorly conductive impurity phases existing in the final products.515,591 Therefore, efforts have been made to eliminate these low-conductivity impurities, particularly by increasing the sodium content and tuning the Sn/P ratio. In 2018, impurity-free Na11Sn2PS12 was successfully synthesized, exhibiting high ionic conductivity at room temperature, i.e., 1.4 mS cm−1 via cold-pressing and 3.7 mS cm−1 via annealing.264,592 Such a rapid ion conduction within Na11Sn2PS12 is attributed to the correlative Na+ motion along equipotential ion migration channels connected by equivalent Na+ sites in its unique structure, promoted by the paddle-wheel mechanism.
5.2.1. Unique equipotential pathways and isotropic conduction. The unit cell of Na11Sn2PS12 is indexed in the tetragonal space group of I41/acd:2 (a = 13.6148(3) Å, c = 27.2244(7) Å), as shown in Fig. 20a, in which the ordered arrangement of SnS4 and PS4 tetrahedra constitutes a unique chessboard-like 3D framework.264 In addition to the five already confirmed Na+ sites in the unit cell of Na11Sn2PS12, an additional interstitial site of Na(6) was later identified from its ambiguous existence concealed in a merohedrally twinning crystal;592,593 the six distinct Na+ sites create the Na+ migration channels along the c axis and within the ab planes. As illustrated in Fig. 20b, Na(1) to Na(5) are in the same coordination environment, where each one of them is square-planar coordinated by four other neighboring Na+ sites and octahedrally coordinated by six sulfur ions to construct face-sharing NaS6 octahedra establishing isotropic 3D ion diffusion pathways. The equivalent Na–Na atomic distance (approximately 3.41 Å) and virtually equipotential Na+ sites promote the low energetic inter-site ion hopping through a spacious iso-energetic pathway.263,264 Among these five sites, Na(3), Na(4) and Na(5) have nearly full occupation, i.e., occ. = 0.96, 0.97, and 0.95, respectively, while Na(1) and Na(2) are partially vacant, i.e., occ. = 0.87 and 0.78, respectively.264 The lower Na+ occupation of the latter two results from the stronger repulsion by P5+, compared to that of others by Sn4+, since Na(1) and Na(2) are placed at the nearest neighborhood of PS4 tetrahedra.263 Interestingly, the vacancy-rich Na+ sites (Na(1) and Na(2)) and fully occupied Na+ sites (Na(3), Na(4), and Na(5)) are arranged in an alternating pattern, creating chains of vacancy/full-occupation/vacancy, featuring low-energy ion hopping pathways in three directions. The isotropic conduction of Na+ is achieved with multiple ion transport pathways, including Na(4)–Na(1)–Na(3)–Na(1) in the c-axis direction, Na(4)–Na(2)–Na(4) or Na(3)–Na(2)–Na(3) within the ab plane at z = 0.25, and Na(5)–Na(1)–Na(5) within the ab plane at z = 0.125 (Fig. 20c). Although other possible pathways, e.g., Na(1)–Na(6)–Na(1) within the ab plane and Na(2)–Na(6)–Na(2) along the c axis, may be enabled due to the newly found Na(6) sites, Na(6) is believed to behave differently during ion conduction (Fig. 20d).592,593 Na(6) is loosely bonded to eight sulfur ions forming a quasi-cubic structure, with a much shorter distance of Na(6)–Na(other) than those among Na(1) to Na(5). The Na+ occupation of Na(6) sites has been a divisive issue, while it is closely related to the determination of the Na+ diffusion mechanism. Ramos et al. confirmed the site occupation of Na(6) being 0.22, but this non-negligible ion transport through Na(6) did not seem to govern ionic conductivity. Instead, the interstitial Na(6) site affects the ion conduction in a subtle way by only acting as a “parking” spot for the excess charge-compensating Na+ ions from adjacent Na(1) and Na(2) sites, which affords more vacancies within the primary ion transport pathways, thereby enhancing the fast ion conduction.593 However, Oh et al. suggested that the presence of Na(6) does not participate in the process of ion diffusion at all, based on the apparent vacancy of occ. = 0, observed even at temperature much higher than the operating temperature (700 K),263 which is opposite to the proposition by Duchardt et al. that the Na(6) vacancy is the key to high ionic conductivity.592 Following the discovery of Na11Sn2PS12 (NSPS), a series of Se-based NSPS and Sb-based NSPS were obtained by substituting S and/or P by Se and Sb, respectively. These varied compositions exhibit roughly similar properties yet slightly different characteristics, which will be further explained in the next section.
 Fig. 20  (a) Structure of Na11Sn2PS12 with different Na+ sites. Note that vacancies and Na(6) sites are not shown in the structure scheme for the purpose of visuality and simplicity, reprinted with permission from ref. 264. (b) The 3D isotropic Na+ conducting network built with Na+ sites in a similar coordination environment and atomic distance, reprinted with permission from ref. 592. (c) Na+ migration channels along the c axis and within the ab planes, enabled by interconnected Na+ sites in an alternating pattern of occupied sites and vacant sites, and (d) the additional migration channels allowed by the Na(6) sites, with their coordination environment and bottleneck size shown, reprinted with permission from ref. 593. 

5.2.2. Conduction mechanisms and modification strategies. In vacancy-driven chalcogenide-based ion conductors, such as Na3PS4-type SEs, introducing a very small number of additional vacancies or interstitials has been proved to drastically improve the ionic conductivity. Intuitively, this modification strategy would also be effective in Na11Sn2PS12-type SEs, considering that their fast ion conduction is enabled by the 3D chessboard-like ion migration pathways interspersed with vacancies. However, artificially shifting the stoichiometry or adding vacancies and Na+ interstitials seems unlikely to greatly influence the ionic conductivity of Na11Sn2PS12-type SEs, demonstrated by many failed attempts. Using AIMD simulations, Oh et al. predicted that the ionic conductivity of Na10.875Sn2PS12 and Na11.125Sn2PS12 is similar to that of stoichiometric Na11Sn2PS12.263 The slightly off-stoichiometric Na10.8Sn1.9PS11.8 was experimentally prepared by Yu et al. shortly after the discovery of Na11Sn2PS12, which exhibited an ionic conductivity of 0.67 mS cm−1 at 25 °C.594 In a series of solid solutions of Na11+xSn2P1−xMxS12 with aliovalent substitution of P by Sn or Ge, both reduced activation energy and intragranular ionic conductivity were observed in a trend with decreasing vacancy concentration.595 These attempts to introduce additional vacancies or Na+ interstitials to expedite Na+ conduction within Na11Sn2PS12 ended up with little impact on its ionic conductivity, probably ascribed to the abundant inherent Na+ disorders, as well as the already sufficient amount of charge carriers (vacancies), which made the additional point defects unnecessary.263
Paddle-wheel mechanism. According to AIMD simulations, the coupling between anion dynamics and cation diffusion was directly revealed, in that Na+ conduction within Na11Sn2PS12-type SEs is essentially encouraged by the paddle-wheel mechanism, viz., the facile dynamic rotation of the (PS4)3− and (PSe4)3− polyanions at room temperature.256 The rotational reorientation of the S ligands, which construct the skeletal framework, not only transiently widens the bottleneck area for Na+ migration, but also leads to instantaneous fluctuation of the local potential which lowers the energy barrier for Na+ escaping the shallow potential wells, thus facilitating their conduction.273,558 In contrast, this paddle-wheel phenomenon is rather trivial in Na11Sn2SbS12-based materials, where the Sb-based polyanions, e.g., (SbS4)3−, exhibit much slower or negligible rotation, even at elevated temperature. This is in good accordance with the experimental ionic conductivity and can be explained by the difference between the bonding nature of P–S, P–Se and Sb–S, in which the former two are nonpolar covalent resulting in a softer rotational mode and facile reorientation of polyanions, while the latter is virtually more ionic (polarized covalent) that the significant partial charge difference between the chalcogenide and the central atom in (SbS4)3− latches its rotation. Most recently, the origin of the paddle-wheel mechanism in Na11Sn2PS12-type SEs was further dug. The non-rotating atoms in Na11M2PS12 (M = Sn, Ge, Si, etc.) were deemed to be the driving force, which should have sufficient capacitance to accommodate the fluctuating potential from rotating anions and migrating Na+, by treating the (MS4)4− and (PS4)3− polyanions as a parallel plate capacitor constructed at a micro scale.596 Thereby, less polarizable atoms are preferred to be substituted into the Sn4+ site to trigger the paddle-wheel mechanism.
Isovalent substitution for tuning the lattice. Compared to the aliovalent-doping strategy for increasing the concentration of mobile charge carriers, tuning the lattice size by isovalent substitution seems to facilitate Na+ conduction in Na11Sn2M(S/Se)12 to a certain limited extent. Upon replacing S with Se, Na11.1Sn2.1P0.9Se12 exhibited a room-temperature ionic conductivity of 3 mS cm−1, which was similar to that of pristine Na11Sn2PS12, but a reduced activation energy of 0.3 eV as a result of the softer crystal lattice formed by Se.597 Changed Sn/P site occupation and rotational disorder of both (PSe4)3− and (SnSe4)4− tetrahedra were spotted in the slightly off-stoichiometric sample, causing different local structures and altered ion transport pathways. By substituting Sb into the P site, a series of analogous Na12−xSn3−xSbxS12 were reported, where the phase-pure Na11.25Sn2.25Sb0.75S12 reached the highest ionic conductivity of 0.51 mS cm−1 upon tuning the Sb content to x = 0.75 and increasing the annealing temperature to 550 °C, while higher Sb contents (x = 1.2 and 1.5) induced the formation of highly conductive Na3SbS4 as the secondary phase but a slightly decreased ionic conductivity of 0.3 mS cm−1 for Na10.8Sn1.8Sb1.2S4 (Sb content x = 0.4).575,598 The stoichiometric Na11Sn2SbS12 was reported by Ramos et al. with the ionic conductivity of 0.56 mS cm−1 at room temperature and they believed that such a lower value than that of Na11Sn2PS12 originated from the reduced population of Na(6), which may actually come from the absence of the paddle-wheel mechanism of (SbS4)3−.593,598 The effect of increased Na vacancy was also experimentally explored by introducing more Sb into Na11Sn2SbS12, and the results showed the formation of Na3SbS4 impurities and lower ionic conductivity of the Sb-rich sample, as suggested by Heo et al.575 Intuitively, the larger lattice volume (Na11Sn2SbS12 = 5235.5 Å3 vs. Na11Sn2PS12 = 5046.4 Å3) and the softer framework constituted by lower electronegativity Sb5+ (1.8 vs. P5+ = 2.1) would benefit fast ion conduction; however Sb-substituted samples tend to exhibit lower ionic conductivity. This phenomenon can be explained by the stronger Na+–S2− Coulombic attractions due to the higher electron density on S2− when lower electronegativity Sb5+ substituted P5+, and the lack of the paddle-wheel mechanism of irrotational (SbS4)3− polyhedra.256,593 Substitution of Sn by Ge and Si was predicted to reduce the ionic conductivity to 0.6 mS cm−1 and 0.3 mS cm−1, respectively, mostly due to the reduced crystal volume (5028 Å3 and 4962 Å3 for Ge and Si substitution), as well as the narrowed Na+ diffusion channels.263 By tuning the content of Si substituted into Na4−xSn1−xSbxS4, Jia et al. reported aliovalent substituted Na4−x(Sn0.67Si0.33)1−xPxS4 (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.4) with enhanced sodium conductivity.599 With the crystal volume (= 5073 Å3) similar to that of Na11Sn2PS12, its highest ionic conductivity reached 1.6 mS cm−1 when x = 0.25, due to the change of vacancy population and weaker Coulombic attraction between Na+ and S2− induced by P site doping. Furthermore, halogen doping was developed based on this system, Na3.57(Sn0.67Si0.33)0.67P0.33S3.9X0.1 (X = Cl, Br, I), with more vacancies introduced and the energy barrier lowered because of the high polarizability of halogen atoms, in which the I-doped Na3.57(Sn0.67Si0.33)0.67P0.33S3.9I0.1 delivered the highest ionic conductivity of 1.08 mS cm−1 and the lowest activation energy of 0.24 eV.600 By replacing P with As, the predicted ionic conductivity of Na11Sn2AsS12 can reach 4.52 mS cm−1, along with improved moisture stability based on HSAB theory.601
Creating correlated-ion motion. The abovementioned doping strategies attempting to improve the ion conduction in Na11Sn2PS12, which typically work well with Na3PS4-type SEs, seem to cause negligible effects on its ionic conductivity. In this case, Oh and Kang proposed a novel conceptual strategy of planting large repulsive ions, such as Cs+, in the vicinity of the Na+-rich ion migration pathways in Na11Sn2PS12 to compel the mobile Na+ to cross the locally induced high-energy sites, thereby activating the correlated ion motion for fast ion conduction.602 According to the results of AIMD simulations, the ionic conductivity of Cs0.5Na10.5Sn2PS12 was increased by an order of magnitude to 19 mS cm−1 compared to that of pristine Na11Sn2PS12.

5.2.3. Electrochemical stability in solid-state SMBs. Despite the intrinsic fast ion conduction of Na11Sn2PS12-type SEs, their theoretical thermodynamic stability window is even narrower than that of Na3PS4-type SEs, e.g., 1.16–1.91 V for Na11Sn2PS12 and 1.15–1.59 V for Na11Ge2PS12.603 Na11Sn2PS12 can be reduced and decomposed into Na2S, N3P, and other Na–Sn alloys below the reduction limit, or oxidized to Na3PS4 at high voltages, as shown in Fig. 21a. Interestingly, some of these decomposition products are well known as Na-ion conductors,301,604 which means their “true” interfacial stability is probably widened under the action of the kinetically stable SEI layers. Based on CV measurements, Na11Sn2PS12 exhibited an ESW in the range of 0.3–3.2 V vs. Na+/Na (Fig. 21b),605 while the ESW of Na11Sn2PSe12 was determined to be 1.22–2.65 V vs. Na+/Na.606 Nevertheless, Na11Sn2PS12 is still unstable at the side of the Na metal anode, demonstrated by means of CV scanning and XPS analysis,594 where a reduction reaction between them was observed (Fig. 21c) in a similar manner to Na3PS4 and Li10GeP2S12.240,310 As a result, the solid-state SMB of TiS2/ball-milled Na11Sn2PS12/Na exhibited a rapid capacity decay from 220 mA h g−1 to 110.5 mA h g−1 within 10 cycles, with an initial CE of merely 50.9% at 20 mA g−1 and 25 °C. Akin to Na3PS4-type SEs, Na11Sn2PS12-type SEs are usually paired with the Na alloy anode, e.g., Na–Sn, for a more stable interface and longer cycling life,594,606 with very few research studies focused on the metallic Na anode. A representative work was done by Weng et al. where the liquid-phase synthesized nanosized Na11Sn2PS12 was assembled in the solid-state SMB with the TiS2 cathode and the Na metal anode.605 The TiS2/nanosized-Na11Sn2PS12/Na full cell, without any interfacial modification, delivered an initial discharge capacity of 231 mA h g−1 and an initial CE of 67.96%, with 142.4 mA h g−1 of the reversible capacity retained after 10 cycles. In future, it is important to develop interfacial modification strategies, e.g., formation of a protective interlayer, surface coating or other strategies employed in Na3PS4-type SEs as reference, to effectively mitigate the instability between Na11Sn2PS12-type SEs and the metallic Na anode, to live up to their high ionic conductivity and high energy density of the SMBs.
 Fig. 21  (a) Theoretically calculated ESW of Na11Sn2PS12 with possible decomposition products, reprinted with permission from ref. 603. (b) Experimentally obtained ESW of Na11Sn2PS12 by CV measurements, which is wider than the theoretically calculated value, reprinted with permission from ref. 605. (c) The reduction reaction observed in the Na/Na10.8Sn1.9PS11.8/Sn cell through CV scanning (0.1 mV s−1; −0.5 to 5 V) at 80 °C (upper left panel). Emerged new peak observed in the S 2p spectra via XPS depth profiling of the surface of Na10.8Sn1.9PS11.8 indicating the formation of decomposition products (bottom left panel). Appearance of new peaks detected in the XPS analysis of Na10.8Sn1.9PS11.8 before and after CV scanning, associated to reduced S species (middle panel) and Sn species (right panel), reprinted with permission from ref. 594. 

5.3. Other chalcogenide-based ISEs
5.3.1. Na7P3S11-type. Na7P3S11 is another type of chalcogenide-based SE, derived from its Li-ion counterpart Li7P3S11. In 2014, a sulfide superionic conductor Li7P3S11 glass-ceramic was reported with an extremely high ionic conductivity of 17 mS cm−1 and a low activation energy of 17 kJ mol−1 at room temperature, even superior to liquid ion conductors.607 The outstanding ionic conductivity of Li7P3S11 has motivated investigation into the topology of anion arrangement,607,608 and the underlying bcc-like anion sublattice is revealed to be responsible for the facile ion conduction.255 Among the representative anion sublattice arrangements, i.e., bcc, fcc, and hcp, the bcc-like anion framework has the minimized change of the coordination environment of mobile ions in their migration pathways. For example, the ion hopping paths in bcc-like arrangement are directly linked by adjacent tetrahedral sites, while mobile ions in the other anion frameworks must travel through an octahedral transition site to achieve site hopping from one tetrahedral site to another (Fig. 9). In this way, the energy landscape of mobile ion migration is largely flattened in the bcc-like framework, thus favoring minimized activation energy and facile ion conduction. Indeed, the bcc-like anion sublattice has been found in many sulfide fast ion conductors with high Li-ion conductivity and low activation energy, including Li10GeP2S12609,610 and Li10SnP2S12.611 Therefore, the room-temperature ionic conductivity of Na7P3S11 and Na7P3Se11 has been predicted to be as high as 10.97 mS cm−1 and 12.56 mS cm−1, respectively, on account of the similar bcc-type anion framework contained in their structure.612 According to DFT calculations, Na7P3(S/Se)11 should have a nearly identical crystal structure as that of Li7P3S11, in which Na+ ions are primarily tetrahedrally coordinated and located in seven distinct crystallographic Na+ sites, filling distorted tetrahedral cavities, while these interconnected Na+ sites build a 3D diffusion network. According to AIMD simulations, the evenly distributed probability densities and the smooth energy landscape along ion transport pathways within the bcc-like anion lattice allow unimpeded Na+ migration among the virtually equipotential Na+ sites (Fig. 22). Meanwhile, considering that the crystal structures of Na7P3(S/Se)11 are closely related to that of Li7P3S11, the Na+ sites should be fully occupied in these compounds, which implies highly collective ion motion.
 Fig. 22  The facile Na+ movements among seven Na+ sites with small site energy difference, within the bcc-like anion framework of Na7P3S11 (upper panel) and Na7P3Se11 (bottom panel), adapted with permission from ref. 612. 
Unfortunately, despite the tempting proposal of Na7P3S11-type SEs, their successful synthesis and practical performance have never been validated experimentally. Compared to the more stable Li counterparts, Na7P3S11-type SEs readily become thermodynamically unstable due to their comparably lower entropic free energy contribution.612 Therefore, the heat-treatment and quenching synthesis method for Na7P3(S/Se)11, proved effective in preparing Li7P3S11, tends to end up with decomposition products such as the more stable Na3P(S/Se)4, as well as some other ternaries or binaries.613 A credible strategy to improve the synthesizability of Na7P3(S/Se)11 is to induce site disorder via anion site doping, thereby enhancing the entropic contributions and stabilizing the desired phases.

5.3.2. Oxysulfide. Although many chalcogenide-based SEs have already exhibited sufficiently high ionic conductivity, most of them have rather poor thermodynamic stability and inferior interfacial stability towards the Na metal anode. To take advantage of highly conductive chalcogenide-based SEs while alleviating the unstable interface between them and metallic Na, sulfide electrolytes have been combined with chemically and electrochemically stable oxides, and the novel oxysulfide SE system Na3PS4−xOx was reported to be inherently unreactive to the metallic Na anode, at the expense of slightly lower ionic conductivity.614 Recently, a series of glassy Na3PS4−xOx (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.6) were prepared by facile mechanochemical milling, among which Na3PS3.85O0.15 showed the highest ionic conductivity of 0.27 mS cm−1 at 60°, while Na3PS3.4O0.6 provided better formability allowing for fabrication of thin-film SEs.615 The introduction of oxygen into sulfide glasses formed abundant bridging oxygen, reinforcing the glass network by replacing some of the P–S bonds with stronger P–O bonds. Thereby, homogeneous and defect-free glassy SEs with high densification and mechanical strength were obtained, which resisted the penetration of Na dendrites. Compared to the pure sulfide glassy materials that are unstable with Na metal, oxysulfide SEs established a self-passivating thin SEI layer at their interface with metallic Na, suppressing the decomposition of SEs and enabling the stable sodiation/desodiation processes. As a result, the sandwiched CSE Na3PS3.4O0.6|Na3PS3.85O0.15|Na3PS3.4O0.6 reached a high CCD of 2.3 mA cm−2, exceeding those of all other chalcogenide-based SEs reported so far, while maintaining cycling stability for up to 500 h at 0.2 mA cm−2 (0.2 mA h cm−2), or 240 h at 0.5 mA cm−2 (0.25 mA h cm−2). Impressively, such a sandwiched oxysulfide SE enabled the highest specific energy of the solid-state Na–S ever achieved. Another composite oxysulfide SE was prepared by dispersing Na4P2S4.5O2.5 (NPSO) in the Na3SbS4 (NSS) matrix for faster room-temperature ion conduction compared to the pristine oxysulfide, which indeed exhibited an improved ionic conductivity of 1.4 mS cm−1 (15%NPSO + NSS) and 0.7 mS cm−1 (30%NPSO + NSS).616 In this oxysulfide CSE, a self-passivating oxide interphase was in situ established at the electrolyte/anode interface because the deoxidized P2S7−aOa units in the oxysulfide could release free O2− to migrate through the SE and react with metallic Na, meanwhile the PS4−aOa units remained stable during the cycling and supported the ion transport network. Moreover, the Na dendrites grown in SEs were effectively suppressed and consumed by the NSS matrix. The NPSO + NSS oxysulfides achieved a CCD of 1.83 mA cm−2, where the Na‖Na symmetric cell exhibited soft short-circuit behavior instead of hard short-circuit behavior due to the reaction between the NSS matrix and the grown Na filaments. The stable anodic interface was demonstrated in the full cell SMB Se0.8S7.2@PAN|NPSO+NSS|Na, while the SIB Se0.8S7.2@PAN|NPSO+NSS|Na15Sn4 exhibited a high specific capacity and excellent stability over 1000 cycles at a high current density of 0.3 C at room temperature. Another oxysulfide SE, co-doped by tungsten and oxygen, Na2.95Sb0.95W0.05S3.9O0.1, showed a further enhanced room-temperature ionic conductivity of 8.49 mS cm−1.617 As the passivating decomposition products formed at the interface, from co-doped W and O, the highly conductive oxysulfide ensured excellent compatibility against the metallic Na anode, and the solid-state SMB TiS2/Na2.95Sb0.95W0.05S3.9O0.1/Na manifested both good cycling stability and rate performance by maintaining 115 mA h g−1 after 50 cycles at 0.1 C and 82 mA h g−1 at 0.5 C.So far, most of the research progress has come from the oxide-based SEs and chalcogenide-based SEs, despite their intrinsic flaws, e.g., the complex and strict fabrication procedures required for high ionic conductivity of oxide-based SEs, as well as their rigid nature that renders poor wettability of Na metal at room temperature,238,618 and the inferior thermodynamic stability and intrinsic interfacial stability of chalcogenide-based SEs towards metallic Na.239–242 Worse still, the high mechanical strength of oxide-based SEs may be inadequate to resist the penetration of sodium dendrites as theoretically predicted. Recent studies have revealed that metal dendrites preferentially form and propagate along the grain boundaries or interconnected pores, where the alkali metal ions combine with sufficient electrons under the locally concentrated electric field and directly plate within the material, which eventually causes a short circuit.504,619–621 These problems have greatly impeded their practical application in high-energy-density metal batteries. Therefore, the screening of SE material systems is still ongoing, dedicated to discovering materials that are balanced in terms of ionic conductivity, electrochemical/chemical stability, interfacial compatibility, and facile processing.

6. Halide-based ISEs
The research on halide-based SEs was delayed because of the insufficient room-temperature ionic conductivity of the early Li-ion compounds (<10−5 S cm−1), such as LiX (X = F, Cl, Br, I)622 and LiAlCl4.623,624 However, in the past few years, some new-found Li-ion halide SEs, including Li3MX6 (M = Y, Er, In, Sc, Yb; X = Cl, Br, I) and Li3−xM1−xPnxCl6 (M = Y, Er, Fe, In, Yb; Pn = Zr, Hf), have been discovered with adequate ionic conductivity (10−3 S cm−1) and high-voltage stability (>4 V vs. Li+/Li),531,625–636 thus igniting the resurgence of interest in Na-ion halide-based SEs. Along with the intrinsic flattened energy landscape confirmed in the halogen anion sublattice,260 the monovalent halide ions interact more weakly with the mobile ions compared to divalent sulfide ions or oxygen ions, meeting the prerequisite for fast ion conductors. Noteworthily, such a fast ion conduction in halide-based SEs does not necessarily require a bcc anion sublattice as in other superionic chalcogenide- or oxide-based SEs, leaving much more available choices for material design.260,637 Moreover, the group 17 elements (halogens) have a high value of electronegativity than group 16 elements (oxygen and chalcogens), which corresponds to better oxidative stability,638 with the general trend of oxidative stability following F > Cl > Br > I.638,639 Due to the larger ionic radii of halide ions compared to oxides and chalcogenides, their longer ionic bond length and higher polarizability are expected, thus resulting in facile ion motion and great deformability in halide-based SEs.625,640,641 Hence, halide-based SEs can be a competitive alternative to the existing oxide-based or chalcogenide-based SEs, owing to their fast-conducting characteristics, adequate thermodynamic stability, great deformability, and good oxidative stability.
