Meanwhile, unlike the chalcogenide-based SEs which are extremely sensitive to the ambient environment, halide-based SEs are generally stable against moisture owing to their positive hydrolysis reaction energy.297 Nevertheless, most of the studied halide-based SEs are based on the Li-ion system, with only a few based on the Na-ion system, e.g., Na2ZrCl6,642 Na3YX6 (X = Cl and Br),643 Na3XI6 (X = Sc, Y, La, and In),644,645 Na3MCl6 (M = In and Sc),646 Na3–xM1–xZrxCl6 (M = Y, Er),647,648 and NaAlCl4.649
6.1. Structural-related ion conduction
Halide-based Na-ion SEs have the general composition of Na3−xM1−xM′xX6 based on the research progress so far, where M can be chosen from 3d transition metals, rare-earth elements, icosagens (group 13 elements), pnictogens (group 15 elements), or lanthanides; M′ can be Zr for partial or full substitution of the M site; and X can be halogens Cl, Br, or I. Due to the difference in the ionic radius between Li+ and Na+, the Na-ion conducting halides can exhibit totally different structural features from their Li-ion equivalents. After the thorough screening of Cl-based and Br-based halide SEs, the common crystal structures include trigonal P1c, monoclinic P21/n, and trigonal R phases, largely depending on the type and ionic radius of the M cation in Na3MX6.650,651 However, there are still discrepancies in the determination of their exact crystal structures, particularly considering that most of the current studies are limited to the theoretical calculation without experimental verification, in that ambiguous results can be derived from the different starting model chosen for structure calculation. Herein, the Na+ ion conduction within halide-based SEs is closely related to the crystal structure and geometry.
Na3MCl6 and Na3MBr6. The crystal structure and ionic conductivity of the pristine Na3YCl6 and Na3YBr6 are widely divergent between the experimental value and calculated results, where the trigonal Na3YCl6 (space group R) exhibited a poor ionic conductivity of 10−3 mS cm−1 at 227 °C,652 while the optimized orthorhombic Na3YCl6 (space group D23), as well as the orthorhombic Na3YBr6 (space group C23), were predicted to have a high room-temperature ionic conductivity of 0.77 and 0.44 mS cm−1, respectively, based on first-principles theory.643 In such a calculated orthorhombic structure of Na3YCl6 and Na3YBr6, rigid NaX6 and YX6 octahedra are formed by six-fold coordination from X (halogen atoms Cl or Br) to Na and Y atoms, where two inherent cation vacancies locate in the neighborhood of the Y site in favor of enhanced ion mobility.625,643 Na3YCl6 and Na3YBr6 are different in the NaX6 octahedral stacking sequence, in which Na3YBr6 has edge-to-edge connected NaBr6 in three axis directions, while Na3YCl6 has NaCl6 stacked face-to-face along the c axis or linked edge-to-edge in the other directions (Fig. 23a). Thereby, the face-to-face stacked NaCl6 octahedra provide a fast track for direct hopping of Na+ between adjacent octahedral sites along the c axis, without migrating through the transitional tetrahedral sites as in a and b axis directions, especially considering that some of the tetrahedral sites are blocked under the repulsion from the neighboring Y3+. In this case, the anisotropic Na+ conduction in Na3YCl6 is preferred along the c-axis direction, in contrast to the 3D isotropic Na+ conduction in an octahedral-tetrahedral-octahedral manner within Na3YBr6. Not surprisingly, the direct hopping of Na+ along the c axis in Na3YCl6 ensured its higher ionic conductivity than that of Na3YBr6, as well as the smaller migration activation energy along the c axis (0.24 eV) compared to other directions in Na3YCl6 (0.33 eV) or all directions in Na3YBr6 (0.32 eV). Nonetheless, this theoretical structure calculation was challenged by the experimental report of crystallization in the space group of P21/n for Na3YBr6, as well as the inappropriate starting model simply imitating its Li-ion counterparts.650 In fact, the preferential stable phase of Na-ion halides can be largely different from their Li-ion counterparts, due to the inherent difference between Li and Na, which creates significantly distinct structural-related ion conduction.
 Fig. 23  (a) Theoretically calculated structure of Na3YCl6 (left panel) and Na3YBr6 (right panel), along with their Na+ diffusion pathways and corresponding activation energies acquired by AIMD simulations, adapted with permission from ref. 643. (b) Heat map of the energy above the convex hull (Ehull) for Na3MCl6 (left panel) and Na3MBr6 (right panel) to determine their preferred and stable phase (black box), where Ehull > 25 meV per atom indicates the spontaneous decomposition to phase equilibria, reprinted with permission from ref. 650 and 651. 
This prompts the thorough and systematic investigation of the crystallization of Na3M(Cl/Br)6 from a huge variety of options for M3+ elements, based on the assessment of their phase stability.650,651 The preferred crystal structure and phase stability were found to be correlated to the choice of the M-site cation, as shown in Fig. 23b. According to the combination of DFT calculations, AIMD simulations, and BVSE analysis, a revision of the crystallization of Na3YCl6 and Na3YBr6 was made, and it was established that the former should belong to the trigonal R phase while the latter should belong to the trigonal P1c phase. Among the three general phases, i.e., trigonal P1c, monoclinic P21/n, and trigonal R, the P1c phase was supposed to manifest faster Na+ conduction than the others, owing to the flat ion migration energy landscape and abundant energetically stable interstitial sites in the Na+ diffusion pathways. The ion migration pathways in trigonal phases (R and P1c) are both built in a similar manner as in D23-phase Na3YCl6, exhibiting anisotropic conduction along which the direction of c is different from the a and b axis direction, while the P1c exhibited the lowest migration energy barrier compared to that of others (0.41 eV in Na3MCl6 and 0.39 eV in Na3MBr6). According to these revised calculation results, even the highest ion-conducting compositions in P1c-Na3MCl6 (Na3CrCl6) and P1c-Na3MBr6 (Na3ScBr6) have rather inferior ionic conductivity of the order of magnitude of 10−7 and 10−5 S cm−1 at room temperature, thereby making it indispensable to aliovalent substitute Zr4+ into the M site for ionic conductivity enhancement. Indeed, the experimentally synthesized Na3InCl6 was confirmed to be P1c via single-crystal X-ray diffraction analysis, with a poor ionic conductivity of 2 × 10−5 S cm−1 at 500 K.653

Na3MI6. The phase-structure-dependent ion migration feature of halide-based SEs was corroborated by the significantly varying ionic conductivity in different crystal structures of Na3YBr6, in a broad range from 9.1 × 10−3 mS cm−1 to 0.35 mS cm−1.644 Among the three investigated space groups c2m, P3m1, and P31c, different Na+ diffusion mechanisms are involved, as well as distinct anion sublattice arrangement of symmetric fcc-like configuration in c2m-Na3YI6 and hcp-like configuration in P3m1 and P31c. The c2m and P3m1 phases provide 3D cross-linking networks for Na+ migration through hopping between equilibrium octahedral sites via transitional tetrahedral sites, while the anisotropic Na+ conduction in P31c-Na3YI6 is required to overcome a very high energy barrier to hop through the narrow triangular bottleneck formed by adjacent YI6 octahedra along the c-axis. As a result, despite the identical stoichiometry and similar formation energy, c2m-Na3YI6, P3m1-Na3YI6, and P31c-Na3YI6 exhibited a greatly distinct ionic conductivity of 0.35, 0.18 and 9.1 × 10−3 mS cm−1, respectively. To further probe into the structure–activity relationship of ion conduction in Na3MI6, where M is Sc, Y, La, and In, the c2m phase was anchored as the starting model according to the principle of minimized electrostatic energy.645 The steady anion framework of Na3MI6 can afford the facile Na+ motion in all three dimensions, taking advantage of partially vacant Na site occupation (1/3), except for unstable Na3InI6 which showed In3+ and I− shifting from their equilibrium site through time. Meanwhile, the direct octahedral–octahedral ion hoppings were again observed with much higher activation energy barriers than the octahedral–tetrahedral–octahedral pathways in all compositions, implying the limiting factor as the lattice geometry and the bottlenecks along the c axis. Furthermore, the lattice softness with weakened bond interactions between mobile Na+ and the skeletons was discovered in Na3MI6, as well as the angular rotation of (MI6)3− polyanions,644,645 both promoting the Na+ ion diffusion. Therefore, C2/m-Na3XI6 should possess a high ionic conductivity of 0.2, 0.35, and 0.36 mS cm−1 for Na3LaI6, Na3YI6, and Na3ScI6, respectively, at room temperature.Meanwhile, in an attempt to achieve the practical cost-effectiveness in terms of raw materials, the expensive rare-earth metals, e.g., Y, Sc, La, Er, etc., in halide-based SEs were replaced by the cheap and easily accessible Al, and the NaAlCl4 halide-based SE was successfully developed by facile mechanochemical ball-milling and was found to exhibit an ionic conductivity of 3.9 × 10−6 S cm−1 at 30 °C.649 Although this ball-milled and cold-pressed sample provided improved ionic conductivity compared to the annealed ones by an order of magnitude, owing to the cationic disorders531,654,655 and stacking faults,656 such a value is still undesirable for practical application.

6.2. Tuning the ionic conductivity
Regardless of the appealing value of theoretical ionic conductivity of halide-based SEs, e.g., C2/m-Na3XI6 with room-temperature ionic conductivity over 10−1 mS cm−1, the experimentally obtained samples usually result in significantly lower ionic conductivity at the level of 10−3 to 10−7 mS cm−1, as well as large deviation from the theoretical crystal structures calculated based on their Li-ion counterparts.627,649,652 For instance, the water-mediated synthesized Na3InCl6 had an ionic conductivity of merely 2.23 × 10−8 S cm−1 at 80 °C,627 in line with the experimentally reported ionic conductivity from the previous literature.653,657 As illustrated, the major determinant of the ion conduction within halide-based SEs is their structure and geometry. Therefore, designing optimized structural parameters becomes the key to superionic conduction in halide-based SEs. Furthermore, as the Na+ migration is still largely assisted by the inherent vacancies, another important strategy can be introducing the charge compensation mechanism to increase the concentration of vacancies in the Na+ diffusion pathways. Luckily, these two reasonable modification routes can be simultaneously accomplished by aliovalent doping into M sites, mostly achieved by Zr4+ due to its low dopant formation energy and low cost. Meanwhile, anion mixing and phase-structure design can also be rational strategies to enhance the ion conduction within halide-based SEs.
Zr4+ doping of M3+ cations. The aliovalent doping of Zr4+ into the M3+ site, with changed size, has been demonstrated to be effective in enhancing the ionic conductivity of quaternary sodium halide SEs, Na3−xM1−xZrxCl6 (M = Y or Er), by simultaneously altering the structural geometry of the pristine Na3YCl6 and Na3ErCl6 by inducing disorder of the diffusion paths,651 as well as increasing the concentration of charge carriers, as shown in Fig. 24a.647,648 In the pristine Na3ErCl6, the Na+ sites are fully occupied, leaving deficient adjacent vacancy for ion hopping, thereby negligible ionic conductivity. On the other hand, the pristine Na2ZrCl6 has a honeycomb-like lattice arrangement in the ab-plane by connecting the polyhedra in an edge-to-edge manner, with partial occupation of Na+, which was proved to favor facile ion conduction in its Li-ion counterparts, e.g., Li3ErCl6 and Li3YCl6.531,625,639 As the lattice structure of Na3−xM1−xZrxCl6 inherits the P21/n phase of the pristine Na3ErCl6, the polyhedral volume of (Er1−xZrxCl6)(3−x)− decreases with the increasing amount of Zr4+ due to its smaller size compared to Er3+ (72 pm vs. 89 pm) (Fig. 24a, upper left).450 Meanwhile, when the higher-valent Zr4+ partially substitutes Er3+, occupation in the octahedral polyhedron constantly decreases, indicating the formation of stable vacancies (Fig. 24a, bottom left). As a result, the ionic conductivity is enhanced with reduced activation barriers, until the impact of introduction of vacancies on ion conduction is overwhelmed by the shrunk polyhedral pathways (Fig. 24a, right). This balance between vacancy concentration and free ion migration volume was achieved up to nearly x = 0.6. After that, when Zr4+ substitution exceeds 0.6, a sharp reduction of lattice parameters following the decreased size of (NaCl6)5− polyhedra induces a highly complex interaction from charge carrier density, diffusion pathway, and local distortion, which results in a decrease of ionic conductivity and an increase of activation barriers. Herein, the optimum Na2.4Er0.4Zr0.6Cl6 exhibited a room-temperature ionic conductivity of 4 × 10−5 S cm−1, significantly improved from that of the pristine Na3ErCl6 (10−9 S cm−1). Later, Zr4+-doped Na3−xY1−xZrxCl6 was also reported with enhanced ionic conductivity up to 6.6 × 10−5 S cm−1 at ambient temperature, which was several orders of magnitude higher than that of the pristine Na3YCl6.647 However, the explanation to this improvement seems to be a far cry from that in Na3−xEr1−xZrxCl6, where Zr4+ was observed to increase the lattice volume and expand the bottleneck region with increasing doping concentration, meanwhile leading to extra vacancy formation. For the first time, the rotational motion of polyhedra, i.e., (YCl6)3− and (ZrCl6)2−, was spotted in the experimentally obtained single-ion Cl-based halide SEs, which facilitates Na+ diffusion in a synergistic manner with increased vacancy concentration (Fig. 24b). Noteworthily, in these Zr4+-doped halide-based SEs, the mechanochemical ball milling synthesis method was believed to enable faster ion conduction, by introducing disorders of the local Na+ environment and resulting in less crystallinity of the final products, which was supported by the three-orders-of-magnitude lower ionic conductivity of ball-milled Na2ZrCl6 after annealing.642
 Fig. 24  (a) The plot of change of the polyhedral volume of (Er1−xZrxCl6)(3−x)− (upper left), Na+ site occupation (bottom left), and total ionic conductivity with the amount of Zr4+ (right), where interconnected polyhedra and calculated lowest energy bond-valence landscape with Na+ sites are incorporated, adapted with permission from ref. 648. (b) The probability density of Na3YCl6 and Na2.25Y0.25Zr0.75Cl6 indicating octahedra rotation promoting Na+ diffusion, via 100 ps. AIMD simulation at 600 K (isosurface value = 5× 10−4), adapted with permission from ref. 647. (c) The probability density of Na3YBr3I3 and Na3GdBr3I3 indicating intensified anion vibration and facilitated Na+ diffusion upon anion mixing, through 50 ps. AIMD simulation at 800 K (isosurface value of 2P0), and (d) the improved ionic conductivity with reduced activation energy of anion-mixed Na3GdBr3I3 compared with pristine or other anion mixed halides, reprinted with permission from ref. 658. 

Anion mixing. Anion mixing of I− and Br− is an already well-known strategy to change the lattice parameters and chemical bonding, as well as the redistributed ion site occupation, in Li-ion halide-based SEs, thereby promoting the ion conduction.659–661 In a similar manner, this anion mixing strategy has been demonstrated to be effective in achieving faster Na+ ion conduction in Na-ion halide SEs. For example, by mixing I− with Br−, the room-temperature ionic conductivity of Na3YBr3I3 and Na3GdBr3I3 was found to be drastically improved to 3.3 and 7.5 mS cm−1, in addition to mitigation of the activation energy,658 due to the promoted Na+ diffusion (Fig. 24c and d). This is likely caused by the local disorder in the chemical bonding and intensified anion vibration in the crystal lattice, as shown in Fig. 24c. Surprisingly, an interesting phenomenon of improved ionic conductivity was observed by incorporation of oxyhydryl groups (–OH) in NaBi3O4Cl2 after absorbing water in a moist ambient environment, as the accumulated electrons around the (–OH)-absorption-induced Schottky defects facilitated Na+ diffusion by electrostatic interaction.662
Phase-structure design. On the other hand, applying phase-structure design of sodium halides may be also effective in guiding the experimental effort and improving the acquired ionic conductivity. For instance, the material screening of Cl-based halide SEs claimed that the stable phases P21/n and P1c of Na3ScCl6 and Na3InCl6, according to the principle of M cation dependent phase stability,651 are supposed to exhibit insufficient ionic conductivity. Nevertheless, when the Pm1 phase was adopted for them, Na3ScCl6 (metastable) and Na3InCl6 (stable) are expected to show an ultrahigh ionic conductivity of 11.54 and 3.09 mS cm−1, respectively. The theoretical calculation results demonstrated the two-dimensional diffusion channels along the a and b axis directions in the unique hcp-like sublattice configuration of Cl− anions.646 Moreover, these Pm1-Na3InCl6 and Na3ScCl6 were confirmed to be both dynamically and thermodynamically stable, even below room temperature, indicating their viable synthesizability from the corresponding phase equilibrium compounds. Unfortunately, there has not been experimental attempt to validate this theory.
6.3. Interfacial compatibility with Na metal
Na3YCl6 and Na3YBr6 are thermodynamically stable in a wide range of 0.51–3.75 V and 0.57–3.36 V vs. Na+/Na, respectively,643 in good agreement with the revised theoretical calculations by Zhang et al. and Yu et al.650,651 Although their sufficiently high oxidative limits and excellent chemical stability against cathode materials make it possible for their application with high-voltage cathodes, their reduction limits are still higher than the potential of the metallic Na anode, indicating the intrinsic thermodynamic instability against the Na anode. While the decomposition reaction energy at the SE/Na interface was claimed to be extremely small, i.e., 50 meV per atom for Na3YCl6 and 17 meV per atom for Na3YBr6, implying their sluggish kinetics or even reversible reactions towards Na metal in practice,643 some other work suggested that the Na metal/halide SE interface might require additional protection modification because of the much-higher-than-expected reduction reaction energy. Meanwhile, as Na3MCl6 and Na3MBr6 are reduced by the Na metal, the decomposition products should contain electron-conducting M metals, resulting in the unstable SEI layer and possible reduction reaction continuing into the SE bulk. Interestingly, the resistance to reduction reaction and hence the decomposition reaction energy are relevant to the M cations.297 As Zr4+ are doped into the M3+ site, the reduction limit of Na3−xY1−xZrxCl6 became even higher compared to that of Na3YCl6 (1.5–3.8 V vs. Na+/Na), in addition to the increased reaction energy with metallic Na (340 meV per atom), implying the impaired stability against the Na anode.647 Recently, the electrolyte/anode interface of Na2.25Y0.25Zr0.75Cl6 was examined against the Sn anode upon electrochemical cycling, and an electronically conducting and ionically insulating interface was found, indicating its reductive instability.663 I-based halide SEs usually have lower oxidation potentials of approximately 3 V, with a similar deficient thermodynamic stability against metal Na as Cl-based and Br-based halides.658 With such a concern, almost all the halide-based SEs are still using the Na alloy metal anode instead of metallic Na.642,647,649
6.4. Guidelines for future interfacial modification
Considering that halide-based SEs are intrinsically compatible with high-voltage cathodes, it would be a pity if the Na metal anode cannot be added into the combination. To the best of our knowledge, there has never been successful or even attempted trial of utilizing Na-ion halide SEs in room-temperature SMBs. Therefore, it is important to propose a strategic guideline for interfacial modification of Na-ion halide SEs against the metallic Na anode. Using their Li-ion counterparts for reference,664 the reasonable modification strategies include designing bilayer-structured SEs,665–668 introducing an electron-blocking interfacial interlayer,579 designing a quasi-binary composite electrolyte,669,670 and doping fluorine or nitrogen.666,671 For example, the bilayer-structured SE strategy was already proved effective in NASICON-based SMBs, by coating a composite polymer electrolyte on the NASICON ceramics to optimize the electrolyte/Na interface.672 In this way, an appropriate reduction-resisting layer can also be built on halide-based SEs to mitigate the interfacial instability between them and the Na metal anode. A similar strategy is to insert an electron-blocking but ion-conducting interlayer between halide-based SEs and the metallic Na anode, thereby stabilizing their interface. The quasi-binary composite electrolyte can be chosen from the compositional range between two phases that are intrinsically thermodynamically stable against Na metal (one of them is halide), such as Li3N–LiX (X = Cl, Br, and I) in the Li-ion counterparts.673 Doping fluorine or nitrogen into the halide-based SEs may favor the in situ formation of a fluorine/nitrogen-rich surface protection layer at the Na/electrolyte interface, which has been used in e lithium-ion solid-state SMBs666,674 and some sodium-ion liquid-electrolyte SMBs.675–677
7. Antiperovskite ISEs
Antiperovskites have been recently recognized as a new type of SEs, despite their common application in a wide range of research fields.678,679 In 2012, the Li+-rich antiperovskites Li3OCl and Li3OBr were discovered to provide 3D fast ion conduction with room-temperature ionic conductivity over 1 mS cm−1 and ultralow activation energies,680 followed by the later research further promoting their ionic conductivity to 25 mS cm−1 and demonstrating their great compatibility with the metallic Li anode.681–683 By sharing the same stoichiometry and topology with the conventional perovskite structure, the antiperovskite structure consists of inverted atomic positions (Fig. 25a), that is the center atom turns from a cation in perovskites into an anion in antiperovskites, while the outer atoms change from anions to cations. Thereby, antiperovskites are usually denoted by an inverse formula of perovskites, written as X3BA, where X is a monovalent cation (usually Li+ or Na+), B is usually a divalent chalcogenide or oxygen anion, and A is usually a monovalent halide or hydride anion, or it can be a polyanion cluster. With such a cation–anion inversion, the antiperovskite structure is intrinsically Na+-rich. Based on the selection of B and A anions, antiperovskite SEs can be categorized into (1) oxyhalide antiperovskites, (2) halide hydroxide antiperovskites,684 (3) cluster-based antiperovskites, (4) sodium hydride antiperovskites,685 or (5) double antiperovskites,686–688 and the current research on Na+ antiperovskite SEs mostly emphasizes on the oxyhalide-type and cluster-based-type.689 Similar to the successful Li-ion precedents, the Na-ion antiperovskite SEs are expected to exhibit high ionic conductivity, wide electrochemical stability, good compatibility with Na metal, and facile structure manipulation. Compared to the Li-ion oxyhalide antiperovskites that are thermodynamically metastable as suggested by first-principles DFT calculations,690 Na-ion antiperovskites favor thermodynamic stability at normal pressure and temperature and thus facile synthesis. However, the direct substitution of Li with Na in antiperovskites initially resulted in much lower ionic conductivity at room temperature,275,691–699 until the recent discovery of the superionic cluster-based Na+-antiperovskite,276 Na3OBH4, that inspired the subsequent research of more fast-conducting antiperovskite SEs. Herein, the ion conduction mechanism and Na metal compatibility of Na-ion antiperovskite SEs, as well as their unsolved mysteries, are thoroughly elucidated to guide their future research.
 Fig. 25  Schematic illustration of the (a) prototypical structure of an antiperovskite (left), sharing the same topology with the perovskite (right), (b) 3D cubic structure of the X3BA antiperovskite and (c) 2D layered structure of the Ruddlesden–Popper (RP) phase X4BA2 antiperovskite. 
7.1. Ion conduction mechanisms and modification strategies
Structure influenced conduction. Ideally, antiperovskite-type SEs have a 3D cubic structure with Pmm space group, or a 2D Ruddlesden–Popper (RP) phase, as shown in Fig. 25b and c. The standard cubic structure of Na3BA is assembled in a bcc-like lattice arrangement, well known as the fast ion conducting structure, where each divalent B anion at the cubic center is six-fold coordinated with the Na+ at the cubic faces, forming corner-sharing Na6B octahedra, while monovalent A anions reside at the cubic corners, being cuboctahedrally coordinated to 12 neighboring Na+. In this isostructural 3D ion-conducting framework, all Na+ ions locate at the crystallographically equivalent sites and mobile Na+ ions diffuse along the preferred migration channels along the octahedral edges depending on the formation of defects.696,700 Despite the poor ionic conductivity of pristine oxyhalide antiperovskites Na3OA (A = Cl, Br, I) with cubic structure Pmm,275,694,697,698,701 the incorporation of larger A anions seemed to generate a mismatching effect, which tailored the free volume for Na+ hopping through interstitials, i.e., Frenkel defects, while the introduction of higher-valent cations into Na+ sites induced charge-compensating vacancies, i.e., Schottky defects, thus both facilitating ion conduction.696 In fact, the facile structure manipulation of antiperovskites usually results in their tilted octahedra and distorted crystal structures from the perfect cubic structure, which is not only closely related to the ionic radii of X, B, and A ions, described by the Goldschidt's tolerance factor (t),700,702,703 but also affected by the external pressure and temperature. The trend of decreasing tolerance factor t with the decreasing size of the A anion in Na3OA (X = Cl, Br, I) continues in a wider range, indicating the crystal structure gradually shifting to lower symmetry (tetragonal or orthorhombic) with smaller A anions, or converting to a hexagonal phase with face-sharing octahedra when oversized A anions or undersized B anions are incorporated.276,693,695–697,699,701,704,705 With the increasing size of A anions, the Na+ ion migration tends to be facilitated, reflected by the quantitative trend of surging ionic conductivity with reduced activation energy. From the perspective of crystal structure and lattice interaction, this can be explained by the increasing effective Na–O bond length as the crystal volume and lattice symmetry shift to a higher value, which results in weaker electrostatic interaction with mobile Na+, while the more polarizable large A anions can soften the lattice. Similarly, replacing the B anion with more polarizable ones may also benefit the ion conduction, e.g., O2− by S2−. A similar trend was observed in Ruddlesden–Popper (RP) antiperovskites Na4BA2, with a layered structure consisting of inter-stacked antiperovskite slabs Na3BA and rock salt layer NaA, where the smaller size of the A anion led to a more compact layer structure and stronger electrostatic interaction with A anions, thus impeding the Na+ hopping.686,706 Although such a structural effect from A anions has been validated from theoretical calculations and experimental data, the room-temperature ionic conductivity of the obtained oxyhalide antiperovskites usually lies below 10−6 S cm−1.
Lattice dynamics. Interestingly, cluster-based antiperovskites with a superhalogen cluster anion at the A site were demonstrated with much higher ionic conductivity,275,694,707,708 particularly Na3O(BH4). Na3O(BH4) delivered a high ionic conductivity of 4.4 mS cm−1 at room temperature and low activation energy of 0.25 eV, several orders of magnitude higher than the oxyhalide antiperovskites,276 due to the slightly larger size of (BH4)− polyanion cluster (203 pm of (BH4)− vs. 196 pm of Br− or 220 pm of I−).450 In addition to the slightly expanded structure and ion hopping channel, such an abnormally high ionic conductivity of Na3O(BH4) was explained by the rotational motion of the polyanion clusters to a large extent, known as the “paddle-wheel” effect.709–711 As illustrated in the previous sections, the paddle-wheel mechanism kicks the surrounding mobile Na+ to achieve much faster ion motion. However, this assertion has been challenged by later research, which failed to reproduce the superionic Na3O(BH4) through either experiment or AIMD simulation.698
Defect concentration and mobility. As in any other solid ion conductor, the Na+ ion conduction in antiperovskite SEs requires the presence of defects, whereas the determination of their dominant charge carriers (vacancies or interstitials) has long been debated in both Li-rich and Na-rich antiperovskites. Compared to the heated haggle over the dominant charge carriers in Li-ion antiperovskites,680,690,712–718 as well as the extremely complex assertions, there has been significantly less detailed studies of this topic in Na-ion antiperovskites and most of the work still used their Li-ion counterpart for reference. By investigating the three common and plausible neutral defect pairs in pristine Na3OCl, i.e., Na Frenkel defect, NaCl Schottky defect pair, and Na2O Schottky pair, Ciucci et al. concluded that the vacancy hopping mechanism was dominant in the Na3OCl antiperovskite, considering the lowest formation energy of the NaCl Schottky defect pair (1.18 eV) with both the Na vacancy and Cl vacancy coexisting,719 while the activation energy of vacancy-mediated Na diffusion was estimated as 0.42 eV. This theory was supported by the recent investigation of the Na3OCl antiperovskite at the atomic scale. Meanwhile, this is different from the previous claim by Zhu et al. stating the Na2O Schottky defect as the dominant type in Na3OBr,697 which referred to the pair of two Na vacancies and one O vacancy. Although the underlying Na+ conduction mechanism in the Na-ion antiperovskite still remains unclear and the exact description may require case by case inspection, the ion hopping mechanism in the Na-ion antiperovskite seems to be mostly relevant to Na vacancies, corresponding to the modification strategies of aliovalent doping.
7.2. Modification strategies for fast ion conduction
Compared to the pristine Li+ oxyhalide antiperovskites with already high ionic conductivity, the Na+ antiperovskite SEs are still weak in room-temperature ion conduction,679,703 especially considering that the perfect antiperovskite structure with the lack of defects or charge carriers is unfavorable for ion conduction. Therefore, rational modification of the Na+ antiperovskite is necessary for fulfilling the requirement of sufficient ionic conductivity, and the common strategies are summarized in Fig. 26.
 Fig. 26  Four common modification strategies to promote Na+ conduction within antiperovskite SEs. 
Aliovalent doping for defect formation. By doping higher-valent cations into the Na+ site, the induced charge compensation can introduce higher concentration of Na+ vacancies, which is believed to be responsible for Na+ ion hopping in antiperovskites. A series of solid solutions of Na3OA (A = Cl, Br, I) were prepared to investigate the effect of aliovalent doping in the Na+ site.696 As a result, the ionic conductivity of Na2.9Sr0.05OBr0.6I0.4 was boosted to 1.89 mS cm−1 at 200 °C, benefited from the formation of Na+ vacancies by the doping of divalent Sr2+, largely improved from that of the pristine Na3OBr and Na3OCl with an ionic conductivity of approximately 10−2 mS cm−1 at 200 °C, or from that of halide-mixed Na3OBr0.6I0.4 of 0.43 mS cm−1. In a similar manner, larger substitutional defects and formation of Na vacancies are also favored by Ba2+ due to their lower substitutional solution energies.684,719 However, this strategy of creating Na vacancies by doping large-size higher-valent cations must take into account their binding energy with associated Na vacancies, since an adverse effect may emerge with the increasing activation energy of Na diffusion with stronger binding. In this way, Ca2+ can be a valid option to effectively improve the ionic conductivity of oxyhalide antiperovskite Na3OCl because of its weak binding with the adjacent Na vacancy.719 However, Islam et al. recently proposed another conclusion that Ca2+, Al3+, and Ga3+ tend to cause local defect clustering and high binding energy with associated Na vacancies, thus hindering the migration of mobile charge carriers.720 Instead, the Mg2+ dopant was affirmed with the weakest binding energy and became the most appropriate candidate for creating Na vacancies and enhancing Na+ conduction.
Isovalent substitution and anion mixing. The O2− anions at the B site in Na3BA or Na4BA2 antiperovskites can be commonly replaced by S2− anions, where such an isovalent substitution can facilitate the mobile Na+ motion due to the reduced electrostatic interaction from S2− compared to that from O2−, known as “lattice softening”.721,722 On the other hand, anion mixing of the A site can be regarded as another kind of isovalent substitution, where the halide ions can be mixed with each other, or with superhalogen polyanion clusters; however the contributing factor is not ion valence but the size effect. In this way, the ordered sublattice in the pristine antiperovskite structures is broken by the mismatch effect of different A anions, thereby inducing structure disordering, which almost always leads to extra free volume for mobile Na+ migration and thus the higher ionic conductivity and lower activation energy compared to the single-halide antiperovskite SEs.717 For instance, the ultrahigh room-temperature ionic conductivity of Na3S(BCl4) of 2.8 mS cm−1 as well as the minimized activation energy of 0.17 eV were shown by Fang and Jena, via the simultaneous isovalent substitution of the B site and A site by S2− and a polyanion cluster, respectively, where lattice softening and lattice dynamics both significantly promoted the Na+ migration.710 By anion mixing of (BCl4)− and I−, the ionic conductivity of Na3S(BCl4)0.5I0.5 was further increased to 3.8 mS cm−1 along with a reduced activation energy of 0.15 eV, attributed to the size effect of mixed A anions with a large size difference. Another example for successful isovalent substitution is presented by simultaneously substituting Li+ in the Na+ site and S2− in the O2− site, where the high ionic conductivity of Ruddlesden–Popper antiperovskite Na3LiS0.5O0.5I2 was predicted to be 6.3 mS cm−1 with 0.12 eV activation energy at room temperature, owing to the “knocking-on effects” of the smaller Li+ encouraging the long-distance migration of coexisting larger Na+.706 Recently, aliovalent doping of Mg2+ in the Na+ site and anion mixing of Cl− with F− in Na-rich fluorinated sulfate antiperovskite Na3SO4F resulted in several-orders-of-magnitude improvement in ionic conductivity, where Na2.98Mg0.01SO4F0.95Cl0.05 exhibited ionic conductivity close to 0.1 mS cm−1 at 60 °C, due to the synergistic effect of created Na vacancies and structure expansion.723 Goodenough et al. demonstrated the high room-temperature ionic conductivity of 10 mS cm−1 and the reduced activation energy of less than 1 eV in co-doped Na2.99Ba0.005OCl1−x(OH)x, in the glass-amorphous phase.684
Inserting the rotational polyanion cluster. Although Ahiavi et al. claimed that they were unable to reproduce the high ionic conductivity of Na3O(BH4) or identify the promoting effect of the rotational (BH4)− cluster,276,698 the rotational polyanion clusters still motivate the design of fast ion-conducting antiperovskites based on both experimental investigation and theoretical analysis. By simultaneously substituting the amide (NH2)− cluster at the B site and replacing the A site with (BH4)− in the Na3BA antiperovskite, a “double paddle-wheel” effect was proposed in Na3(NH2)(BH4) with both rotational cluster groups promoting Na+ conduction, in distinct ways.724 In this dual-cluster-based antiperovskite, as the slowly rotating amide cluster obeyed the “paddle-wheel mechanism”, the indirect correlation between the fast-rotating borohydride cluster and Na+ diffusion was observed for the first time, which may explain the failed attempt at the replication of fast-conducting Na3O(BH4). Meanwhile, by comparing the calculated jump attempt frequencies of carrier migrations in Na3OBr and Na3O(BH4), the (BH4)− cluster was proved to mitigate the activation energy and facilitate the ion hopping by a large margin.725 Even though the calculated ionic conductivity of cluster-based antiperovskites still cannot match the high value from the experimental result,276 such a difference can be interpreted by the overestimated migration barrier and underestimated defect concentration. In this manner, a variety of polyanion clusters were predicted to accomplish the high ionic conductivity and low activation energy of cluster-based antiperovskite SEs, such as (AlF4)−, (ClO4)−, (Icl4)−, and (IO4)−, among which Na3SAlF4 manifested the highest ionic conductivity of 65.5 mS cm−1 and the activation energy of 0.19 eV based on first-principles DFT calculations,726 due to the multifold optimizations including active lattice dynamics, widened diffusion channels and stabilized geometry structure. Doping of large polyanion units into the B site, e.g., (SO4)2− and (SeO4)2−, is also an effective way to improve ion transport. Theoretically formulated Na3(SO4)F0.5Cl0.5 and Na3(SeO4)F0.5Cl0.5 antiperovskite SEs exhibited a superior ionic conductivity of 1.09 mS cm−1 and 8.17 mS cm−1 at room temperature, respectively, as well as the activation energy down to 0.137 eV.727However, the summarized ion conduction mechanism and modification strategies are made up on an ideal scenario within the bulk grain of single-crystal antiperovskites, which only concerns point defects other than the microstructural defects. Regarding the oxygen backbone of antiperovskite SEs, they are supposed to feature similar characteristics as the conventional oxide SEs. This means the grain boundary effect might play an important role in their ion conduction in practical application, which has already been extensively studied in Li+ antiperovskites.289,728–732

7.3. Interfacial compatibility with Na
Antiperovskite SEs are known for their great thermodynamic stability against the metallic Na anode. As most of the SEs can be reduced in contact with Na metal (0V vs. Na+/Na) due to the reducible transition metal elements, prototypical antiperovskite SEs are considered robust against the Na metal anode with either no decomposition or an almost negligible driving force for reduction. For example, Na3OBr is considered thermodynamically stable in the range of 0–1.79 V vs. Na+/Na.396 The pristine Ruddlesden–Popper antiperovskites and double antiperovskites are also stable against the metallic Na anode, i.e., ESW of 0–1.66 V for Na4OI2 and 0–1.5 V vs. Na+/Na for Na6SOI2.686,733 This is ascribed to both the electronically insulating nature of the pristine antiperovskite structure and unreducible elements contained.689 For some of the modified antiperovskite SEs, the coating layer may be necessary for a thermodynamically unstable interface with the Na metal anode; e.g., Na3SeO4F0.5Cl0.5 and Na3SO4F0.5Cl0.5 have reductive limits of 0.954 V and 0.68 V vs. Na+/Na, respectively.687 Nonetheless, the formation of ionically conductive but electronically insulating NaF and NaCl might help their Na metal interface maintain kinetic stability in some way. On the other hand, the major obstacle for the practical application of antiperovskite SEs in high-energy-density batteries comes from their limited oxidative thresholds. Unfortunately, considering that the development of Na-rich antiperovskite SEs is still at its early stage, there has not been many SMBs fabricated based on them. In 2020, Fan et al. fabricated solid-state SIBs and SMBs using Na2.98Mg0.01SO4F0.95Cl0.05 SEs and the Prussian blue cathode, with the Na–Sn alloy anode and Na metal anode, respectively.723 The solid-state SMB exhibited a fast capacity decay from 83.3 mA h g−1 to 34.5 mA h g−1 within 10 cycles at 0.05 mA cm−2 and 60 °C, despite the use of an ionic liquid for wetting the electrode/electrolyte interface. In comparison, the cell with the Na–Sn alloy anode delivered a higher initial discharge capacity of 91 mA h g−1 with 77 mA h g−1 retained after 20 cycles.
8. Complex hydride ISEs
To optimize the application of solid-state SMBs, the development of SEs has been expanded beyond the typical ones, in which complex hydrides might be one of the most forefront categories. In the early 2010s, the research of Na+ ion conduction and electrochemical stability of complex hydrides sprang up,734–736 spurred by the studies of Li+-conducting complex hydride systems, e.g. complex anions (BH4)−, (NH2)−, (AlH4)−, (AlH6)3−, etc.670,737–742 Unfortunately, these Na+ pioneers of Na(BH4)-type complex hydrides tended to exhibit much lower room-temperature ionic conductivity compared to their successful Li-ion counterparts, e.g. 2.1 × 10−10 S cm−1 and 6.4 × 10−7 S cm−1 for NaAlH4 and NA3AlH6, respectively. Although the mechanochemically ball-milled Na(BH4)0.5(NH2)0.5 readily owns a high-temperature phase in the antiperovskite-like structure, with sufficient intrinsic Na+ vacancies, its ionic conductivity is still of the order of magnitude of 10−6 S cm−1, which is far from that of the highly conductive high-temperature phase in Li-ion complex hydrides after the order–disorder phase transition occurring at certain transition temperature.278,743–746 Even so, these complex hydrides were predicted to have wide electrochemical stability windows, high thermal stability, and great compatibility toward the Na metal anode. Luckily, a particular type of complex hydride, closo-borohydrides, emerged as a promising candidate for Na-ion SEs due to their vacancy-rich disordered structure above a relatively low phase transition temperature.
8.1. Ion conduction in borohydrides
closo-Borohydride SEs are written as Na2(BnHn) (usually 6 ≤ n ≤ 12), by incorporating large-size cage-like closo-borate anions, e.g. (B10H10)2− and (B12H12)2−, in which the spacious interstitial framework of gigantic anions favors Na+ migration compared to that constructed by much smaller polyanions like (BH4)− (Fig. 27a and b).747,749 In addition to the widened Na+ transport channels, the order–disorder phase transition was found to occur at relatively low temperatures, in addition to the “paddle-wheel” mechanism of these large polyanions, both promoting the Na+ diffusion. At low temperature, Na2B12H12 is ground-stable in the ordered monoclinic phase with P21/n symmetry, where eight Na+ locate around the (B12H12)2− icosahedron and each one of these Na+ coordinates with the four most adjacent (B12H12)2− icosahedra through nine H atoms, as shown in Fig. 27b and Fig. 27c (left).748 At high temperature near 529 K, Na2B12H12 undergoes phase transition to a disordered bcc polymorph with Pmn symmetry where the quasi-spherical (B12H12)2− anions arrange in bcc-like packing with substantial orientational disorder (Fig. 27c, center). The redistribution of partially occupied Na+ cations among a large amount of off-centered Na+ sites within the distorted tetrahedral interstices of the anion sublattice facilitates the facile hopping of mobile Na+, in contrast to the fully occupied Na+ sites in the ordered low-temperature phase.749,750 As the anion and cation disorder extends with increasing temperature, a second bcc phase with Imm symmetry appears at 545 K, in addition to a minor fraction of the high-temperature structure with the fcc pattern (Fig. 27c, right). Upon phase transition to the highly disordered high-temperature phases, the reinforced rotation of (B12H12)2− anions, indicated by a hundred-fold increase in reorientational jump rates, greatly promotes the mobile ion diffusion with the aid of large population of Na+ vacancies in the high-temperature phases.750,751 The ionic conductivity of high-temperature phase Na2B12H12 dramatically increases by nearly three orders of magnitude from that of its low-temperature phase, exceeding 0.1 S cm−1 at temperature between 540 K and 573 K.749 Compared to Na2B12H12, sodium decahydro-closo-decaborate Na2B10H10 has significantly lower phase transition temperature near 360 K, above which a greatly disordered fcc-type arrangement forms with a highly mobile vacancy-rich Na+ cationic sublattice within the large-size (B10H10)2− anionic framework, rendering a high ionic conductivity of 10 mS cm−1 at 383 K with a low activation energy of 0.47 eV, or 0.1 S cm−1 at 500 K.747 In a similar manner to Na2B12H12, by virtue of the large spheroidal polyanions and liquid-like Na+ cationic sublattice, mobile Na+ ions are allowed to migrate along the unrestricted pathways with a shallow migration energy landscape provided by the reduced occupation of Na+ over a variety of interstices in the high-temperature phase of Na2B10H10, while the paddle-wheeling polyanions further lubricate the Na+ hopping among the voids of the anion framework. Nevertheless, these fast ion conduction behaviors in closo-borohydride SEs only occur at temperatures above the phase transition temperature, which are still much higher than room temperature. To realize the superionic conducting borohydrides for room-temperature solid-state SMBs, efforts must be made to either lower or even eliminate the order–disorder transition temperature, or stabilize the highly conductive high-temperature disorder at room temperature.
 Fig. 27  (a) The top and side views of the geometry of cage-like closo-borate anions (B10H10)2− and (B12H12)2−, and polyanion (BH4)−, along with their relative sizes, reprinted with permission from ref. 747. (b) The unit cell framework of Na2B12H12 and Na+ interaction with neighboring atoms and polyanions, in the ordered low-temperature monoclinic phase (P21/n), from the view along the lattice direction of (100), reprinted with permission from ref. 748. (c) The order–disorder phase transition from the low-temperature monoclinic phase P21/n (left panel) to the partially disordered high-temperature Pmn phase (center panel), then to the fully disordered high-temperature cubic Imm phase (right panel), at different transition temperatures with hysteresis, from the view along the c axis, reprinted with permission from ref. 749 and 750. 
8.2. Modification strategies for room-temperature conduction
The principal modification methods for closo-borohydride SEs include chemical alteration of closo-borane, anion mixing, and crystalline nanosizing and disordering. As the order–disorder phase transition is driven by the structure entropy caused by cation and anion disorder, the transition temperature is supposed to be correlated with the variation of enthalpy during the process. The rational tuning of the size ratio between cation and anion or change of polyanion symmetry should adopt reduced phase transition temperature and enhanced ionic conductivity.
Chemical alteration/substitution within closo-borane. Chemical alteration of the closo-borate anion is commonly achieved by replacing one boron atom in (BnHn)2− with a carbon atom, resulting in monocarba-closo-borate anions, e.g., (CB9H10)− (Fig. 28a) and (CB11H12)− (Fig. 28b), and compositions such as NaCB9H10 and NaCB11H12. The carbon-substituted closo-borohydride SEs can manifest both enhanced Na+ conduction and declined phase transition temperature. For example, NaCB11H12 transforms from an ordered orthorhombic structure to a disordered face-centered-cubic phase near 380 K, which is substantially lower than that of its pristine material Na2B12H12 at near 529 K.752 Moreover, the ionic conductivity of NaCB11H12 is over 0.12 S cm−1 at T ≥ 383K with a low activation energy of 0.22 eV. The significant enhancement of ion conduction can be explained by multiple reasons, including the change of the anion valence, the orientational mobility of monocarba-closo-borane, the concentration of Na+ vacancies, and the alteration of the lattice constant. Compared to the divalent (B12H12)2− anion, the reduced anion charge of the monovalent (CB11H12)− anion results in weaker coulombic interaction between mobile Na+ cations and anion clusters, while the locally repulsive nature of the introduced carbon atom creates a dipole that drives local redistribution of cations in response to the fluctuating potential, both of which lead to facile ion conduction of Na+ ions.753 Notwithstanding the similar size and geometry of the carbon incorporated closo-borate cluster as its pristine counterpart, the slightly larger lattice constant of NaCB11H12 indicates more free space for Na+ ion transport.754 Additional Na+ vacancy sites are likely generated in the crystal lattice due to less charge compensation required for monovalent (CB11H12)− anions, providing more accessible ion diffusion pathways. In addition to the modification of the local static cation–anion interactions, the incorporation of the carbon atom further modifies the orientational preference and dynamics of the anion, producing frustration that hinders the formation of a stable ordered structure, hence lowering the phase transition temperature and enhancing Na+ diffusion. Impressively, the temperature for the onset of order–disorder transition was further reduced to 290 K for NaCB9H10.755 Once the high-temperature disordered phase forms above 310 K, it remains stable down to 285 K, which means that the room-temperature superionic disordered phase is available as long as it can be heated up to 310 K whenever the temperature happens to drop below 285 K. Consequentially, NaCB9H10 delivers a supreme ionic conductivity of 3 mS cm−1 at room temperature after the abovementioned initial activation cycle of heating and cooling. Likewise, the hydrogen atom can also be substituted by halides to adjust the relative enthalpies of the ordered and disordered state, thereby lowering the phase transition temperature.749 A series of Na2B12H12−xIx were obtained by partially substituting hydrogen by iodine, which delivered an ionic conductivity close to 0.1 S cm−1 at 360 K, where the phase transition temperature was found to lie between those of NaCB11H12 and NaCB9H10.756 However, full substitution of hydrogen with halogen atoms resulted in a rather high phase transition temperature for Na2B12X12 (X is halide ions), following a positive relationship with the increasing anion size and mass H < Cl < Br < I, which is ascribed to the increasing strength and directionality of Na–halogen interactions that restricted the reorientation disorder and dynamic motion until high temperature.757
 Fig. 28  The relative geometry of (a) (CB9H10)− by replacing one boron atom with a carbon atom from (B10H10)2−, reprinted with permission from ref. 755 and (b) (CB11H12)− from (B12H12)2−, reprinted with permission from ref. 752 with Mulliken charges marked on the structurally different atoms of the isolated anions. (c) The relative geometry of open-structure nest-like nido-poly(carba)borate anions, reprinted with permission from ref. 758. 

Reducing the symmetry of closo-borane. On the basis of quasi-spherical cage-like closo-polyborate and closo-polycarbaborate anion clusters, e.g., (B12H12)2−, (B10H10)2−, (CB11H12)−, and (CB9H10)−, a related type of nest-like nido-poly(carba)borate polyanion, i.e., (B11H14)−, (CB10H13)−, and (C2B9H12)−, was discovered to favor a room-temperature-stable and intrinsically disordered structure that exhibited high ionic conductivity and an unobvious order–disorder phase transition.758 The nido-structure is formed in a similar manner as the closo-structure, but one of the icosahedral cage atoms in the closo-structure is completely removed for forming the nido-structure.759–761 Thereby, the unity and symmetry of the closed and cage-like closo-structure is reduced, shifting to an open and nest-like nido-structure (Fig. 28c). Although the investigated nido-borane SEs generally exhibited lower ionic conductivity compared to that of closo-carbaborane SEs, most of them are still better than closo-borane SEs, among which Na3B11H14 delivers the highest ionic conductivity of 45 mS cm−1 at 413 K or approximately 1 mS cm−1 at room temperature. In the future, due to the similar size and geometry to closo-borane, nido-borane may be substituted into the closo-boranes to form anion-mixed solid solutions of borohydride SEs to acquire room-temperature-stable structures with intrinsic disorder.
Anion mixing. Anion mixing, or sectional substitution of the polyanion by another, has been the most promising approach to suppress or eliminate the phase transition temperature so far. Through the introduction of geometric frustration from mixed anions, the phase ordering can be restrained, and the desired disordered phase can be stabilized over a wide range of temperature from sub ambient temperature to high temperature. By ball-milling NaBH4 and Na2B12H12, borohydride and closo-borate anions were well mixed without reaction at high energies, where such a mixed-anion compound Na3(BH4)(B12H12) maintains stability in a broad range of temperature from 100 K to 653 K and does not require thermal activation for ionic motion once the structure has already been established in the synthesis process.711 Na3(BH4)(B12H12) presented a 2D conduction pathway constructed by alternating stacking of Na-rich slabs and Na-free layers, where mobile Na+ favored the superionicity in the Na-rich mixed-anion slabs in contrast to the impeded motion within the Na-free closo-borane slabs, resulting in the superior ionic conductivity of 0.5 mS cm−1 at room temperature as well as activation energy down to 0.34 eV. Prepared by drying aqueous solutions of equimolar amounts of NaCB9H10 and NaCB11H12, mixed-closo-polycarbaborate compound Na2(CB9H10)(CB11H12) adopted an intrinsically disordered hexagonal phase,762 which usually only exists in the high-temperature phase of pristine NaCB9H10,755 indicating the readily obtained highly conductive phase with a suppressed phase transition temperature. As a result, Na2(CB9H10)(CB11H12) delivered an ultrahigh ionic conductivity of 70 mS cm−1 at room temperature. Another pseudo-binary mixed-closo-polyborane SE Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 also offered a high ionic conductivity of 0.9 mS cm−1 at 20 °C, while its excellent thermodynamic stability against Na metal made it practicable in solid-state SMBs.302 A similar result was obtained by varying the molar ratio of (B12H12)2− and (B10H10)2− to 3:1 in 0.25Na2B10H10–0.75Na2B12H12.763 A series of mixed-anion solid-solutions of Na2−x(CB11H12)x(B12H12)1−x and Na4(CB11H12)2(B12H12) have been presented with superior ionic conductivity within range of 1–2 mS cm−1 as well as high oxidative electrochemical stability above 4 V vs. Na+/Na.764–767 Most recently, a novel conjuncto-hydroborane SE Na3B24H23 was prepared by oxidizing closo-borate (B12H12)2−, where its composite electrolyte Na3B24H23–5Na2B12H12 exhibited a high room-temperature ionic conductivity and Na+ transference number of 1.43 mS cm−1 and 0.97, respectively, in low density, regardless of its more impressive electrochemical stability and compatibility with Na metal.768 With more superionic borohydride SEs being found, their compatibility in all-solid-state batteries with metallic Na as the anode was experimentally investigated.
Crystallite nanosizing and ball-milling disordering. While several material synthesis strategies have been proved to favor the formation of a conductive disordered phase,762,769–773 the most effective synthesis strategy to achieve fast ion conduction within borohydride SEs at room temperature is the introduction of crystallite nanosizing or disordering via mechanochemical ball-milling. With the continuous impact during mechanical ball-milling, the disordered phases, which should only form above the order–disorder phase transition temperature, can be generated and stabilized at room temperature.774 Moreover, unexpected compounds or alloy phases may also result from the ball-milling of the mixture of various precursors, resulting in surprising behaviors of mixed ion conduction. As a proof, the room-temperature ionic conductivity of ball-milled Na2B12H12 drastically increases by three orders of magnitude compared to that of the pristine material. While both low-conducting and superconducting phases coexisting in the ball-milled materials, superconducting nanocrystallites interconnect and randomly distribute in larger crystallites, largely promoting the ion conduction in bulk materials. More aggressive or well-designed milling conditions tend to result in more complete pulverization of particles, maximizing the necessary nanoparticulate morphologies. Revisiting the abovementioned anion-mixed Na3(BH4)(B12H12) and Na2(CB9H10)(CB11H12) by adopting the mechanical ball-milling synthesis method, their fast ion conduction is believed to be due to not only polyanion insertion, but also the effect of crystallite nanosizing and induced disordering.719,762 However, the low-temperature phase with sluggish ion conduction can probably recrystallize during thermal cycling, as found in Li2B12H12.774
8.3. Compatibility with the Na metal anode
The advantage of closo-borohydride SEs may include not only their high ionic conductivity, but also the wide ESW and interfacial compatibility with the Na metal anode.742,775,776 While the archetypical Na2B10H10 was proved to be stable up to 5 V vs. Na+/Na at 393 K according to preliminary CV measurements and air-stable at room temperature without noticeable degradation until 500 K, owing to the high stability of the closed and symmetric “aromatic-like” closo-borane, the rotational flexibility of large closo-borate anions also results in weaker mechanical strength, and their ductility in turn can make more intimate contact with electrodes and allow facile material processing.747,765,777,778 Considering the limitation of the disordered phase of pristine closo-borohydride SEs at high temperature, the Na compatibility of the room-temperature conductive variants becomes more important. Based on the CV measurement, Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 was determined to be thermodynamically stable against the metallic Na anode without observing reduction currents down to 0 V vs. Na+/Na, while the compatibility was experimentally investigated in the symmetric Na|Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5|Na cell at 60 °C, which exhibited fully reversible Na stripping/plating and long-term stability at 0.1 mA cm−2.302 Later, a full-cell solid-state SMB was assembled using the Na2(B12H12)0.5(B10H10)0.5 SE, the NaCrO2 cathode and the Na metal anode, demonstrating a reversible capacity of 80 mA h g−1 and 68 mA h g−1 retained after 250 cycles at 0.2C and 60 °C. Meanwhile, 25Na2B10H10–0.75Na2B12H12 was used to fabricate a bulk-type all-solid-state SMB using the TiS2 cathode and the Na metal anode without interface modification, which delivered a high initial charge capacity of 237 mA h g−1, with 216 mA h g−1 retained at the 11th cycle at room temperature and 0.02C.763 Impressively, the highly conductive Na3(BH4)(B12H12) showed outstanding electrochemical stability up to 10 V and compatibility with metal Na based on CV measurements, despite the unfortunate miss-out on the cell testing.711 Due to the slightly lower room-temperature ionic conductivity of Na3(NH2)(B12H12), the all-solid-state SMB TiS2|Na3(NH2)(B12H12)|Na was cycled at 353 K and 0.1C, which demonstrated a discharge capacity of 146 mA h g−1 and stable cycling for more than 200 cycles, with a capacity retention of 77 mA h g−1.779 Recently, the mixed-anion closo-polycarbaborane SE Na4(CB11H12)2(B12H12) was found to be intrinsically stable with the Na metal anode while forming an in situ passivation layer to achieve dynamical stability against a high-voltage cathode. Thereby, a 4 V-class solid-state SMB Na3(VOPO4)2F|Na4(CB11H12)2(B12H12)|Na was successfully operated at room temperature and 0.2C, without interfacial modification or high cell pressure, which enabled a discharge capacity of 99 mA h g−1 with 76% of the capacity retained after 800 cycles.767 Most recently, the conjuncto-hydroborane Na3B24H23 was reported with the widest electrochemical stability in all hydroborate-type SEs so far, of 6.7 V vs. Na+/Na, while its composite conjuncto-closo-hydroborane SE Na3B24H23–5Na2B12H12 still exhibited a wide ESW of 5.8 V and great compatibility toward the Na metal anode, which was demonstrated by the CCD of 1 mA cm−2 and stable cycling over 100 h of the symmetric Na cell at 0.2 mA cm−2 and 60 °C, or CCD of 0.7 mA cm−2 at room temperature. Assembled with 4 V-class cathode Na(Ni1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3)O2, the solid-state SMB Na(Ni1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3)O2/Na3B24H23-5Na2B12H12/Na ensured good rate capability and cycling stability by providing an initial discharge capacity of 62.3 mA h g−1 with 84% capacity retention after 100 cycles at 60 °C and 0.1C.768 Coupled with wide electrochemical stability and chemical compatibility against various high-voltage cathodes and the Na metal anode, complex hydrides have great development potential in the research of ISEs. However, some of their electrochemical stabilities have also been doubted to be overestimated, as for chalcogenide SEs due to the very small oxidation currents and the artificial increase of the interfacial contact area.302 Meanwhile, as the soft and ductile mechanical properties of borohydrides endow them with intimate contact with electrode materials, their low shear modulus may be incapable of suppressing the growth of sodium dendrites.777
9. Outlook and perspective
After reviewing the long list of Na-ion ISEs, it is unfortunate that none of them, at least at this current stage, can simultaneously satisfy all of the expectations for an ideal SE in achieving high-energy-density and high-rate-capability SMBs. Interestingly, despite the intrinsic advantages owned by different types of ISEs, there seems always to be a tricky trade-off between fast ion conduction and thermodynamic stability.224 This phenomenon is best reflected by the oxide-based and chalcogenide-based ISEs, where the chalcogenide-based ISEs usually exhibit ultrahigh ionic conductivity, some exceeding 10 mS cm−1, owing to the shielding effect of highly polarizable S2− anions on the interactions between mobile cations and host structure or other cations. However, at the same time, the loosely bonded electrons in S2− anions are subject to electron extraction at high voltage, thereby resulting in a rather low oxidation limit, and vice versa for oxide-based SEs. A similar conflict also exists between the mechanical strength and interfacial wettability of SEs, where the softer nature favors the intimate contact with the Na metal anode, but at the same time sacrificing the mechanical stability. Nonetheless, we can still see great potential and a brilliant future in these promising candidates, in light of the unceasing improvement of the existing materials, as well as the exciting discoveries of novel materials or underlying mechanisms, by which a perfect balance on the teeter-totter between their properties is expected in the future. To realize the practical application of ISEs in SMBs, the most essential considerations are still their sufficiently high ionic conductivity and compatibility with the Na metal anode. In our review, the specific ion conduction mechanisms have been thoroughly elaborated in the given materials, as well as their thermodynamic stability and interfacial kinetics against the Na metal anode, which provided the fundamental understanding of the activities in solid-state SMBs. Based on these fundamentals, the rational modification strategies to improve Na+ conduction and stabilize the electrolyte/Na interfaces have been expounded.
Despite the widely different ion conduction mechanisms underlying the varying compositions and structures, and their extremely complex entanglement at multiple length scales, the general principles of future modification strategies for fast ion conductors can be concluded as in Fig. 29:
 Fig. 29  The general rules and designing principles for fast-ion-conducting solid electrolytes in the future. 
(1) To incorporate a high concentration of mobile charge carriers in the Na+ ion diffusion pathways, e.g., vacancies and interstitials (Fig. 29(i)). As a matter of fact, materials with flawless crystalline structure are essentially ion insulators because the ion hopping events are unlikely to happen if all Na+ ions are at their stable sites and the mobile Na+ ions have no landing point after jumping. In addition to the Schottky and Frenkel defects formed in the intrinsic regime, excess Na+ vacancies and interstitials can be introduced by the charge compensation mechanism, and they have been demonstrated to be effective in enhancing the ionic conductivity of a wide array of ISEs, e.g., NASICON,284,468 antiperovskites,720,723 and NPS.538,539
(2) To build isoenergetic Na+ ion migration pathways along the Na+ sites with similar site energies and local environment (Fig. 29(ii)). In solid ion conductors, the activation energy for Na+ migration is determined by the maximum energy barrier that mobile Na+ ions have to overcome in the ion transport channels. With virtually the same site energy and reduced migration bottlenecks, mobile Na+ should adopt a liquid-like flowing among the interconnected hopping sites, and thus facile ion transportation. For example, the bcc-like anion framework was validated as a faster ion-conducting structure owing to the interlinked energy-equivalent tetrahedral sites that allow mobile Na+ diffusion in a shallow energy landscape.255
(3) To trigger the concerted ion motion upon the single-ion movements (Fig. 29(iii)). Generally, the correlative motion of mobile Na+ exhibits a much lower activation barrier and faster ion conduction. This is usually achieved by adding a significantly excess number of mobile cations, i.e., Na+, into the SEs, which forces the excessive Na+ to occupy the high energy sites instead of ground-state sites, thereby igniting the correlative ion motion with high-site-energy Na+ ions flowing downhill and kicking the low-site-energy Na+ for continuous migration.253 The second approach is to intentionally create high-energy sites by implanting a large repulsive center, e.g., Cs+, in the regular Na+ diffusion pathways, thus facilitating the long-distance and facile Na+ concerted hopping under the action of Coulombic interaction.602
(4) To weaken the electrostatic interaction between mobile Na+ and the anion frameworks (Fig. 29(iv)). Intuitively, mobile Na+ ions moving in the 3D skeletons must be susceptible to their local environment, in that stronger electrostatic interaction from the stationary framework may largely cumber the free movement of mobile Na+. Such a theory has been reflected by the improvement of ionic conductivity by rationally increasing the lattice volume and bottleneck area in the NASICON structure.449,454,455 Akin to this theory, more polarizable anion frameworks should exhibit faster conducting features via lattice softening, where the softer bonding favors the facile transport of Na+, e.g. S2− compared to O2−.
(5) To induce lattice disorder and structure frustration. In the disordered lattice, Na+ ions tend to slightly shift from their ground-state equilibrium sites to locate at the so-called “destabilized” state, which favors their facile ion motion with shallow potential wells (Fig. 29(v)). For example, a trend of increasing ionic conductivity can be observed in Na8.5(AlS4)0.25(SiS4)0.75 with lower local coordination symmetry of Na+,266 while the disordered structure of complex hydrides also exhibited higher ionic conductivity than the ordered phase by several orders of magnitude.747,758,780
(6) To mitigate the grain boundary effect. The blocking effect of grain boundaries has often been neglected during the material design, despite its significant impact on polycrystalline ion-conducting ceramics in practice (Fig. 29(vi)). The optimization of the microstructure of SEs is usually associated with controlled synthesis and processing conditions or adding dopants to decorate the grain boundaries, in that the rapid and low-temperature sintering of oxide-based SEs manifests restricted abnormal grain growth as well as low concentration of grain boundaries, while the in situ formation of some secondary phases can mitigate the grain boundary resistance, thereby acquiring higher ionic conductivity in the polycrystalline final products.284,352,368,378,434,521
(7) To design the paddle-wheeling polyanion clusters (Fig. 29(vii)). The rotational dynamics of some of the polyanions have been revealed to be responsible for some of the superionic SEs,256,277–281 such as γ-Na3PS4,521,522 Na11Sn2PS12-type SEs,256 and Na3O(BH4).276
Likewise, the general rules for optimizing the interfacial compatibility between ISEs and the Na metal anode can be concluded as in Fig. 30:
 Fig. 30  The general rules and the designing principle for solid electrolytes with good interfacial compatibility towards the Na metal anode. 
(1) To investigate the thermodynamic stability of SEs with the Na metal anode through computational screening (Fig. 30(i)). To guide more efficient work on experimentally validating the compatibility of optimum SEs with the Na metal anode, a delicate and thorough theoretical calculation can be conducted in advance to investigate the intrinsic properties of a large variety of potential SE candidates, including their thermodynamic stability, structural preference, predicted decomposition reactions, calculated reaction energies, or even the optimum dopants to improve electrochemical stability, thus screening out the most reliable material designs. Benefited from the sophisticated modeling systems and advanced computing power nowadays, a credible and simple strategic guideline can be generated for the subsequent research, which has been widely used in the material design of both Na-ion and Li-ion SEs.650,651,781–784
(2) To lower the electronic conductivity (Fig. 30(ii)). While the research of SEs descends on the improvement of ionic conductivity, their electronic conductivity is usually overlooked. However, high electronic conductivity can be one of the origins of the unstable interface between SEs and the metallic Na anode, due to the proliferent growth of metal dendrites.228,467,785–787 In this case, eliminating the inherent impurities and formation of electronically conducting phases, as well as adopting appropriate dopants with lower electronegativity, becomes necessary.211,503
(3) To regulate the microstructural defects (Fig. 30(iii)). While the internal pores and grain boundaries have a huge impact on Na+ ion transport and growth of sodium dendrites, the microstructural characteristics determine the mechanical properties to a large extent, in that the bulk cracking and grain interstices expose plenty of unprotected electrochemically active surfaces. Therefore, tuning the microstructure of ISEs, e.g., density, mechanical stability, grain size, and grain boundary concentration, creates a profound influence on the interfacial stability and cycling stability with the Na metal anode. This strategy can be achieved mostly by the control of processing conditions, selection of precursors, and adding dopants or additives to decorate the grain boundaries.380,386,387,457,508
(4) To build a kinetically stable interface with a robust in situ formed SEI layer (Fig. 30(iv)). It is noteworthy that the interfacial stability between SEs and the Na metal anode depends not only on the thermodynamic aspects, but also on the kinetic stability of the in situ formed SEI layer; e.g., the true electrochemical stability window of some sulfide-based SEs was found to be wider than their thermodynamic stability window. By adding certain dopants or additives in the bulk materials or at the SE/anode interface, the ESW of the SEs can be expanded beyond their thermodynamic limits by maintaining a kinetically stable interface during the repeated plating and stripping of Na, e.g., the in situ formation of the fluorine/chorine-rich interface layer587,588,687,788,789 and the Na-K alloy interface layer.790
(5) To insert a thermodynamically stable interlayer between the unstable SEs and the Na metal anode (Fig. 30(v)). It is acknowledged that a highly conductive, yet electrochemically stable ISE hardly exists. To take advantage of the high ionic conductivity, the poor reduction stability or inferior compatibility with the metallic Na anode must usually be dealt with. In general, the polymer-based SEs have much better thermodynamic stability against the Na metal anode, despite their generally lower Na+ transference number, room-temperature ionic conductivity and oxidative stability compared to ISEs. Therefore, polymer-based SEs can be used a thermodynamic stable interlayer separating the unstable ISEs and the Na metal anode.578,672
(6) To reduce the interfacial resistance between SEs and the Na metal anode (Fig. 30(vi)). While the large interfacial resistance to Na+ conduction leads to large internal resistance deteriorating the battery performance, the unintimate interface contact between SEs and Na metal may significantly promote the unwanted interfacial reactions and notorious sodium dendrites.791 There have been many technical routes developed to reduce the interfacial resistance, e.g., coating the sodiophilic surface layer,215,792,793 constructing a sandwich structure with polymers,238 hunting interfacial impurities,794,795 or simply applying high pressure.216
(7) To design composite SEs composed of a polymer matrix and inorganic fillers (Fig. 30(vii)). Although the bilayer or sandwich structures of polymers and inorganic SEs have demonstrated a much more stable interface during cycling, the additional interfaces between these layered structures can impede the fast ion diffusion and increase the total resistance of the SEs. By adding inorganic fillers in the polymer matrix, composite SEs should exhibit merits from both highly conductive ISEs and interfacial compatible polymer SEs,796–800 which deserve future in-depth research for all-solid-state SMBs.

Table Content:
Jiawen Huang	Jiawen Huang is currently a PhD candidate in Shanghai University (SHU). He received his BS degree and MS degree from the University of Washington (UW), majorly investigating halide-based solid electrolytes in lithium-metal batteries. His current research interests focus on sodium-ion inorganic solid electrolytes and composite solid electrolytes for solid-state sodium-metal batteries, as well as other metal-based batteries.
Minghong Wu	Minghong Wu is a Distinguished Professor at Shanghai University and the Academician of Chinese Academy of Engineering. She received her PhD in Shanghai Institute of Applied Physics of Chinese Academy of Sciences in 1999. After several years of postdoctoral research in Japan, she became a Professor at the School of Environmental and Chemical Engineering at SHU in 2002. Her research interest focuses on the fundamental study and application of environmental and energy functional materials. She has been consecutively selected as a global highly cited scholar since 2019.
Shi-Xue Dou	Shi Xue Dou is a Distinguished Professor at the University of Shanghai for Science and Technology, University of Wollongong (UOW) Research Ambassador Asia and the founding director of the Institute for Superconducting & Electronic Materials at UOW. He received his PhD at Dalhousie University, Canada in 1984, DSc at the University of New South Wales in 1998 and was elected as a Fellow of the Australian Academy of Technological Science and Engineering in 1994. He focuses on the research of energy and electronic materials. He is awarded the Australian Government's Centenary Medal in 2003 and Australian Order of Member in 2019 for his contribution to materials science and engineering, multiple Australian Professorial Fellowships from 1993 to 2011, the Vice-Chancellors Senior Excellence Award in 2008, Outstanding Partnership Award in 2012, the ASTS Lifetime Achievement Award in 2018, Lifetime Achiever by Australian Research Magazine in 2020 and the ICMC Lifetime Achievement Award in 2021. He is named a highly cited researcher in materials science by Thomson Reuters since 2018 with 90000 citations and an h-index of 145.
Chao Wu	Chao Wu received his PhD from Shanghai Jiao Tong University in 2014, followed by postdoctoral research experience in the Department of Physical Chemistry of Solids at the Max-Planck-Institute for Solid State Research from 2014 to 2017, and later became a research fellow at the Institute for Superconducting and Electronic Materials (ISEM), University of Wollongong. He is currently a professor at the Institute of Energy Materials Science (IEMS), University of Shanghai for Science and Technology. His research interests emphasize high energy metal-based batteries, including Li, Na, and Zn, in different electrolyte systems.
